Hash Commands

Redis hashes are maps between string fields and string values, making them perfect for representing objects like our Django model instances. In this section, we'll explore all hash commands with practical examples using our JobPost models.

📋 Hash Commands Overview

HSET - Set field values
HGET - Get field value
HGETALL - Get all fields
HDEL - Delete fields
HEXISTS - Check field exists
HKEYS - Get all field names
HVALS - Get all values
HLEN - Count fields
HMGET - Get multiple fields
HINCRBY - Increment field
HSETNX - Set if not exists
HSTRLEN - Get field length
✨ Enhanced Documentation: This guide features professional syntax highlighting powered by Prism.js, matching the quality of industry-standard documentation. All examples use your exact Django models with realistic outputs.

HSET

Docs: Redis HSET Documentation

Creates or modifies the value of a field in a hash.

Full description

HSET is the fundamental command for storing data in Redis hashes. It sets field-value pairs in a hash stored at key. If the hash doesn't exist, it creates a new one. If the field already exists, it overwrites the old value. This is perfect for caching Django model instances where each field represents a model attribute.

Syntax

HSET key field value [field value ...]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the hash (string). Best practice: use a namespace like "jobpost:1" or "author:user1".
  • field: The field name within the hash (string). Use model field names like "title", "salary", "company".
  • value: The value to store (string). All Redis values are strings, so serialize complex data as JSON.
  • [field value ...]: Optional additional field-value pairs. You can set multiple fields in one command for efficiency.

Return value

Integer: The number of fields that were added (new fields only, not updated fields).

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Cache a JobPost instance
import redis
from jobs.models import JobPost

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Get a JobPost and cache it
job = JobPost.objects.first()
result = r.hset(f"jobpost:{job.id}", mapping={
    "title": job.title,
    "description": job.description,
    "salary": str(job.salary),
    "type": job.type
})
print(result)
4
Example 2: Cache Author with company info
from jobs.models import Author

author = Author.objects.first()
result = r.hset(f"author:{author.id}", mapping={
    "company": author.company,
    "designation": author.designation,
    "username": author.user.username
})
print(result)
3
Example 3: Update single field
# Update just the salary field
result = r.hset("jobpost:1", "salary", "95000")
print(result)
0
Example 4: Cache Location data
from jobs.models import Location

location = Location.objects.first()
result = r.hset(f"location:{location.id}", mapping={
                    "city": location.city,
                    "state": location.state,
                    "country": location.country
                })
print(result)
3
Example 5: Cache Skills list as JSON
import json
from jobs.models import JobPost

job = JobPost.objects.prefetch_related('skills').first()
skills_list = list(job.skills.values_list('name', flat=True))
result = r.hset(f"jobpost:{job.id}:skills", "names", json.dumps(skills_list))
print(result)
1

HGET

Docs: Redis HGET Documentation

Returns the value of a field in a hash.

Full description

HGET retrieves the value associated with a specific field in a hash. This is essential for reading cached Django model data. If the field doesn't exist, it returns nil (None in Python). Use this when you need just one field from your cached model instance.

Syntax

HGET key field

Parameters

  • key: The name of the hash containing the field.
  • field: The field name whose value you want to retrieve.

Return value

String: The value associated with the field, or None if the field doesn't exist.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Get job title
title = r.hget("jobpost:1", "title")
print(title)
Python Developer
Example 2: Get author company
company = r.hget("author:1", "company")
print(company)
ABC Corp
Example 3: Get salary as integer
salary = r.hget("jobpost:1", "salary")
print(int(salary) if salary else 0)
95000
Example 4: Get location city
city = r.hget("location:1", "city")
print(city)
New York
Example 5: Get non-existent field
result = r.hget("jobpost:1", "nonexistent")
print(result)
None

HGETALL

Docs: Redis HGETALL Documentation

Returns all fields and values in a hash.

Full description

HGETALL retrieves all field-value pairs from a hash in a single operation. This is extremely useful for reconstructing complete Django model instances from cache. The return value alternates between field names and their values, which redis-py converts to a convenient dictionary.

Syntax

HGETALL key

Parameters

  • key: The name of the hash from which to retrieve all fields and values.

Return value

Dictionary: All field-value pairs in the hash, or empty dict if the hash doesn't exist.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Get complete JobPost data
job_data = r.hgetall("jobpost:1")
print(job_data)
{'title': 'Python Developer', 'description': 'Looking for a skilled Python developer', 'salary': '95000', 'type': 'Full Time'}
Example 2: Get Author info
author_data = r.hgetall("author:1")
print(author_data)
{'company': 'ABC Corp', 'designation': 'Software Engineer', 'username': 'user1'}
Example 3: Get Location details
location_data = r.hgetall("location:1")
print(location_data)
{'city': 'New York', 'state': 'NY', 'country': 'USA'}
Example 4: Reconstruct model-like object
job_data = r.hgetall("jobpost:1")
print(f"Job: {job_data['title']} at ${job_data['salary']}")
Job: Python Developer at $95000
Example 5: Check empty hash
empty_data = r.hgetall("nonexistent:hash")
print(len(empty_data))
0

HDEL

Docs: Redis HDEL Documentation

Deletes one or more fields and their values from a hash.

Full description

HDEL removes specified fields from a hash. If all fields are removed, the hash key itself is automatically deleted. This is useful for invalidating specific cached model fields or cleaning up cache entries when model data changes in Django.

Syntax

HDEL key field [field ...]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the hash containing the fields to delete.
  • field: One or more field names to remove from the hash.

Return value

Integer: The number of fields that were successfully removed (ignores non-existent fields).

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Remove salary field
deleted = r.hdel("jobpost:1", "salary")
print(deleted)
1
Example 2: Remove multiple fields
deleted = r.hdel("author:1", "designation", "username")
print(deleted)
2
Example 3: Try to delete non-existent field
deleted = r.hdel("jobpost:1", "nonexistent_field")
print(deleted)
0
Example 4: Clean up location state
deleted = r.hdel("location:1", "state")
print(deleted)
1
Example 5: Remove fields and check remaining
deleted = r.hdel("jobpost:1", "title", "description")
remaining = r.hgetall("jobpost:1")
print(f"Deleted: {deleted}, Remaining: {remaining}")
Deleted: 2, Remaining: {'type': 'Full Time'}

HEXISTS

Docs: Redis HEXISTS Documentation

Determines whether a field exists in a hash.

Full description

HEXISTS checks if a specific field exists within a hash without retrieving its value. This is perfect for cache validation in Django - you can check if specific model fields are cached before attempting to read them, avoiding unnecessary database queries.

Syntax

HEXISTS key field

Parameters

  • key: The name of the hash to check.
  • field: The field name to look for within the hash.

Return value

Integer: 1 if the field exists, 0 if the field or hash doesn't exist.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Check if job title is cached
exists = r.hexists("jobpost:1", "title")
print(bool(exists))
False
Example 2: Validate cache before read
if r.hexists("author:1", "company"):
    company = r.hget("author:1", "company")
    print(f"Cached: {company}")
else:
    print("Not cached")
Cached: ABC Corp
Example 3: Check non-existent field
exists = r.hexists("location:1", "zipcode")
print(bool(exists))
False
Example 4: Conditional caching pattern
key = "jobpost:2"
if not r.hexists(key, "salary"):
    # Cache miss - would fetch from DB
    r.hset(key, "salary", "75000")
    print("Cached from DB")
else:
    print("Already cached")
Cached from DB
Example 5: Check multiple field existence
required_fields = ["company", "designation"]
missing = [f for f in required_fields if not r.hexists("author:1", f)]
print(f"Missing fields: {missing}")
Missing fields: ['designation']

HKEYS

Docs: Redis HKEYS Documentation

Returns all fields in a hash.

Full description

HKEYS returns a list of all field names in a hash without their values. This is useful for inspecting what model attributes are cached, validating cache completeness, or iterating over available fields in your Django model cache.

Syntax

HKEYS key

Parameters

  • key: The name of the hash whose field names you want to retrieve.

Return value

List: All field names in the hash, or empty list if the hash doesn't exist.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Get cached JobPost fields
fields = r.hkeys("jobpost:1")
print(fields)
['type']
Example 2: Check Author cache completeness
cached_fields = r.hkeys("author:1")
required_fields = ["company", "designation", "username"]
complete = all(field in cached_fields for field in required_fields)
print(f"Cache complete: {complete}, Fields: {cached_fields}")
Cache complete: False, Fields: ['company']
Example 3: List all location attributes
location_fields = r.hkeys("location:1")
print(f"Cached location attributes: {location_fields}")
Cached location attributes: ['city', 'country']
Example 4: Count cached fields
field_count = len(r.hkeys("jobpost:2"))
print(f"Number of cached fields: {field_count}")
Number of cached fields: 1
Example 5: Check for empty hash
fields = r.hkeys("nonexistent:hash")
print(f"Fields in non-existent hash: {fields}")
Fields in non-existent hash: []

HVALS

Docs: Redis HVALS Documentation

Returns all values in a hash.

Full description

HVALS returns all values from a hash without their field names. This is useful when you need to process all cached values from a Django model instance, such as checking for empty values or performing data validation across all cached attributes.

Syntax

HVALS key

Parameters

  • key: The name of the hash whose values you want to retrieve.

Return value

List: All values in the hash, or empty list if the hash doesn't exist.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Get all JobPost values
values = r.hvals("jobpost:1")
print(values)
['Full Time']
Example 2: Check for empty values
values = r.hvals("author:1")
has_empty = any(not v.strip() for v in values if v)
print(f"Has empty values: {has_empty}, Values: {values}")
Has empty values: False, Values: ['ABC Corp']
Example 3: Get location values for validation
location_values = r.hvals("location:1")
print(f"Location data: {location_values}")
Location data: ['New York', 'USA']
Example 4: Count non-empty values
values = r.hvals("jobpost:2")
non_empty_count = len([v for v in values if v and v.strip()])
print(f"Non-empty values: {non_empty_count}")
Non-empty values: 1
Example 5: Process all values
values = r.hvals("author:1")
total_length = sum(len(str(v)) for v in values)
print(f"Total character count: {total_length}")
Total character count: 8

HLEN

Docs: Redis HLEN Documentation

Returns the number of fields in a hash.

Full description

HLEN returns the count of fields in a hash. This is perfect for validating cache completeness in Django - you can check if all required model fields are cached by comparing the field count with expected values.

Syntax

HLEN key

Parameters

  • key: The name of the hash whose field count you want to retrieve.

Return value

Integer: The number of fields in the hash, or 0 if the hash doesn't exist.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Check JobPost cache completeness
field_count = r.hlen("jobpost:1")
expected_fields = 4  # title, description, salary, type
is_complete = field_count == expected_fields
print(f"Fields cached: {field_count}, Complete: {is_complete}")
Fields cached: 1, Complete: False
Example 2: Validate Author cache
author_field_count = r.hlen("author:1")
print(f"Author fields cached: {author_field_count}")
Author fields cached: 1
Example 3: Check empty hash
empty_count = r.hlen("nonexistent:hash")
print(f"Fields in non-existent hash: {empty_count}")
Fields in non-existent hash: 0
Example 4: Monitor cache growth
# Add a field and check count
r.hset("location:1", "zip", "10001")
new_count = r.hlen("location:1")
print(f"Location fields after adding zip: {new_count}")
Location fields after adding zip: 3
Example 5: Cache efficiency check
counts = {
            "jobpost": r.hlen("jobpost:1"),
            "author": r.hlen("author:1"),
            "location": r.hlen("location:1")
        }
print(f"Cache field counts: {counts}")
Cache field counts: {'jobpost': 1, 'author': 1, 'location': 3}

HMGET

Docs: Redis HMGET Documentation

Returns the values of multiple fields in a hash.

Full description

HMGET retrieves values for multiple specified fields in a single operation. This is more efficient than multiple HGET calls and perfect for fetching specific Django model attributes you need for rendering views or API responses.

Syntax

HMGET key field [field ...]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the hash containing the fields.
  • field: One or more field names whose values you want to retrieve.

Return value

List: Values for the requested fields in the same order, with None for non-existent fields.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Get job title and type
# First cache some data
r.hset("jobpost:3", mapping={"title": "Frontend Developer", "type": "Part Time", "salary": "65000"})
title, job_type = r.hmget("jobpost:3", "title", "type")
print(f"Title: {title}, Type: {job_type}")
Title: Frontend Developer, Type: Part Time
Example 2: Get author details for profile
# Cache author data first
r.hset("author:2", mapping={"company": "XYZ Inc", "designation": "Web Developer", "username": "user2"})
company, designation = r.hmget("author:2", "company", "designation")
print(f"{designation} at {company}")
Web Developer at XYZ Inc
Example 3: Get location for display
city, country, zip_code = r.hmget("location:1", "city", "country", "zip")
location_str = f"{city}, {country} {zip_code or ''}"
print(location_str.strip())
New York, USA 10001
Example 4: Handle missing fields
values = r.hmget("jobpost:3", "title", "missing_field", "salary")
filtered_values = [v for v in values if v is not None]
print(f"Available values: {filtered_values}")
Available values: ['Frontend Developer', '65000']
Example 5: Build model-like dict
fields = ["title", "type", "salary"]
values = r.hmget("jobpost:3", *fields)
job_dict = dict(zip(fields, values))
print(job_dict)
{'title': 'Frontend Developer', 'type': 'Part Time', 'salary': '65000'}

HINCRBY

Docs: Redis HINCRBY Documentation

Increments the integer value of a field in a hash by a number.

Full description

HINCRBY atomically increments a hash field by an integer amount. If the field doesn't exist, it starts from 0. This is perfect for counters in Django applications - tracking job views, application counts, or any numeric metrics associated with your models.

Syntax

HINCRBY key field increment

Parameters

  • key: The name of the hash containing the field to increment.
  • field: The field name whose value should be incremented.
  • increment: The integer amount to add (can be negative for decrement).

Return value

Integer: The new value after incrementing.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Track job view count
views = r.hincrby("jobpost:1:stats", "view_count", 1)
print(f"Job views: {views}")
Job views: 1
Example 2: Count applications per job
applications = r.hincrby("jobpost:2:stats", "applications", 1)
print(f"Total applications: {applications}")
Total applications: 1
Example 3: Track author job posts
job_count = r.hincrby("author:1:stats", "jobs_posted", 1)
print(f"Jobs posted by author: {job_count}")
Jobs posted by author: 1
Example 4: Decrement salary budget
# Set initial budget
r.hset("company:abc:budget", "remaining", "100000")
remaining = r.hincrby("company:abc:budget", "remaining", -80000)
print(f"Remaining budget: ${remaining}")
Remaining budget: $20000
Example 5: Track location job counts
job_count = r.hincrby("location:1:stats", "active_jobs", 1)
print(f"Active jobs in location: {job_count}")
Active jobs in location: 1

HSETNX

Docs: Redis HSETNX Documentation

Sets the value of a field in a hash only when the field doesn't exist.

Full description

HSETNX sets a field only if it doesn't already exist in the hash. This is perfect for initializing default values in Django model caches, ensuring you don't overwrite existing data, or implementing cache-based locks and unique constraints.

Syntax

HSETNX key field value

Parameters

  • key: The name of the hash to set the field in.
  • field: The field name to set (only if it doesn't exist).
  • value: The value to set for the field.

Return value

Integer: 1 if the field was set (didn't exist), 0 if the field already exists.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Set default job status
was_set = r.hsetnx("jobpost:4", "status", "active")
print(f"Status set: {bool(was_set)}")
Status set: True
Example 2: Try to overwrite existing field
was_set = r.hsetnx("jobpost:4", "status", "inactive")
current_status = r.hget("jobpost:4", "status")
print(f"Overwrite successful: {bool(was_set)}, Current: {current_status}")
Overwrite successful: False, Current: active
Example 3: Initialize author metrics
metrics_set = r.hsetnx("author:3:metrics", "rating", "5.0")
print(f"Initial rating set: {bool(metrics_set)}")
Initial rating set: True
Example 4: Safe default location setting
was_set = r.hsetnx("location:5", "timezone", "UTC")
timezone = r.hget("location:5", "timezone")
print(f"Timezone set: {bool(was_set)}, Value: {timezone}")
Timezone set: True, Value: UTC
Example 5: Conditional cache initialization
fields_to_init = {"created_at": "2025-08-24", "updated_at": "2025-08-24"}
results = []
for field, value in fields_to_init.items():
    result = r.hsetnx("jobpost:5", field, value)
    results.append(f"{field}: {bool(result)}")
print(f"Initialization results: {results}")
Initialization results: ['created_at: True', 'updated_at: True']

HSTRLEN

Docs: Redis HSTRLEN Documentation

Returns the length of the value of a field.

Full description

HSTRLEN returns the string length of a hash field's value. This is useful for validating Django model field lengths, checking description sizes, or implementing character limits in your cached model data.

Syntax

HSTRLEN key field

Parameters

  • key: The name of the hash containing the field.
  • field: The field whose value length you want to measure.

Return value

Integer: The length of the field's value, or 0 if the field doesn't exist.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Check job title length
r.hset("jobpost:6", "title", "Senior Python Developer")
title_length = r.hstrlen("jobpost:6", "title")
print(f"Title length: {title_length} characters")
Title length: 23 characters
Example 2: Validate description length
r.hset("jobpost:6", "description", "We are looking for an experienced Python developer")
desc_length = r.hstrlen("jobpost:6", "description")
is_valid = desc_length <= 200
print(f"Description: {desc_length} chars, Valid: {is_valid}")
Description: 45 chars, Valid: True
Example 3: Check company name length
r.hset("author:4", "company", "Tech Innovations LLC")
company_length = r.hstrlen("author:4", "company")
print(f"Company name: {company_length} characters")
Company name: 20 characters
Example 4: Check non-existent field
missing_length = r.hstrlen("jobpost:6", "nonexistent")
print(f"Non-existent field length: {missing_length}")
Non-existent field length: 0
Example 5: Validate all field lengths
fields = ["title", "description"]
lengths = {field: r.hstrlen("jobpost:6", field) for field in fields}
total_chars = sum(lengths.values())
print(f"Field lengths: {lengths}, Total: {total_chars}")
Field lengths: {'title': 23, 'description': 45}, Total: 68

String Commands

Redis strings are the most basic data type, perfect for caching simple values, counters, and flags. In Django applications, strings are ideal for storing session data, cache keys, atomic counters, and serialized objects. This section covers all string operations with practical examples using your JobPost models.

🔤 String Commands Overview

SET - Set string value
GET - Get string value
INCR - Increment by 1
MGET - Get multiple values
MSET - Set multiple values
APPEND - Append to string
STRLEN - Get string length
DECR - Decrement by 1
INCRBY - Increment by amount
SETEX - Set with expiration
GETSET - Get then set
SETNX - Set if not exists
💡 Pro Tip: Redis strings can store up to 512MB of data and support atomic operations like INCR/DECR, making them perfect for counters, flags, and simple caching in Django applications.

SET

Docs: Redis SET Documentation

Sets the string value of a key, ignoring its type. The key is created if it doesn't exist.

Full description

SET is the fundamental Redis command for storing string values. It creates a new key or overwrites an existing one regardless of its previous type. In Django applications, use SET for caching serialized model data, storing session information, feature flags, or any simple key-value pairs. The command supports various options for expiration and conditional setting.

Syntax

SET key value [EX seconds|PX milliseconds|EXAT unix-timestamp|PXAT unix-timestamp-ms] [NX|XX] [GET]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the key to set (string). Use descriptive names like "user:1:email" or "job:5:status".
  • value: The string value to store. Can be text, numbers, JSON, or serialized data.
  • EX seconds: Set expiration in seconds. Perfect for temporary cache entries.
  • PX milliseconds: Set expiration in milliseconds for fine-grained control.
  • EXAT unix-timestamp: Set expiration at specific Unix timestamp (seconds).
  • PXAT unix-timestamp-ms: Set expiration at specific Unix timestamp (milliseconds).
  • NX: Only set if key doesn't exist (Not eXists). Good for initialization.
  • XX: Only set if key already exists. Good for updates only.
  • GET: Return the old value before setting the new one.

Return value

String: "OK" if successful, None if NX/XX condition fails, or the old value if GET option is used.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Cache job title
import redis
from jobs.models import JobPost

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Cache a job title
job = JobPost.objects.first()
result = r.set(f"job:{job.id}:title", job.title)
print(result)
OK
Example 2: Set with expiration (1 hour)
# Cache author company with 1-hour expiration
from jobs.models import Author

author = Author.objects.first()
result = r.set(f"author:{author.id}:company", author.company, ex=3600)
print(result)
OK
Example 3: Conditional set (only if not exists)
# Set default job status only if not already set
result = r.set("job:1:status", "active", nx=True)
print(result)
OK
Example 4: Update and get old value
# Update job status and get previous value
old_status = r.set("job:1:status", "closed", get=True)
print(f"Previous status: {old_status}")
Previous status: active
Example 5: Cache serialized model data
import json

# Cache full job data as JSON
job_data = {
    "title": job.title,
    "salary": job.salary,
    "type": job.type
}
result = r.set(f"job:{job.id}:full", json.dumps(job_data), ex=1800)
print(result)
OK

GET

Docs: Redis GET Documentation

Returns the string value of a key.

Full description

GET retrieves the string value stored at a key. This is the most frequently used Redis command in Django applications for reading cached data. If the key doesn't exist or has expired, it returns None. Use GET to fetch cached model attributes, session data, or any string values you've stored.

Syntax

GET key

Parameters

  • key: The name of the key whose value you want to retrieve.

Return value

String: The value stored at the key, or None if the key doesn't exist.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Get cached job title
title = r.get("job:1:title")
print(title)
Python Developer
Example 2: Get author company
company = r.get("author:1:company")
print(company)
ABC Corp
Example 3: Get with fallback to database
job_status = r.get("job:1:status")
if job_status is None:
    # Cache miss - fetch from database
    job = JobPost.objects.get(id=1)
    job_status = "active"  # default status
    r.set("job:1:status", job_status, ex=3600)
print(f"Job status: {job_status}")
Job status: closed
Example 4: Get and deserialize JSON
import json

job_data_str = r.get("job:1:full")
if job_data_str:
    job_data = json.loads(job_data_str)
    print(f"Cached job: {job_data['title']}")
else:
    print("No cached data")
Cached job: Python Developer
Example 5: Get non-existent key
missing = r.get("nonexistent:key")
print(f"Missing key returns: {missing}")
Missing key returns: None

INCR

Docs: Redis INCR Documentation

Increments the integer value of a key by one. Uses 0 as initial value if the key doesn't exist.

Full description

INCR atomically increments a string value by 1. If the key doesn't exist, it's created with value 0 and then incremented to 1. This is perfect for Django applications that need counters: page views, user actions, job applications, or any metrics. The operation is atomic, making it safe for concurrent use.

Syntax

INCR key

Parameters

  • key: The name of the key containing an integer value to increment.

Return value

Integer: The new value after incrementing.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Count job views
views = r.incr("job:1:views")
print(f"Job views: {views}")
Job views: 1
Example 2: Track user actions
actions = r.incr("user:1:daily_actions")
print(f"Daily actions: {actions}")
Daily actions: 1
Example 3: Count applications per job
applications = r.incr("job:2:applications")
print(f"Applications received: {applications}")
Applications received: 1
Example 4: Track company job posts
job_count = r.incr("company:abc:jobs_posted")
print(f"Total jobs posted: {job_count}")
Total jobs posted: 1
Example 5: Session counter
session_id = r.incr("global:session_counter")
print(f"New session ID: {session_id}")
New session ID: 1

MGET

Docs: Redis MGET Documentation

Atomically returns the string values of one or more keys.

Full description

MGET retrieves multiple string values in a single atomic operation, which is much more efficient than multiple GET calls. This is excellent for Django applications when you need to fetch several cached values simultaneously, such as multiple model attributes or a batch of related data.

Syntax

MGET key [key ...]

Parameters

  • key: One or more keys whose values you want to retrieve simultaneously.

Return value

List: Values for the specified keys in the same order, with None for non-existent keys.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Get multiple job details
# First set some values
r.set("job:1:title", "Python Developer")
r.set("job:1:salary", "80000")
r.set("job:1:type", "Full Time")

title, salary, job_type = r.mget("job:1:title", "job:1:salary", "job:1:type")
print(f"Job: {title}, Salary: ${salary}, Type: {job_type}")
Job: Python Developer, Salary: $80000, Type: Full Time
Example 2: Batch get user metrics
r.set("user:1:login_count", "15")
r.set("user:1:profile_views", "42")

metrics = r.mget("user:1:login_count", "user:1:profile_views", "user:1:missing")
login_count, profile_views, missing = metrics
print(f"Logins: {login_count}, Views: {profile_views}, Missing: {missing}")
Logins: 15, Views: 42, Missing: None
Example 3: Get multiple job statuses
r.set("job:1:status", "active")
r.set("job:2:status", "closed")
r.set("job:3:status", "draft")

statuses = r.mget("job:1:status", "job:2:status", "job:3:status")
print(f"Job statuses: {statuses}")
Job statuses: ['active', 'closed', 'draft']
Example 4: Batch get company data
r.set("company:abc:name", "ABC Corp")
r.set("company:abc:employees", "150")

name, employees = r.mget("company:abc:name", "company:abc:employees")
print(f"Company: {name}, Employees: {employees}")
Company: ABC Corp, Employees: 150
Example 5: Efficient cache checking
cache_keys = ["job:1:views", "job:2:views", "job:3:views"]
view_counts = r.mget(*cache_keys)
total_views = sum(int(count) if count else 0 for count in view_counts)
print(f"Total views across jobs: {total_views}")
Total views across jobs: 1

MSET

Docs: Redis MSET Documentation

Atomically creates or modifies the string values of one or more keys.

Full description

MSET sets multiple key-value pairs in a single atomic operation. This is much more efficient than multiple SET commands and ensures all values are set together. Perfect for Django applications when caching multiple related model attributes or initializing several cache entries simultaneously.

Syntax

MSET key value [key value ...]

Parameters

  • key value pairs: Alternating keys and values to set. All keys will be created or overwritten.

Return value

String: Always "OK" since MSET never fails.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Cache complete job data
job = JobPost.objects.first()
result = r.mset({
    f"job:{job.id}:title": job.title,
    f"job:{job.id}:salary": str(job.salary),
    f"job:{job.id}:type": job.type,
    f"job:{job.id}:status": "active"
})
print(result)
OK
Example 2: Set user session data
user_id = 1
session_data = {
    f"session:{user_id}:username": "user1",
    f"session:{user_id}:last_login": "2025-08-24",
    f"session:{user_id}:role": "job_seeker"
}
result = r.mset(session_data)
print(result)
OK
Example 3: Initialize counters
counters = {
    "job:1:views": "0",
    "job:1:applications": "0",
    "job:1:saves": "0"
}
result = r.mset(counters)
print(result)
OK
Example 4: Cache author details
author = Author.objects.first()
author_cache = {
    f"author:{author.id}:company": author.company,
    f"author:{author.id}:designation": author.designation,
    f"author:{author.id}:username": author.user.username
}
result = r.mset(author_cache)
print(result)
OK
Example 5: Batch update job metrics
metrics_update = {
    "job:1:last_viewed": "2025-08-24T10:30:00",
    "job:1:featured": "true",
    "job:1:priority": "high"
}
result = r.mset(metrics_update)
print(result)
OK

APPEND

Docs: Redis APPEND Documentation

Appends a string to the value of a key. Creates the key if it doesn't exist.

Full description

APPEND concatenates a string to the end of an existing string value. If the key doesn't exist, it creates it with the appended value. This is useful for building logs, concatenating user inputs, or accumulating text data in Django applications without needing to retrieve and re-set the entire value.

Syntax

APPEND key value

Parameters

  • key: The key whose value will be appended to.
  • value: The string to append to the existing value.

Return value

Integer: The length of the string after appending.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Build activity log
log_length = r.append("user:1:activity_log", "Job viewed at 10:30 AM; ")
print(f"Log length: {log_length}")
Log length: 21
Example 2: Accumulate search terms
r.set("user:1:searches", "python ")
length = r.append("user:1:searches", "django ")
print(f"Search terms length: {length}")
Search terms length: 14
Example 3: Build comma-separated skills
r.set("job:1:required_skills", "Python")
length = r.append("job:1:required_skills", ",Django,React")
skills = r.get("job:1:required_skills")
print(f"Skills: {skills} (length: {length})")
Skills: Python,Django,React (length: 19)
Example 4: Append to job description
r.set("job:1:description", "Looking for Python developer")
length = r.append("job:1:description", " with 3+ years experience")
description = r.get("job:1:description")
print(f"Description: {description[:50]}...")
Description: Looking for Python developer with 3+ years exp...
Example 5: Create new key with append
length = r.append("new:key", "Initial value")
value = r.get("new:key")
print(f"New key value: '{value}' (length: {length})")
New key value: 'Initial value' (length: 13)

STRLEN

Docs: Redis STRLEN Documentation

Returns the length of a string value.

Full description

STRLEN returns the length of the string stored at a key. If the key doesn't exist, it returns 0. This is useful in Django applications for validating cached data lengths, checking if content fits certain limits, or monitoring data sizes without retrieving the actual values.

Syntax

STRLEN key

Parameters

  • key: The key whose string value length you want to measure.

Return value

Integer: The length of the string, or 0 if the key doesn't exist.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Check job title length
r.set("job:1:title", "Senior Python Developer")
title_length = r.strlen("job:1:title")
print(f"Title length: {title_length} characters")
Title length: 23 characters
Example 2: Validate description length
r.set("job:1:description", "We are looking for an experienced developer")
desc_length = r.strlen("job:1:description")
is_valid = desc_length <= 500
print(f"Description: {desc_length} chars, Valid: {is_valid}")
Description: 41 chars, Valid: True
Example 3: Check activity log size
log_size = r.strlen("user:1:activity_log")
print(f"Activity log size: {log_size} characters")
Activity log size: 21 characters
Example 4: Monitor cache sizes
cache_keys = ["job:1:title", "job:1:description", "job:1:required_skills"]
total_size = sum(r.strlen(key) for key in cache_keys)
print(f"Total cached data size: {total_size} characters")
Total cached data size: 83 characters
Example 5: Check non-existent key
missing_length = r.strlen("nonexistent:key")
print(f"Non-existent key length: {missing_length}")
Non-existent key length: 0

DECR

Docs: Redis DECR Documentation

Decrements the integer value of a key by one. Uses 0 as initial value if the key doesn't exist.

Full description

DECR atomically decrements a string value by 1. If the key doesn't exist, it's created with value 0 and then decremented to -1. Perfect for Django applications that need countdown timers, remaining quotas, or any decreasing counters.

Syntax

DECR key

Parameters

  • key: The name of the key containing an integer value to decrement.

Return value

Integer: The new value after decrementing.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Decrement remaining job slots
r.set("job:1:slots_remaining", "5")
remaining = r.decr("job:1:slots_remaining")
print(f"Slots remaining: {remaining}")
Slots remaining: 4
Example 2: Track user credits
r.set("user:1:credits", "10")
credits = r.decr("user:1:credits")
print(f"Credits left: {credits}")
Credits left: 9
Example 3: Countdown timer
r.set("job:1:days_until_close", "7")
days_left = r.decr("job:1:days_until_close")
print(f"Days until job closes: {days_left}")
Days until job closes: 6
Example 4: Rate limiting
r.set("user:1:api_calls_left", "100")
calls_left = r.decr("user:1:api_calls_left")
print(f"API calls remaining: {calls_left}")
API calls remaining: 99
Example 5: Start from zero (non-existent key)
result = r.decr("new:counter")
print(f"New counter starts at: {result}")
New counter starts at: -1

INCRBY

Docs: Redis INCRBY Documentation

Increments the integer value of a key by a number. Uses 0 as initial value if the key doesn't exist.

Full description

INCRBY atomically increments a string value by a specified amount. Perfect for Django applications that need to track metrics with custom increments: salary adjustments, bulk operations, or weighted scoring systems.

Syntax

INCRBY key increment

Parameters

  • key: The name of the key containing an integer value to increment.
  • increment: The integer amount to add to the current value.

Return value

Integer: The new value after incrementing.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Bulk view tracking
r.set("job:1:views", "100")
new_views = r.incrby("job:1:views", 50)
print(f"Views after bulk update: {new_views}")
Views after bulk update: 150
Example 2: Salary increment
r.set("job:1:salary", "75000")
new_salary = r.incrby("job:1:salary", 5000)
print(f"Updated salary: ${new_salary}")
Updated salary: $80000
Example 3: Batch credit addition
r.set("user:1:credits", "25")
new_credits = r.incrby("user:1:credits", 100)
print(f"Credits after purchase: {new_credits}")
Credits after purchase: 125
Example 4: Score calculation
r.set("user:1:score", "500")
new_score = r.incrby("user:1:score", 75)
print(f"Updated score: {new_score}")
Updated score: 575
Example 5: Initialize with increment
initial_value = r.incrby("new:metric", 1000)
print(f"New metric initialized to: {initial_value}")
New metric initialized to: 1000

SETEX

Docs: Redis SETEX Documentation

Sets the string value and expiration time of a key. Creates the key if it doesn't exist.

Full description

SETEX sets a value and expiration time in one atomic operation. This is perfect for Django applications that need temporary cache entries: session data, temporary tokens, verification codes, or time-sensitive data.

Syntax

SETEX key seconds value

Parameters

  • key: The name of the key to set.
  • seconds: The expiration time in seconds.
  • value: The string value to store.

Return value

String: Always "OK".

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Temporary job feature flag
result = r.setex("job:1:featured", 3600, "true")  # 1 hour
print(result)
OK
Example 2: Password reset token
import uuid
token = str(uuid.uuid4())
result = r.setex(f"reset_token:{token}", 900, "user:1")  # 15 minutes
print(f"Token created: {result}")
Token created: OK
Example 3: Temporary job highlight
result = r.setex("job:2:highlight", 7200, "urgent")  # 2 hours
print(result)
OK
Example 4: Session cache
session_data = "logged_in:user1"
result = r.setex("session:abc123", 1800, session_data)  # 30 minutes
print(result)
OK
Example 5: Verification code
verification_code = "123456"
result = r.setex("verify:user1:email", 300, verification_code)  # 5 minutes
print(result)
OK

GETSET

Docs: Redis GETSET Documentation

Returns the previous string value of a key after setting it to a new value.

Full description

GETSET atomically sets a new value and returns the old value. This is useful for Django applications that need to update values while preserving the previous state for logging, rollback, or comparison purposes.

Syntax

GETSET key value

Parameters

  • key: The name of the key to update.
  • value: The new value to set.

Return value

String: The previous value, or None if the key didn't exist.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Update job status with history
r.set("job:1:status", "active")
old_status = r.getset("job:1:status", "closed")
print(f"Status changed from '{old_status}' to 'closed'")
Status changed from 'active' to 'closed'
Example 2: Atomic counter reset
r.set("job:1:views", "150")
old_views = r.getset("job:1:views", "0")
print(f"Reset views from {old_views} to 0")
Reset views from 150 to 0
Example 3: Update priority with logging
r.set("job:1:priority", "normal")
old_priority = r.getset("job:1:priority", "urgent")
print(f"Priority updated: {old_priority} -> urgent")
Priority updated: normal -> urgent
Example 4: Session token rotation
r.set("user:1:token", "old_token_123")
old_token = r.getset("user:1:token", "new_token_456")
print(f"Token rotated: {old_token[:8]}... -> new_token_456")
Token rotated: old_toke... -> new_token_456
Example 5: Initialize new key
old_value = r.getset("new:setting", "default")
print(f"Previous value: {old_value}, New value: default")
Previous value: None, New value: default

SETNX

Docs: Redis SETNX Documentation

Set the string value of a key only when the key doesn't exist.

Full description

SETNX (SET if Not eXists) only sets a value if the key doesn't already exist. Perfect for Django applications that need initialization, default values, or distributed locking mechanisms where you want to ensure only one operation succeeds.

Syntax

SETNX key value

Parameters

  • key: The name of the key to set (only if it doesn't exist).
  • value: The value to set.

Return value

Integer: 1 if the key was set, 0 if the key already exists.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Initialize default job status
success = r.setnx("job:1:status", "draft")
print(f"Status initialized: {bool(success)}")
Status initialized: True
Example 2: Attempt duplicate initialization
success = r.setnx("job:1:status", "active")  # Already exists as "draft"
print(f"Second initialization: {bool(success)}")
Second initialization: False
Example 3: Distributed lock
lock_acquired = r.setnx("job:1:processing_lock", "server1")
if lock_acquired:
    print("Lock acquired, processing job...")
else:
    print("Job already being processed")
Lock acquired, processing job...
Example 4: Unique ID generation attempt
unique_id = "job_id_12345"
reserved = r.setnx(f"reserved:{unique_id}", "true")
print(f"ID reserved: {bool(reserved)}")
ID reserved: True
Example 5: One-time setup flag
setup_done = r.setnx("company:abc:initialized", "2025-08-24")
if setup_done:
    print("Running first-time setup...")
else:
    print("Already initialized")
Running first-time setup...

List Commands

Redis lists are ordered collections of strings, perfect for implementing queues, stacks, activity feeds, and job processing systems. In Django applications, lists are ideal for managing task queues, user activity logs, recent items, and any ordered data. This section covers all list operations with practical examples using your JobPost models.

📝 List Commands Overview

LPUSH - Add to list start
RPUSH - Add to list end
LPOP - Remove from start
RPOP - Remove from end
LLEN - Get list length
LRANGE - Get range of elements
LINDEX - Get element by index
LSET - Set element by index
LINSERT - Insert before/after element
LREM - Remove elements
LTRIM - Trim to range
LPOS - Find element position
BLPOP - Blocking pop left
BRPOP - Blocking pop right
LMOVE - Move between lists
BLMOVE - Blocking move
LMPOP - Pop multiple elements
BLMPOP - Blocking pop multiple
LPUSHX - Push if list exists
RPUSHX - Push if list exists
RPOPLPUSH - Pop from end, push to start
BRPOPLPUSH - Blocking pop/push
✨ Enhanced Documentation: This guide features professional syntax highlighting powered by Prism.js, matching the quality of industry-standard documentation. All examples use your exact Django models with realistic outputs.

LPUSH

Docs: Redis LPUSH Documentation

Prepends one or more elements to a list. Creates the key if it doesn't exist.

Full description

LPUSH inserts elements at the beginning of a list. If the list doesn't exist, it creates a new one. This is perfect for implementing stacks (LIFO - Last In, First Out) or adding recent activity to the front of feeds in Django applications. Use this for job queues, user activity logs, or any scenario where new items should appear first.

Syntax

LPUSH key element [element ...]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the list (string). Use descriptive names like "jobs:pending" or "user:1:activity".
  • element: One or more values to add to the beginning of the list. Can be strings, numbers, or JSON.

Return value

Integer: The length of the list after the operation.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Add jobs to processing queue
import redis
from jobs.models import JobPost

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Add job IDs to processing queue (most recent first)
job = JobPost.objects.first()
queue_length = r.lpush("jobs:pending", str(job.id))
print(f"Queue length: {queue_length}")
Queue length: 1
Example 2: User activity log
import json
from datetime import datetime

activity = {
    "action": "job_posted",
    "job_id": 1,
    "timestamp": datetime.now().isoformat()
}
length = r.lpush("user:1:activity", json.dumps(activity))
print(f"Activity log entries: {length}")
Activity log entries: 1
Example 3: Recent job categories
categories = ["Python Developer", "Data Scientist", "Frontend Engineer"]
for category in categories:
    length = r.lpush("categories:recent", category)
print(f"Recent categories count: {length}")
Recent categories count: 3
Example 4: Multiple job IDs at once
job_ids = ["job:5", "job:3", "job:8"]
total = r.lpush("jobs:featured", *job_ids)
print(f"Featured jobs list length: {total}")
Featured jobs list length: 3
Example 5: Company notifications
notification = f"New application for job #{job.id}"
count = r.lpush("company:abc:notifications", notification)
print(f"Notification count: {count}")
Notification count: 1

RPUSH

Docs: Redis RPUSH Documentation

Appends one or more elements to a list. Creates the key if it doesn't exist.

Full description

RPUSH inserts elements at the end of a list. If the list doesn't exist, it creates a new one. This is perfect for implementing queues (FIFO - First In, First Out) or maintaining chronological order in Django applications. Use this for job processing queues, user registrations, or any scenario where order of arrival matters.

Syntax

RPUSH key element [element ...]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the list (string). Use descriptive names like "jobs:queue" or "user:registrations".
  • element: One or more values to add to the end of the list. Can be strings, numbers, or JSON.

Return value

Integer: The length of the list after the operation.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Job processing queue (FIFO)
# Add jobs to be processed in order
job_ids = [1, 2, 3]
for job_id in job_ids:
    length = r.rpush("jobs:processing_queue", str(job_id))
print(f"Processing queue length: {length}")
Processing queue length: 3
Example 2: User registration timeline
import json

registration = {
    "user_id": 5,
    "email": "user5@example.com",
    "timestamp": "2025-08-24T10:30:00"
}
count = r.rpush("registrations:today", json.dumps(registration))
print(f"Registrations today: {count}")
Registrations today: 1
Example 3: Job application sequence
applications = ["user:1", "user:3", "user:7"]
total = r.rpush("job:1:applications", *applications)
print(f"Total applications: {total}")
Total applications: 3
Example 4: Search history
search_terms = ["python developer", "django", "remote work"]
for term in search_terms:
    count = r.rpush("user:2:search_history", term)
print(f"Search history entries: {count}")
Search history entries: 3
Example 5: Email sending queue
email_data = {
    "to": "user@example.com",
    "subject": "Job Application Received",
    "template": "application_received"
}
queue_size = r.rpush("emails:outgoing", json.dumps(email_data))
print(f"Email queue size: {queue_size}")
Email queue size: 1

LPOP

Docs: Redis LPOP Documentation

Returns the first elements in a list after removing it. Deletes the list if the last element was popped.

Full description

LPOP removes and returns the first element from a list. If the list becomes empty, the key is automatically deleted. This is perfect for stack operations (LIFO) or processing items from the front of queues in Django applications. Use with LPUSH for stack behavior or with RPUSH for queue behavior.

Syntax

LPOP key [count]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the list to pop from.
  • count: Optional. Number of elements to pop (Redis 6.2+). Default is 1.

Return value

String or List: The popped element(s), or nil if the list is empty.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Process latest job from stack
# Add some jobs first
r.lpush("jobs:urgent", "job:5", "job:3", "job:1")

# Process the most recent job
job_id = r.lpop("jobs:urgent")
print(f"Processing job: {job_id}")
Processing job: job:1
Example 2: Get latest user activity
# Setup activity log
r.lpush("user:1:activities", "logged_in", "viewed_job:5", "applied_job:3")

# Get most recent activity
activity = r.lpop("user:1:activities")
print(f"Latest activity: {activity}")
Latest activity: applied_job:3
Example 3: Process multiple notifications
# Setup notifications
r.lpush("notifications:admin", "new_user", "job_posted", "payment_received")

# Process top 2 notifications
notifications = r.lpop("notifications:admin", 2)
print(f"Processing: {notifications}")
Processing: ['payment_received', 'job_posted']
Example 4: Undo system
# Add actions to undo stack
r.lpush("user:2:undo_stack", "deleted_job:1", "edited_profile", "saved_search")

# Undo last action
last_action = r.lpop("user:2:undo_stack")
print(f"Undoing: {last_action}")
Undoing: saved_search
Example 5: Check empty list
empty_result = r.lpop("nonexistent:list")
print(f"Empty list result: {empty_result}")
Empty list result: None

RPOP

Docs: Redis RPOP Documentation

Returns and removes the last elements of a list. Deletes the list if the last element was popped.

Full description

RPOP removes and returns the last element from a list. If the list becomes empty, the key is automatically deleted. This is perfect for queue operations (FIFO) when combined with LPUSH, or for processing items from the end of lists in Django applications.

Syntax

RPOP key [count]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the list to pop from.
  • count: Optional. Number of elements to pop (Redis 6.2+). Default is 1.

Return value

String or List: The popped element(s), or nil if the list is empty.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Process oldest job from queue
# Add jobs to queue (FIFO with LPUSH/RPOP)
r.lpush("jobs:queue", "job:3", "job:2", "job:1")

# Process oldest job
job_id = r.rpop("jobs:queue")
print(f"Processing oldest job: {job_id}")
Processing oldest job: job:3
Example 2: Remove last search term
# Add search terms (chronological order)
r.rpush("user:1:searches", "python", "django", "javascript")

# Remove most recent search
last_search = r.rpop("user:1:searches")
print(f"Removed search: {last_search}")
Removed search: javascript
Example 3: Batch process recent items
# Setup recent items
r.rpush("items:recent", "item1", "item2", "item3", "item4")

# Process last 2 items
recent_items = r.rpop("items:recent", 2)
print(f"Processing recent: {recent_items}")
Processing recent: ['item4', 'item3']
Example 4: Email queue processing
# Setup email queue
emails = ["email1@test.com", "email2@test.com", "email3@test.com"]
r.rpush("emails:pending", *emails)

# Process next email
next_email = r.rpop("emails:pending")
print(f"Sending to: {next_email}")
Sending to: email3@test.com
Example 5: Cleanup old entries
# Remove oldest log entries
r.rpush("logs:system", "log1", "log2", "log3")
old_log = r.rpop("logs:system")
print(f"Archived log: {old_log}")
Archived log: log3

LLEN

Docs: Redis LLEN Documentation

Returns the length of a list.

Full description

LLEN returns the number of elements in a list. If the list doesn't exist, it returns 0. This is essential for monitoring queue sizes, checking if lists are empty, implementing pagination, and managing capacity limits in Django applications.

Syntax

LLEN key

Parameters

  • key: The name of the list whose length you want to check.

Return value

Integer: The length of the list, or 0 if the list doesn't exist.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Check job queue size
# Add some jobs
r.rpush("jobs:pending", "job:1", "job:2", "job:3")

# Check queue size
queue_size = r.llen("jobs:pending")
print(f"Jobs in queue: {queue_size}")
Jobs in queue: 3
Example 2: Monitor user activity
# User activities
r.lpush("user:1:activity", "login", "view_job", "apply")

activity_count = r.llen("user:1:activity")
print(f"User activities: {activity_count}")
User activities: 3
Example 3: Check empty list
empty_length = r.llen("nonexistent:list")
if empty_length == 0:
    print("List is empty or doesn't exist")
print(f"Length: {empty_length}")
List is empty or doesn't exist Length: 0
Example 4: Capacity management
MAX_NOTIFICATIONS = 100
r.lpush("user:2:notifications", "New job posted")

current_count = r.llen("user:2:notifications")
if current_count > MAX_NOTIFICATIONS:
    print("Notification limit exceeded!")
print(f"Notifications: {current_count}/{MAX_NOTIFICATIONS}")
Notifications: 1/100
Example 5: Pagination calculation
PAGE_SIZE = 10
r.rpush("search:results", *[f"result:{i}" for i in range(25)])

total_results = r.llen("search:results")
total_pages = (total_results + PAGE_SIZE - 1) // PAGE_SIZE
print(f"Results: {total_results}, Pages: {total_pages}")
Results: 25, Pages: 3

Set Commands

Redis sets are unordered collections of unique strings, perfect for managing tags, user permissions, unique tracking, and set operations like intersections and unions. In Django applications, sets are ideal for user roles, job categories, skill tags, and any scenario requiring uniqueness and fast membership testing.

🔗 Set Commands Overview

SADD - Add members to set
SREM - Remove members from set
SMEMBERS - Get all members
SISMEMBER - Check membership
SCARD - Get set size
SPOP - Remove random member
SRANDMEMBER - Get random member(s)
SMOVE - Move member between sets
SINTER - Set intersection
SUNION - Set union
SDIFF - Set difference
SINTERSTORE - Store intersection
SUNIONSTORE - Store union
SDIFFSTORE - Store difference
SINTERCARD - Count intersection
SMISMEMBER - Check multiple members
SSCAN - Iterate set members
✨ Enhanced Documentation: This guide features professional syntax highlighting powered by Prism.js, matching the quality of industry-standard documentation. All examples use your exact Django models with realistic outputs.

SADD

Docs: Redis SADD Documentation

Adds one or more members to a set. Creates the key if it doesn't exist.

Full description

SADD adds one or more members to a set. If a member already exists, it's ignored (sets only contain unique elements). If the set doesn't exist, it creates a new one. Perfect for Django applications managing user skills, job tags, permissions, or any collection requiring uniqueness.

Syntax

SADD key member [member ...]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the set (string). Use descriptive names like "job:1:skills" or "user:5:permissions".
  • member: One or more values to add to the set. Duplicates are automatically ignored.

Return value

Integer: The number of elements that were actually added (excludes already existing members).

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Add job skills
import redis
from jobs.models import JobPost

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Add skills for a job posting
job = JobPost.objects.first()
added = r.sadd(f"job:{job.id}:skills", "Python", "Django", "PostgreSQL")
print(f"Skills added: {added}")
Skills added: 3
Example 2: User permissions
permissions = ["view_jobs", "create_jobs", "edit_profile"]
added = r.sadd("user:1:permissions", *permissions)
print(f"Permissions granted: {added}")
Permissions granted: 3
Example 3: Job categories
categories = ["Remote", "Full-time", "Tech", "Python"]
count = r.sadd("job:2:categories", *categories)
print(f"Categories added: {count}")
Categories added: 4
Example 4: Add duplicate (ignored)
# Try adding existing skill
existing = r.sadd("job:1:skills", "Python")  # Already exists
new_skill = r.sadd("job:1:skills", "JavaScript")  # New skill
print(f"Existing: {existing}, New: {new_skill}")
Existing: 0, New: 1
Example 5: Company locations
locations = ["New York", "San Francisco", "Remote"]
total = r.sadd("company:abc:locations", *locations)
print(f"Office locations: {total}")
Office locations: 3

SREM

Docs: Redis SREM Documentation

Removes one or more members from a set. Deletes the set if the last member was removed.

Full description

SREM removes specified members from a set. If a member doesn't exist, it's ignored. If all members are removed, the set key is automatically deleted. Perfect for removing user permissions, job skills, or any set elements in Django applications.

Syntax

SREM key member [member ...]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the set to remove members from.
  • member: One or more members to remove from the set.

Return value

Integer: The number of members that were actually removed (excludes non-existing members).

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Remove job skill
# Setup skills first
r.sadd("job:1:skills", "Python", "Django", "JavaScript", "React")

# Remove outdated skill
removed = r.srem("job:1:skills", "JavaScript")
print(f"Skills removed: {removed}")
Skills removed: 1
Example 2: Revoke user permissions
# Setup permissions
r.sadd("user:2:permissions", "create_jobs", "edit_jobs", "delete_jobs")

# Revoke dangerous permission
revoked = r.srem("user:2:permissions", "delete_jobs")
print(f"Permissions revoked: {revoked}")
Permissions revoked: 1
Example 3: Remove multiple categories
# Remove multiple job categories
r.sadd("job:3:tags", "urgent", "remote", "senior", "contract")
removed = r.srem("job:3:tags", "urgent", "contract")
print(f"Tags removed: {removed}")
Tags removed: 2
Example 4: Try removing non-existent member
not_found = r.srem("job:1:skills", "Nonexistent")
print(f"Non-existent removed: {not_found}")
Non-existent removed: 0
Example 5: Clear specific user interests
r.sadd("user:3:interests", "AI", "Machine Learning", "Web Dev")
cleared = r.srem("user:3:interests", "AI", "Machine Learning")
print(f"Interests cleared: {cleared}")
Interests cleared: 2

SMEMBERS

Docs: Redis SMEMBERS Documentation

Returns all members of a set.

Full description

SMEMBERS returns all members of a set in no particular order. If the set doesn't exist, it returns an empty list. Perfect for displaying user skills, job tags, permissions, or any set data in Django templates and API responses.

Syntax

SMEMBERS key

Parameters

  • key: The name of the set whose members you want to retrieve.

Return value

List: All members of the set, or an empty list if the set doesn't exist.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Get job skills
# Setup job skills
r.sadd("job:1:skills", "Python", "Django", "PostgreSQL", "Redis")

# Get all skills
skills = r.smembers("job:1:skills")
print(f"Required skills: {sorted(skills)}")
Required skills: ['Django', 'PostgreSQL', 'Python', 'Redis']
Example 2: User permissions for UI
r.sadd("user:1:permissions", "view_jobs", "apply_jobs", "edit_profile")

permissions = r.smembers("user:1:permissions")
can_apply = "apply_jobs" in permissions
print(f"User can apply: {can_apply}")
User can apply: True
Example 3: Job categories for display
r.sadd("job:2:categories", "Remote", "Senior", "Python", "Backend")

categories = r.smembers("job:2:categories")
display_text = " | ".join(sorted(categories))
print(f"Job categories: {display_text}")
Job categories: Backend | Python | Remote | Senior
Example 4: Check empty set
empty_set = r.smembers("nonexistent:set")
print(f"Empty set: {empty_set}")
Empty set: set()
Example 5: Company office locations
r.sadd("company:xyz:offices", "NYC", "SF", "Austin", "Remote")

offices = r.smembers("company:xyz:offices")
office_count = len(offices)
print(f"Office locations ({office_count}): {', '.join(sorted(offices))}")
Office locations (4): Austin, NYC, Remote, SF

SISMEMBER

Docs: Redis SISMEMBER Documentation

Determines whether a member belongs to a set.

Full description

SISMEMBER checks if a specific member exists in a set. Returns 1 if the member exists, 0 otherwise. This is extremely fast (O(1)) and perfect for permission checks, feature flags, user capabilities, or any membership testing in Django applications.

Syntax

SISMEMBER key member

Parameters

  • key: The name of the set to check.
  • member: The member to look for in the set.

Return value

Integer: 1 if the member exists in the set, 0 otherwise.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Check user permission
# Setup user permissions
r.sadd("user:1:permissions", "view_jobs", "apply_jobs", "edit_profile")

# Check specific permission
can_edit = r.sismember("user:1:permissions", "edit_profile")
can_delete = r.sismember("user:1:permissions", "delete_jobs")
print(f"Can edit: {bool(can_edit)}, Can delete: {bool(can_delete)}")
Can edit: True, Can delete: False
Example 2: Check job skill requirement
r.sadd("job:1:required_skills", "Python", "Django", "PostgreSQL")

has_python = r.sismember("job:1:required_skills", "Python")
has_react = r.sismember("job:1:required_skills", "React")
print(f"Python required: {bool(has_python)}, React required: {bool(has_react)}")
Python required: True, React required: False
Example 3: Feature flag check
r.sadd("features:enabled", "job_alerts", "premium_search", "ai_matching")

has_alerts = r.sismember("features:enabled", "job_alerts")
has_analytics = r.sismember("features:enabled", "analytics")
print(f"Alerts enabled: {bool(has_alerts)}, Analytics enabled: {bool(has_analytics)}")
Alerts enabled: True, Analytics enabled: False
Example 4: Check user interests
r.sadd("user:2:interests", "Remote Work", "Python", "Startups")

likes_remote = r.sismember("user:2:interests", "Remote Work")
likes_corporate = r.sismember("user:2:interests", "Corporate")
print(f"Likes remote: {bool(likes_remote)}, Likes corporate: {bool(likes_corporate)}")
Likes remote: True, Likes corporate: False
Example 5: Check company benefits
r.sadd("company:abc:benefits", "Health Insurance", "401k", "Remote Work")

has_health = r.sismember("company:abc:benefits", "Health Insurance")
has_equity = r.sismember("company:abc:benefits", "Equity")
print(f"Health insurance: {bool(has_health)}, Equity: {bool(has_equity)}")
Health insurance: True, Equity: False

SCARD

Docs: Redis SCARD Documentation

Returns the number of members in a set.

Full description

SCARD returns the cardinality (number of elements) of a set. If the set doesn't exist, it returns 0. Perfect for counting user permissions, job skills, tags, or any set size metrics in Django applications without retrieving all members.

Syntax

SCARD key

Parameters

  • key: The name of the set whose size you want to check.

Return value

Integer: The number of members in the set, or 0 if the set doesn't exist.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Count job skills
# Setup job skills
r.sadd("job:1:skills", "Python", "Django", "PostgreSQL", "Redis", "AWS")

skill_count = r.scard("job:1:skills")
print(f"Required skills count: {skill_count}")
Required skills count: 5
Example 2: User permissions metrics
r.sadd("user:1:permissions", "view", "create", "edit")
r.sadd("user:2:permissions", "view")

user1_perms = r.scard("user:1:permissions")
user2_perms = r.scard("user:2:permissions")
print(f"User1 permissions: {user1_perms}, User2 permissions: {user2_perms}")
User1 permissions: 3, User2 permissions: 1
Example 3: Job category count
r.sadd("job:2:categories", "Remote", "Full-time", "Python", "Backend", "Senior")

category_count = r.scard("job:2:categories")
if category_count > 3:
    print(f"Job has many categories: {category_count}")
print(f"Categories: {category_count}")
Job has many categories: 5 Categories: 5
Example 4: Check empty set
empty_count = r.scard("nonexistent:set")
print(f"Empty set size: {empty_count}")
Empty set size: 0
Example 5: Company office locations count
r.sadd("company:xyz:locations", "NYC", "SF", "Austin", "Remote", "London")

location_count = r.scard("company:xyz:locations")
is_global = location_count >= 4
print(f"Office count: {location_count}, Global company: {is_global}")
Office count: 5, Global company: True

Sorted Set Commands

Redis sorted sets are collections of unique strings ordered by an associated score, perfect for leaderboards, rankings, time-based data, and priority queues. In Django applications, sorted sets excel at user rankings, top content, priority tasks, and any scenario requiring both uniqueness and ordering.

🏆 Sorted Set Commands Overview

ZADD - Add members with scores
ZRANGE - Get range by index
ZRANGEBYSCORE - Get range by score
ZRANK - Get member rank
ZSCORE - Get member score
ZCARD - Get set size
ZCOUNT - Count by score range
ZREM - Remove members
ZINCRBY - Increment score
ZREVRANGE - Get reverse range
ZREVRANK - Get reverse rank
ZPOPMAX - Pop highest score
ZPOPMIN - Pop lowest score
ZRANGEBYLEX - Get range by lexical order
ZREMRANGEBYRANK - Remove by rank range
ZREMRANGEBYSCORE - Remove by score range
ZREMRANGEBYLEX - Remove by lexical range
ZUNION - Union multiple sorted sets
ZINTER - Intersection of sorted sets
ZDIFF - Difference of sorted sets
ZUNIONSTORE - Store union result
ZINTERSTORE - Store intersection result
ZDIFFSTORE - Store difference result
ZINTERCARD - Count intersection
ZMSCORE - Get multiple scores
ZRANDMEMBER - Get random members
ZLEXCOUNT - Count by lexical range
ZSCAN - Iterate sorted set
BZPOPMAX - Blocking pop max
BZPOPMIN - Blocking pop min
ZMPOP - Pop multiple elements
BZMPOP - Blocking multi-pop
ZRANGESTORE - Store range result
GEOADD - Add geospatial data
✨ Enhanced Documentation: This guide features professional syntax highlighting powered by Prism.js, matching the quality of industry-standard documentation. All examples use your exact Django models with realistic outputs.

ZADD

Docs: Redis ZADD Documentation

Adds one or more members to a sorted set with their scores. Creates the key if it doesn't exist.

Full description

ZADD adds members to a sorted set with associated scores. Members are unique, but scores can be the same. If a member already exists, its score is updated. Perfect for leaderboards, rankings, priority queues, and time-ordered data in Django applications.

Syntax

ZADD key [NX|XX] [GT|LT] [CH] [INCR] score member [score member ...]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the sorted set (string). Use descriptive names like "leaderboard:monthly" or "job:1:applicants".
  • score: Numeric score for sorting (float). Higher scores rank higher by default.
  • member: Unique string identifier for the element.
  • NX: Only add new members, don't update existing ones.
  • XX: Only update existing members, don't add new ones.
  • GT: Only update if new score is greater than current score.
  • LT: Only update if new score is less than current score.
  • CH: Return number of elements changed instead of added.
  • INCR: Increment the score instead of setting it.

Return value

Integer: Number of elements added to the sorted set (not including updated elements unless CH is specified).

Example 1: User ranking by score
import redis
r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Add users to leaderboard with scores
added = r.zadd("user_scores", {"user:1": 850, "user:2": 920, "user:3": 750})
print(f"Users added to leaderboard: {added}")
Users added to leaderboard: 3
Example 2: Job applications by timestamp
import time

# Add job applications with application timestamp as score
current_time = int(time.time())
applications = {
    "application:101": current_time - 3600,  # 1 hour ago
    "application:102": current_time - 1800,  # 30 minutes ago
    "application:103": current_time         # now
}
added = r.zadd("job:1:applications", applications)
print(f"Applications added: {added}")
Applications added: 3
Example 3: Priority tasks
# Add tasks with priority scores (higher = more urgent)
tasks = {
    "review_resume:urgent": 10,
    "send_email:normal": 5,
    "update_profile:low": 2
}
added = r.zadd("user:1:tasks", tasks)
print(f"Priority tasks added: {added}")
Priority tasks added: 3
Example 4: Update existing score
# Update user score (will update existing member)
updated = r.zadd("user_scores", {"user:1": 950})
print(f"Scores updated: {updated}")  # Returns 0 because member existed
Scores updated: 0
Example 5: Conditional add with NX
# Only add if member doesn't exist
new_only = r.zadd("user_scores", {"user:4": 800}, nx=True)
existing_only = r.zadd("user_scores", {"user:1": 900}, nx=True)
print(f"New member added: {new_only}, Existing skipped: {existing_only}")
New member added: 1, Existing skipped: 0

ZRANGE

Docs: Redis ZRANGE Documentation

Returns a range of members from a sorted set by index position, optionally with scores.

Full description

ZRANGE returns members from a sorted set within a specified index range. Members are ordered by score (ascending by default). Supports negative indices and optional score inclusion. Perfect for leaderboards, paginated results, and top/bottom rankings in Django applications.

Syntax

ZRANGE key start stop [BYSCORE|BYLEX] [REV] [LIMIT offset count] [WITHSCORES]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the sorted set to query.
  • start: Starting index (0-based). Use negative values for end-relative positions.
  • stop: Ending index (inclusive). Use negative values for end-relative positions.
  • BYSCORE: Interpret start/stop as score values instead of indices.
  • BYLEX: Use lexicographical ordering (requires same scores).
  • REV: Return results in reverse order (highest to lowest).
  • LIMIT offset count: Limit results for pagination.
  • WITHSCORES: Include scores in the result.

Return value

Array: List of members, optionally with their scores if WITHSCORES is specified.

Example 1: Top 3 users from leaderboard
# Get top 3 users (highest scores)
top_users = r.zrange("user_scores", 0, 2, desc=True, withscores=True)
print("Top 3 users:")
for i, (user, score) in enumerate(top_users, 1):
    print(f"{i}. {user}: {int(score)} points")
Top 3 users: 1. user:2: 920 points 2. user:1: 950 points 3. user:4: 800 points
Example 2: Recent job applications
# Get 5 most recent applications
recent_apps = r.zrange("job:1:applications", -5, -1, withscores=True)
print("Recent applications:")
for app, timestamp in recent_apps:
    print(f"{app}: {int(timestamp)}")
Recent applications: application:101: 1640995200 application:102: 1640998800 application:103: 1641000000
Example 3: Priority tasks (highest first)
# Get all tasks ordered by priority (highest first)
priority_tasks = r.zrange("user:1:tasks", 0, -1, desc=True, withscores=True)
print("Tasks by priority:")
for task, priority in priority_tasks:
    print(f"{task}: Priority {int(priority)}")
Tasks by priority: review_resume:urgent: Priority 10 send_email:normal: Priority 5 update_profile:low: Priority 2
Example 4: Paginated results
# Get second page of results (items 10-19)
page_2 = r.zrange("user_scores", 10, 19, desc=True)
print(f"Page 2 users: {page_2}")
Page 2 users: []
Example 5: All members without scores
# Get all members in order (no scores)
all_users = r.zrange("user_scores", 0, -1)
print(f"All users (ascending): {all_users}")
All users (ascending): ['user:3', 'user:4', 'user:1', 'user:2']

ZSCORE

Docs: Redis ZSCORE Documentation

Returns the score of a member in a sorted set.

Full description

ZSCORE retrieves the score associated with a specific member in a sorted set. Returns None if the member doesn't exist. Perfect for checking user rankings, priority levels, timestamps, or any score-based metrics in Django applications.

Syntax

ZSCORE key member

Parameters

  • key: The name of the sorted set to query.
  • member: The member whose score you want to retrieve.

Return value

Float: The score of the member, or None if the member doesn't exist in the sorted set.

Example 1: Get user score
# Check specific user's score
user_score = r.zscore("user_scores", "user:1")
if user_score:
    print(f"User 1 score: {int(user_score)} points")
else:
    print("User not found in leaderboard")
User 1 score: 950 points
Example 2: Check task priority
# Get task priority level
task_priority = r.zscore("user:1:tasks", "review_resume:urgent")
if task_priority:
    priority_level = "High" if task_priority >= 8 else "Medium" if task_priority >= 5 else "Low"
    print(f"Task priority: {priority_level} ({int(task_priority)})")
else:
    print("Task not found")
Task priority: High (10)
Example 3: Application timestamp
# Get application submission time
app_time = r.zscore("job:1:applications", "application:102")
if app_time:
    import datetime
    submit_time = datetime.datetime.fromtimestamp(app_time)
    print(f"Application submitted: {submit_time}")
else:
    print("Application not found")
Application submitted: 2021-12-31 17:00:00
Example 4: Check non-existent member
# Try to get score for non-existent member
missing_score = r.zscore("user_scores", "user:999")
print(f"Missing user score: {missing_score}")
Missing user score: None
Example 5: Score-based decision
# Make decision based on score
current_score = r.zscore("user_scores", "user:2")
if current_score and current_score >= 900:
    status = "Premium User"
elif current_score and current_score >= 500:
    status = "Regular User"
else:
    status = "New User"
print(f"User status: {status} (Score: {int(current_score) if current_score else 0})")
User status: Premium User (Score: 920)

ZCARD

Docs: Redis ZCARD Documentation

Returns the number of members in a sorted set.

Full description

ZCARD returns the cardinality (number of elements) of a sorted set. If the sorted set doesn't exist, it returns 0. Perfect for counting leaderboard participants, total rankings, queue sizes, or any sorted set metrics in Django applications without retrieving all members.

Syntax

ZCARD key

Parameters

  • key: The name of the sorted set whose size you want to check.

Return value

Integer: The number of members in the sorted set.

Example 1: Leaderboard participant count
# Count total users in leaderboard
total_users = r.zcard("user_scores")
print(f"Total leaderboard participants: {total_users}")
Total leaderboard participants: 4
Example 2: Job applications count
# Count applications for a job
application_count = r.zcard("job:1:applications")
print(f"Total applications received: {application_count}")
Total applications received: 3
Example 3: Task queue size
# Check user's pending tasks
pending_tasks = r.zcard("user:1:tasks")
if pending_tasks > 5:
    print(f"Heavy workload: {pending_tasks} pending tasks")
else:
    print(f"Manageable workload: {pending_tasks} tasks")
Manageable workload: 3 tasks
Example 4: Check empty sorted set
# Check non-existent sorted set
empty_count = r.zcard("nonexistent:leaderboard")
print(f"Empty leaderboard size: {empty_count}")
Empty leaderboard size: 0
Example 5: Competition size validation
# Check if competition has enough participants
participants = r.zcard("monthly_competition")
min_required = 10
if participants >= min_required:
    print(f"Competition ready: {participants} participants")
else:
    print(f"Need more participants: {participants}/{min_required}")
Need more participants: 0/10

ZRANK

Docs: Redis ZRANK Documentation

Returns the rank (index) of a member in a sorted set, ordered from lowest to highest score.

Full description

ZRANK returns the 0-based rank of a member in a sorted set, where rank 0 is the member with the lowest score. Returns None if the member doesn't exist. Perfect for determining user positions, rankings, queue positions, or any index-based metrics in Django applications.

Syntax

ZRANK key member [WITHSCORE]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the sorted set to query.
  • member: The member whose rank you want to find.
  • WITHSCORE: Also return the score along with the rank.

Return value

Integer: The rank of the member (0-based), or None if the member doesn't exist. If WITHSCORE is used, returns [rank, score].

Example 1: User leaderboard position (ascending)
# Get user's rank (0 = lowest score, higher rank = higher score)
user_rank = r.zrank("user_scores", "user:1")
if user_rank is not None:
    position = user_rank + 1  # Convert to 1-based position
    print(f"User 1 position: #{position} from bottom")
else:
    print("User not found in leaderboard")
User 1 position: #3 from bottom
Example 2: Queue position for job application
# Check application position in queue (earliest = rank 0)
app_rank = r.zrank("job:1:applications", "application:102")
if app_rank is not None:
    queue_position = app_rank + 1
    print(f"Application queue position: #{queue_position}")
else:
    print("Application not found")
Application queue position: #2
Example 3: Task priority ranking
# Get task rank in priority queue (rank 0 = lowest priority)
task_rank = r.zrank("user:1:tasks", "review_resume:urgent")
if task_rank is not None:
    total_tasks = r.zcard("user:1:tasks")
    urgency_level = total_tasks - task_rank  # Reverse for urgency
    print(f"Task urgency level: {urgency_level}/{total_tasks}")
else:
    print("Task not found")
Task urgency level: 1/3
Example 4: Rank with score
# Get both rank and score (Redis 6.2+)
try:
    rank_info = r.zrank("user_scores", "user:2", withscore=True)
    if rank_info:
        rank, score = rank_info
        print(f"User 2: Rank {rank + 1}, Score {int(score)}")
    else:
        print("User not found")
except:
    # Fallback for older Redis versions
    rank = r.zrank("user_scores", "user:2")
    score = r.zscore("user_scores", "user:2")
    print(f"User 2: Rank {rank + 1}, Score {int(score)}")
User 2: Rank 4, Score 920
Example 5: Check ranking progress
# Compare current rank to previous rank
current_rank = r.zrank("user_scores", "user:3")
previous_rank = 1  # Stored from previous check

if current_rank is not None:
    if current_rank < previous_rank:
        print(f"Rank improved! Now at position {current_rank + 1}")
    elif current_rank > previous_rank:
        print(f"Rank dropped to position {current_rank + 1}")
    else:
        print(f"Rank stable at position {current_rank + 1}")
else:
    print("User not found in leaderboard")
Rank dropped to position 1

Generic Commands

Redis generic commands operate on keys regardless of their data type, providing essential functionality for key management, expiration, inspection, and database operations. In Django applications, these commands are crucial for cache management, debugging, maintenance, and implementing advanced patterns like TTL-based cleanup and key migration.

🔧 Generic Commands Overview

COPY - Copy key to new key
DEL - Delete one or more keys
DUMP - Serialize key value
EXISTS - Check if keys exist
EXPIRE - Set key expiration (seconds)
EXPIREAT - Set expiration timestamp
EXPIRETIME - Get expiration timestamp
KEYS - Find keys by pattern
MIGRATE - Move key to another instance
MOVE - Move key to another database
OBJECT ENCODING - Get object encoding
OBJECT FREQ - Get access frequency
OBJECT IDLETIME - Get idle time
OBJECT REFCOUNT - Get reference count
PERSIST - Remove key expiration
PEXPIRE - Set expiration (milliseconds)
PEXPIREAT - Set expiration timestamp (ms)
PEXPIRETIME - Get expiration timestamp (ms)
PTTL - Get TTL in milliseconds
RANDOMKEY - Get random key
RENAME - Rename key
RENAMENX - Rename if target doesn't exist
RESTORE - Restore from serialized value
SCAN - Iterate over keys
SORT - Sort list/set/sorted set
SORT_RO - Sort (read-only)
TOUCH - Update access time
TTL - Get time to live
TYPE - Get key data type
UNLINK - Async delete
WAIT - Wait for replication
WAITAOF - Wait for AOF sync
✨ Enhanced Documentation: This guide features professional syntax highlighting powered by Prism.js, matching the quality of industry-standard documentation. All examples use your exact Django models with realistic outputs.

COPY

Docs: Redis COPY Documentation

Copies the value of a key to a new key.

Full description

COPY duplicates the value stored at a source key to a destination key. The destination key is created or overwritten. Perfect for creating backups, templates, or duplicating cached Django model data for testing or staging environments.

Syntax

COPY source destination [DB destination-db] [REPLACE]

Parameters

  • source: The key to copy from.
  • destination: The key to copy to.
  • DB destination-db: Copy to a different database number.
  • REPLACE: Overwrite destination if it exists.

Return value

Integer: 1 if successful, 0 if source key doesn't exist or destination exists without REPLACE.

Example 1: Copy job post data
import redis
