r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Set up original job data
r.hset("job:1", mapping={"title": "Python Developer", "company": "TechCorp", "salary": "95000"})

# Copy to create a template
copied = r.copy("job:1", "job:template")
print(f"Job template created: {bool(copied)}")
Job template created: True
Example 2: Backup user session
# Copy session data for backup
session_copied = r.copy("session:user1", "session:user1:backup")
print(f"Session backup created: {bool(session_copied)}")
Session backup created: True
Example 3: Copy with REPLACE flag
# Copy and overwrite existing destination
r.set("cache:key1", "original_value")
r.set("cache:key2", "existing_value")
replaced = r.copy("cache:key1", "cache:key2", replace=True)
print(f"Value replaced: {bool(replaced)}")
Value replaced: True
Example 4: Copy to different database
# Copy to database 1 (requires Redis >= 6.2)
try:
    copied_db = r.execute_command("COPY", "job:1", "job:1", "DB", "1")
    print(f"Copied to DB 1: {bool(copied_db)}")
except Exception as e:
    print(f"Cross-DB copy not available: {e}")
Cross-DB copy not available: unknown command 'COPY'
Example 5: Copy non-existent key
# Try to copy non-existent key
not_copied = r.copy("nonexistent:key", "new:key")
print(f"Non-existent key copied: {bool(not_copied)}")
Non-existent key copied: False

DEL

Docs: Redis DEL Documentation

Deletes one or more keys.

Full description

DEL removes specified keys from Redis. If a key doesn't exist, it's ignored. This is a blocking operation - use UNLINK for non-blocking deletion of large objects. Essential for cache invalidation, cleanup, and memory management in Django applications.

Syntax

DEL key [key ...]

Parameters

  • key: One or more keys to delete.

Return value

Integer: Number of keys that were actually deleted.

Example 1: Delete single cache entry
# Cache some job data first
r.set("job:1:views", "150")
r.set("job:1:status", "active")

# Delete view count cache
deleted = r.delete("job:1:views")
print(f"Cache entries deleted: {deleted}")
Cache entries deleted: 1
Example 2: Delete multiple related keys
# Delete all user session data
deleted = r.delete("session:user1", "session:user1:backup", "session:user1:temp")
print(f"Session keys deleted: {deleted}")
Session keys deleted: 2
Example 3: Clean up expired job listings
# Set up some job data
r.hset("job:expired:1", "title", "Old Job")
r.hset("job:expired:2", "title", "Another Old Job")

# Clean up expired jobs
cleanup_keys = ["job:expired:1", "job:expired:2", "job:expired:3"]
cleaned = r.delete(*cleanup_keys)
print(f"Expired jobs cleaned: {cleaned}")
Expired jobs cleaned: 2
Example 4: Cache invalidation pattern
# Invalidate related cache entries when job is updated
job_id = 1
cache_keys = [
    f"job:{job_id}:details",
    f"job:{job_id}:applicants",
    f"job:{job_id}:views",
    f"search:cache:jobs"  # Invalidate search cache too
]
invalidated = r.delete(*cache_keys)
print(f"Cache entries invalidated: {invalidated}")
Cache entries invalidated: 1
Example 5: Delete with existence check
# Check if key exists before attempting deletion
key = "temp:data:123"
if r.exists(key):
    deleted = r.delete(key)
    print(f"Temporary data deleted: {deleted}")
else:
    print("Key didn't exist")
deleted = r.delete(key)  # Try anyway
print(f"Delete attempt result: {deleted}")
Key didn't exist Delete attempt result: 0

EXISTS

Docs: Redis EXISTS Documentation

Determines whether one or more keys exist.

Full description

EXISTS checks if specified keys exist in Redis. Returns the count of existing keys. Perfect for cache hit/miss detection, conditional operations, and avoiding unnecessary database queries in Django applications.

Syntax

EXISTS key [key ...]

Parameters

  • key: One or more keys to check for existence.

Return value

Integer: Number of keys that exist among the specified keys.

Example 1: Check cache before database query
# Check if job data is cached
job_id = 1
cache_key = f"job:{job_id}:details"

if r.exists(cache_key):
    job_data = r.hgetall(cache_key)
    print(f"Cache hit! Job: {job_data.get('title', 'N/A')}")
else:
    print("Cache miss - would fetch from database")
    # job = JobPost.objects.get(id=job_id)
    # Cache the data...
Cache miss - would fetch from database
Example 2: Check multiple session keys
# Set up some session data
r.set("session:user1", "active")
r.set("session:user2", "active")

# Check which sessions exist
session_keys = ["session:user1", "session:user2", "session:user3"]
existing_count = r.exists(*session_keys)
print(f"Active sessions: {existing_count} out of {len(session_keys)}")
Active sessions: 2 out of 3
Example 3: Conditional cache update
# Only update cache if it doesn't exist
cache_key = "expensive:computation:result"
if not r.exists(cache_key):
    # Simulate expensive computation
    result = "computed_value_123"
    r.setex(cache_key, 3600, result)  # Cache for 1 hour
    print("Cache updated with new computation")
else:
    print("Using existing cached result")
Cache updated with new computation
Example 4: Verify cleanup completion
# Check if cleanup was successful
cleanup_keys = ["temp:data:1", "temp:data:2", "temp:data:3"]
remaining = r.exists(*cleanup_keys)
if remaining == 0:
    print("Cleanup completed successfully")
else:
    print(f"Warning: {remaining} temporary keys still exist")
Cleanup completed successfully
Example 5: Feature flag check
# Check if feature flags are set
feature_flags = ["feature:new_ui", "feature:beta_access", "feature:advanced_search"]
enabled_features = r.exists(*feature_flags)
print(f"Enabled features: {enabled_features}")

# Check specific feature
new_ui_enabled = bool(r.exists("feature:new_ui"))
print(f"New UI enabled: {new_ui_enabled}")
Enabled features: 1 New UI enabled: True

EXPIRE

Docs: Redis EXPIRE Documentation

Sets the expiration time of a key in seconds.

Full description

EXPIRE sets a timeout on a key. After the timeout expires, the key is automatically deleted. Essential for implementing TTL-based cache invalidation, session management, and temporary data cleanup in Django applications.

Syntax

EXPIRE key seconds [NX|XX|GT|LT]

Parameters

  • key: The key to set expiration on.
  • seconds: Expiration time in seconds.
  • NX: Set expiry only if key has no expiry.
  • XX: Set expiry only if key already has expiry.
  • GT: Set expiry only if new expiry is greater than current.
  • LT: Set expiry only if new expiry is less than current.

Return value

Integer: 1 if timeout was set, 0 if key doesn't exist or timeout couldn't be set.

Example 1: Cache with 1-hour expiration
# Cache job search results for 1 hour
search_results = ["job:1", "job:5", "job:12"]
r.set("search:python_developer", ",".join(search_results))
expired = r.expire("search:python_developer", 3600)  # 1 hour
print(f"Search cache will expire in 1 hour: {bool(expired)}")
Search cache will expire in 1 hour: True
Example 2: Session timeout
# Set session to expire in 30 minutes
r.set("session:user123", "logged_in")
session_timeout = r.expire("session:user123", 1800)  # 30 minutes
print(f"Session timeout set: {bool(session_timeout)}")
Session timeout set: True
Example 3: Temporary rate limiting
# Rate limit: max 100 requests per minute
user_ip = "192.168.1.100"
rate_limit_key = f"rate_limit:{user_ip}"
r.incr(rate_limit_key)
r.expire(rate_limit_key, 60)  # Reset counter every minute
current_requests = int(r.get(rate_limit_key))
print(f"Requests from {user_ip}: {current_requests}/100")
Requests from 192.168.1.100: 1/100
Example 4: Email verification token
import uuid
# Create verification token that expires in 24 hours
token = str(uuid.uuid4())
r.set(f"verify_email:{token}", "user:123")
expired = r.expire(f"verify_email:{token}", 86400)  # 24 hours
print(f"Verification token expires in 24h: {bool(expired)}")
Verification token expires in 24h: True
Example 5: Conditional expiration (Redis 7.0+)
# Only set expiration if key doesn't already have one
r.set("persistent:config", "important_data")
try:
    # NX: only set expiry if no expiry exists
    set_expiry = r.execute_command("EXPIRE", "persistent:config", "3600", "NX")
    print(f"Expiry set on persistent config: {bool(set_expiry)}")
except:
    # Fallback for older Redis versions
    if r.ttl("persistent:config") == -1:  # No expiry set
        r.expire("persistent:config", 3600)
        print("Expiry set using fallback method")
Expiry set using fallback method

TTL

Docs: Redis TTL Documentation

Returns the expiration time in seconds of a key.

Full description

TTL returns the remaining time to live of a key that has a timeout. Perfect for monitoring cache expiration, implementing cache refresh logic, and debugging TTL-based systems in Django applications.

Syntax

TTL key

Parameters

  • key: The key to check TTL for.

Return value

Integer: TTL in seconds, -1 if key exists but has no expiry, -2 if key doesn't exist.

Example 1: Check cache freshness
# Check how long until cache expires
cache_key = "search:python_developer"
ttl_seconds = r.ttl(cache_key)
if ttl_seconds > 0:
    minutes_left = ttl_seconds // 60
    print(f"Cache expires in {minutes_left} minutes ({ttl_seconds}s)")
elif ttl_seconds == -1:
    print("Cache exists but never expires")
else:
    print("Cache doesn't exist")
Cache expires in 59 minutes (3581s)
Example 2: Session monitoring
# Monitor session expiration
session_key = "session:user123"
ttl = r.ttl(session_key)
if ttl > 0:
    if ttl < 300:  # Less than 5 minutes
        print(f"Session expires soon: {ttl} seconds left")
    else:
        print(f"Session active: {ttl//60} minutes remaining")
else:
    print("Session expired or doesn't exist")
Session active: 29 minutes remaining
Example 3: Rate limit check
# Check rate limit reset time
rate_key = "rate_limit:192.168.1.100"
reset_time = r.ttl(rate_key)
current_count = r.get(rate_key) or 0
if reset_time > 0:
    print(f"Rate limit resets in {reset_time}s (current: {current_count})")
else:
    print("Rate limit not active")
Rate limit resets in 59s (current: 1)
Example 4: Cache refresh decision
# Decide whether to refresh cache
def should_refresh_cache(cache_key, refresh_threshold=300):
    ttl = r.ttl(cache_key)
    if ttl == -2:
        return True, "Cache doesn't exist"
    elif ttl == -1:
        return False, "Cache never expires"
    elif ttl < refresh_threshold:
        return True, f"Cache expires soon ({ttl}s)"
    else:
        return False, f"Cache fresh ({ttl}s remaining)"

refresh, reason = should_refresh_cache("search:python_developer")
print(f"Should refresh: {refresh} - {reason}")
Should refresh: False - Cache fresh (3575s remaining)
Example 5: Batch TTL monitoring
# Monitor multiple cache keys
cache_keys = ["session:user123", "search:python_developer", "rate_limit:192.168.1.100"]
for key in cache_keys:
    ttl = r.ttl(key)
    status = "active" if ttl > 0 else "expired/missing" if ttl == -2 else "persistent"
    print(f"{key}: {status} ({ttl}s)")
session:user123: active (1765s) search:python_developer: active (3569s) rate_limit:192.168.1.100: active (53s)

Bloom Filter Commands

Redis Bloom Filters are space-efficient probabilistic data structures used to test whether an element is a member of a set. They can tell you with certainty if an element is NOT in the set, but can only tell you with probability if an element IS in the set. Perfect for Django applications requiring fast membership testing with minimal memory usage - like checking if usernames exist, preventing duplicate operations, or implementing cache-aside patterns.

🌸 Bloom Filter Commands Overview

ADD - Add single item to filter
CARD - Get filter cardinality estimate
EXISTS - Check if item exists
INFO - Get filter information
INSERT - Add items with options
LOADCHUNK - Restore filter chunk
MADD - Add multiple items
MEXISTS - Check multiple items
RESERVE - Create new filter
SCANDUMP - Dump filter for backup
📝 Note: Bloom Filter commands require the RedisBloom module. These examples demonstrate practical Django use cases with realistic probabilistic data handling patterns.

ADD

Docs: Redis BF.ADD Documentation

Adds an item to a Bloom Filter.

Full description

BF.ADD adds a single item to an existing Bloom Filter. If the filter doesn't exist, it creates one with default parameters. Returns 1 if the item was newly added, 0 if it might have already existed. Perfect for Django applications tracking user actions, preventing duplicate submissions, or maintaining sets of processed items.

Syntax

BF.ADD key item

Parameters

  • key: The Bloom Filter key name.
  • item: The item to add to the filter.

Return value

Integer: 1 if item was newly added, 0 if item might already exist.

Example 1: Track processed job applications
import redis
r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Track processed job applications to prevent duplicate processing
job_app_id = "app_12345_user_678"
is_new = r.execute_command("BF.ADD", "processed_applications", job_app_id)
print(f"Application {job_app_id} newly processed: {bool(is_new)}")
Application app_12345_user_678 newly processed: True
Example 2: Prevent duplicate user actions
# Track user "like" actions to prevent duplicates
user_id = 123
job_id = 456
like_key = f"user_{user_id}_job_{job_id}"

# Add to "user_likes" bloom filter
is_new_like = r.execute_command("BF.ADD", "user_likes", like_key)
if is_new_like:
    print("Like registered successfully")
    # Proceed with like logic (increment counter, notifications, etc.)
else:
    print("User may have already liked this job")
Like registered successfully
Example 3: Track seen job recommendations
# Track which jobs a user has already seen
user_id = 789
job_id = 101
seen_key = f"user_{user_id}_seen_job_{job_id}"

is_newly_seen = r.execute_command("BF.ADD", "job_recommendations_seen", seen_key)
print(f"Job {job_id} is new recommendation for user {user_id}: {bool(is_newly_seen)}")
Job 101 is new recommendation for user 789: True
Example 4: Email notification deduplication
# Prevent sending duplicate notifications
user_email = "developer@example.com"
notification_type = "job_match"
today = "2025-08-24"
notif_key = f"{user_email}_{notification_type}_{today}"

should_send = r.execute_command("BF.ADD", "sent_notifications", notif_key)
if should_send:
    print("Sending notification email...")
    # Send email logic here
else:
    print("Notification already sent today")
Sending notification email...
Example 5: API rate limiting bloom filter
# Add API request to rate limit tracking
import time
client_ip = "192.168.1.100"
current_minute = int(time.time() // 60)
rate_key = f"{client_ip}_minute_{current_minute}"

is_first_request = r.execute_command("BF.ADD", "api_requests", rate_key)
print(f"First request this minute from {client_ip}: {bool(is_first_request)}")
First request this minute from 192.168.1.100: True

EXISTS

Docs: Redis BF.EXISTS Documentation

Checks whether an item exists in a Bloom Filter.

Full description

BF.EXISTS tests if an item might exist in a Bloom Filter. Returns 1 if the item might exist (possible false positive), 0 if the item definitely does NOT exist (no false negatives). Essential for Django applications implementing fast membership testing, duplicate prevention, and cache-aside patterns with probabilistic guarantees.

Syntax

BF.EXISTS key item

Parameters

  • key: The Bloom Filter key name.
  • item: The item to check for existence.

Return value

Integer: 1 if item might exist, 0 if item definitely does NOT exist.

Example 1: Check before processing job application
# Check if job application was already processed
job_app_id = "app_12345_user_678"
might_exist = r.execute_command("BF.EXISTS", "processed_applications", job_app_id)

if might_exist:
    print("Application might already be processed - check database")
    # Expensive database lookup only when necessary
else:
    print("Application definitely not processed - safe to proceed")
    # Process application directly
Application might already be processed - check database
Example 2: Username availability quick check
# Fast check before expensive database query
username = "john_developer_2025"
might_be_taken = r.execute_command("BF.EXISTS", "taken_usernames", username)

if might_be_taken:
    print("Username might be taken - checking database...")
    # Only query database when bloom filter suggests it might exist
    # is_taken = User.objects.filter(username=username).exists()
else:
    print("Username definitely available!")
    # Skip database check, username is available
Username definitely available!
Example 3: Check if user already saw job
# Recommendation engine - avoid showing seen jobs
user_id = 789
job_id = 101
seen_key = f"user_{user_id}_seen_job_{job_id}"

already_seen = r.execute_command("BF.EXISTS", "job_recommendations_seen", seen_key)
if already_seen:
    print(f"User {user_id} might have seen job {job_id} - skip recommendation")
else:
    print(f"Job {job_id} is definitely new for user {user_id} - include in recommendations")
User 789 might have seen job 101 - skip recommendation
Example 4: Duplicate email notification check
# Check before sending notification email
user_email = "developer@example.com"
notification_type = "job_match"
today = "2025-08-24"
notif_key = f"{user_email}_{notification_type}_{today}"

if already_sent:
    print("Notification might already be sent today")
else:
    print("Notification definitely not sent - safe to send")
Notification might already be sent today
Example 5: Cache miss optimization
# Use bloom filter to avoid cache misses
cache_key = "expensive_computation_result_123"
might_be_cached = r.execute_command("BF.EXISTS", "cached_computations", cache_key)

if might_be_cached:
    # Check actual cache
    result = r.get(cache_key)
    if result:
        print("Cache hit!")
    else:
        print("False positive - computing result...")
else:
    print("Definitely not cached - computing result...")
    # Skip cache check, compute directly
Cache hit!

MADD

Docs: Redis BF.MADD Documentation

Adds one or more items to a Bloom Filter. A filter will be created if it does not exist.

Full description

BF.MADD efficiently adds multiple items to a Bloom Filter in a single operation. Returns an array indicating whether each item was newly added (1) or might already exist (0). Perfect for Django batch operations like processing multiple user actions, bulk importing data, or batch tracking operations.

Syntax

BF.MADD key item [item ...]

Parameters

  • key: The Bloom Filter key name.
  • item: One or more items to add to the filter.

Return value

Array: List of integers (1 for newly added, 0 for might already exist).

Example 1: Batch process job applications
# Process multiple job applications at once
application_ids = [
    "app_12345_user_678",
    "app_12346_user_679", 
    "app_12347_user_680",
    "app_12348_user_681"
]

results = r.execute_command("BF.MADD", "processed_applications", *application_ids)
for app_id, is_new in zip(application_ids, results):
    status = "newly processed" if is_new else "might be duplicate"
    print(f"Application {app_id}: {status}")
Application app_12345_user_678: might be duplicate Application app_12346_user_679: newly processed Application app_12347_user_680: newly processed Application app_12348_user_681: newly processed
Example 2: Bulk username reservation

    "sarah_designer", 
    "mike_manager",
    "alex_analyst"
]

for username, is_available in zip(usernames, reservation_results):
    if is_available:
        print(f"Username '{username}' reserved successfully")
    else:
        print(f"Username '{username}' might already be reserved")
Username 'john_developer' reserved successfully Username 'sarah_designer' reserved successfully Username 'mike_manager' reserved successfully Username 'alex_analyst' reserved successfully
Example 3: Track multiple user interactions

RESERVE

Docs: Redis BF.RESERVE Documentation

Creates a new Bloom Filter.

Full description

BF.RESERVE creates a new Bloom Filter with specified error rate and capacity. This allows you to optimize memory usage and false positive rate for your specific Django application needs. Essential for performance-critical applications where you need predictable behavior and optimal space efficiency.

Syntax

BF.RESERVE key error_rate capacity [EXPANSION expansion] [NONSCALING]

Parameters

  • key: The Bloom Filter key name.
  • error_rate: Desired false positive rate (0 < rate < 1).
  • capacity: Expected number of items to store.
  • EXPANSION: Growth factor when capacity is exceeded (default: 2).
  • NONSCALING: Prevent automatic scaling when capacity is exceeded.

Return value

String: "OK" if the filter was created successfully.

Example 1: High-precision user tracking filter
# Create bloom filter for tracking user actions with low false positive rate
result = r.execute_command(
    "BF.RESERVE", 
    "user_actions_precise",
    0.001,  # 0.1% false positive rate
    100000  # Expected 100K user actions
)
print(f"High-precision user actions filter created: {result}")
High-precision user actions filter created: OK
Example 2: Memory-optimized email tracking
# Create compact filter for email tracking (higher error rate acceptable)
result = r.execute_command(
    "BF.RESERVE",
    "email_tracking_compact", 
    0.05,   # 5% false positive rate (more memory efficient)
    50000   # Expected 50K email interactions
)
print(f"Compact email tracking filter created: {result}")
Compact email tracking filter created: OK
Example 3: Fixed-size API rate limiting filter
# Create non-scaling filter for rate limiting (predictable memory usage)
result = r.execute_command(
    "BF.RESERVE",
    "api_rate_limit_fixed",
    0.01,   # 1% false positive rate
    10000,  # Expected 10K unique API clients
    "NONSCALING"  # Don't allow growth beyond capacity
)
print(f"Fixed-size rate limiting filter created: {result}")
Fixed-size rate limiting filter created: OK
Example 4: Scalable job recommendation filter
# Create filter that can grow as user base expands
result = r.execute_command(
    "BF.RESERVE",
    "job_recommendations_scalable",
    0.02,   # 2% false positive rate
    25000,  # Initial capacity for 25K recommendations
    "EXPANSION", 4  # Quadruple size when expanding
)
print(f"Scalable job recommendations filter created: {result}")
Scalable job recommendations filter created: OK
Example 5: Username availability filter
# Create filter optimized for username checks
result = r.execute_command(
    "BF.RESERVE",
    "username_availability",
    0.005,  # 0.5% false positive rate (very low for usernames)
    1000000 # Support up to 1M usernames
)
print(f"Username availability filter created: {result}")

# Add some example usernames
usernames = ["admin", "root", "test", "user", "guest"]
for username in usernames:
    r.execute_command("BF.ADD", "username_availability", username)
print(f"Added {len(usernames)} reserved usernames")
Username availability filter created: OK Added 5 reserved usernames

INFO

Docs: Redis BF.INFO Documentation

Returns information about a Bloom Filter.

Full description

BF.INFO provides detailed statistics about a Bloom Filter including capacity, size, number of items, number of filters, expansion rate, and more. Essential for monitoring and optimizing your Django application's Bloom Filter performance, memory usage, and false positive rates.

Syntax

BF.INFO key

Parameters

  • key: The Bloom Filter key name.

Return value

Array: Key-value pairs containing filter information and statistics.

Example 1: Monitor user actions filter performance
# Get detailed information about user actions filter
info = r.execute_command("BF.INFO", "user_actions_precise")

# Parse the info array into a dictionary
info_dict = {}
for i in range(0, len(info), 2):
    key = info[i].decode() if isinstance(info[i], bytes) else info[i]
    value = info[i + 1]
    info_dict[key] = value

print("User Actions Filter Statistics:")
print(f"Capacity: {info_dict.get('Capacity', 'N/A'):,}")
print(f"Size: {info_dict.get('Size', 'N/A'):,} bytes") 
print(f"Number of filters: {info_dict.get('Number of filters', 'N/A')}")
print(f"Number of items inserted: {info_dict.get('Number of items inserted', 'N/A'):,}")
print(f"Expansion rate: {info_dict.get('Expansion rate', 'N/A')}")
User Actions Filter Statistics: Capacity: 100,000 Size: 239,627 bytes Number of filters: 1 Number of items inserted: 4 Expansion rate: 2
Example 2: Check filter memory efficiency
# Monitor memory usage for email tracking filter
def analyze_filter_efficiency(filter_key):
    info = r.execute_command("BF.INFO", filter_key)
    info_dict = {info[i]: info[i + 1] for i in range(0, len(info), 2)}
    
    capacity = info_dict.get(b'Capacity', 0)
    size_bytes = info_dict.get(b'Size', 0)
    items_inserted = info_dict.get(b'Number of items inserted', 0)
    
    if items_inserted > 0:
        bytes_per_item = size_bytes / items_inserted
        utilization = (items_inserted / capacity) * 100 if capacity > 0 else 0
        
        print(f"Filter: {filter_key}")
        print(f"Memory efficiency: {bytes_per_item:.2f} bytes per item")
        print(f"Capacity utilization: {utilization:.1f}%")
        print(f"Total memory: {size_bytes:,} bytes")

analyze_filter_efficiency("email_tracking_compact")
Filter: email_tracking_compact Memory efficiency: 5993.17 bytes per item Capacity utilization: 8.0% Total memory: 119,863 bytes
Example 3: Monitor filter scaling behavior
# Check if filter has scaled beyond initial capacity
info = r.execute_command("BF.INFO", "job_recommendations_scalable")

num_filters = info_dict.get(b'Number of filters', 1)
capacity = info_dict.get(b'Capacity', 0)
items_inserted = info_dict.get(b'Number of items inserted', 0)

print(f"Job Recommendations Filter Status:")
print(f"Number of filter layers: {num_filters}")
print(f"Current capacity: {capacity:,}")
print(f"Items inserted: {items_inserted:,}")

if num_filters > 1:
    print("⚠️  Filter has scaled beyond initial capacity")
else:
    print("✅ Filter operating within initial capacity")
Job Recommendations Filter Status: Number of filter layers: 1 Current capacity: 25,000 Items inserted: 4 ✅ Filter operating within initial capacity
Example 4: Performance monitoring dashboard
# Create monitoring function for multiple filters
def monitor_bloom_filters():
    filters = [
        "user_actions_precise",
        "email_tracking_compact", 
        "api_rate_limit_fixed",
        "username_availability"
    ]
    
    print("=== Bloom Filter Dashboard ===")
    for filter_name in filters:
        try:
            info = r.execute_command("BF.INFO", filter_name)
            info_dict = {info[i]: info[i + 1] for i in range(0, len(info), 2)}
            
            capacity = info_dict.get(b'Capacity', 0)
            items = info_dict.get(b'Number of items inserted', 0)
            size_kb = info_dict.get(b'Size', 0) / 1024
            
            print(f"\n{filter_name}:")
            print(f"  Items: {items:,} / {capacity:,}")
            print(f"  Memory: {size_kb:.1f} KB")
            print(f"  Utilization: {(items/capacity*100) if capacity > 0 else 0:.1f}%")
            
        except Exception as e:
            print(f"\n{filter_name}: Error - {e}")

Example 5: Alert on filter capacity
# Alert when filter approaches capacity
def check_filter_capacity_alerts():
    filters_to_monitor = ["username_availability", "user_actions_precise"]
    
    for filter_name in filters_to_monitor:
        info = r.execute_command("BF.INFO", filter_name)
        info_dict = {info[i]: info[i + 1] for i in range(0, len(info), 2)}
        
        capacity = info_dict.get(b'Capacity', 0)
        items = info_dict.get(b'Number of items inserted', 0)
        utilization = (items / capacity * 100) if capacity > 0 else 0
        
        if utilization > 80:
            print(f"🚨 HIGH CAPACITY: {filter_name} is {utilization:.1f}% full")
        elif utilization > 60:
            print(f"⚠️  MEDIUM CAPACITY: {filter_name} is {utilization:.1f}% full")
        else:
            print(f"✅ NORMAL: {filter_name} is {utilization:.1f}% full")

check_filter_capacity_alerts()
✅ NORMAL: username_availability is 0.0% full ✅ NORMAL: user_actions_precise is 0.0% full

Bitmap Commands

Redis Bitmaps are space-efficient binary arrays that enable fast bit-level operations on large datasets. Perfect for Django applications requiring efficient tracking of binary states - like user activity patterns, feature flags, permissions, online/offline status, or analytics where you need to track millions of boolean values with minimal memory usage. Each bit represents a boolean state, making bitmaps ideal for real-time analytics and high-performance tracking systems.

🔢 Bitmap Commands Overview

BITCOUNT - Count set bits in range
BITFIELD - Multi-bit integer operations
BITFIELD_RO - Read-only bitfield operations
BITOP - Bitwise operations (AND/OR/XOR)
BITPOS - Find first set/clear bit
GETBIT - Get single bit value
SETBIT - Set single bit value
💡 Pro Tip: Bitmaps can represent up to 4 billion boolean values in just 512MB of memory, making them incredibly efficient for large-scale user tracking and analytics in Django applications.

SETBIT

Docs: Redis SETBIT Documentation

Sets or clears the bit at offset of the string value. Creates the key if it doesn't exist.

Full description

SETBIT sets or clears a specific bit in a Redis string, treating it as a bitmap. The offset parameter specifies which bit to modify (0-based). Returns the previous value of the bit. Essential for Django applications tracking user states, feature flags, permissions, or any boolean property across large user bases with minimal memory usage.

Syntax

SETBIT key offset value

Parameters

  • key: The bitmap key name.
  • offset: The bit position to set (0-based).
  • value: The bit value to set (0 or 1).

Return value

Integer: The previous bit value at the specified offset.

Example 1: Track daily user activity
import redis
from datetime import datetime, timedelta
r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Track daily active users using user ID as bit offset
user_id = 12345
today = datetime.now().strftime("%Y-%m-%d")
activity_key = f"daily_active:{today}"

# Mark user as active today
previous_value = r.setbit(activity_key, user_id, 1)
print(f"User {user_id} marked active today. Previously active: {bool(previous_value)}")

# Set expiration for 30 days
r.expire(activity_key, 30 * 24 * 3600)
User 12345 marked active today. Previously active: False
Example 2: Feature flag management
# Manage feature flags for users using bitmaps
def set_user_feature_flag(user_id, feature_name, enabled):
    flag_key = f"feature_flags:{feature_name}"
    previous = r.setbit(flag_key, user_id, 1 if enabled else 0)
    status = "enabled" if enabled else "disabled"
    was_previous = "was enabled" if previous else "was disabled"
    print(f"Feature '{feature_name}' {status} for user {user_id} ({was_previous})")
    return bool(previous)

# Enable new UI for specific users
set_user_feature_flag(100, "new_dashboard", True)
set_user_feature_flag(101, "new_dashboard", True)
set_user_feature_flag(102, "new_dashboard", False)
Feature 'new_dashboard' enabled for user 100 (was disabled) Feature 'new_dashboard' enabled for user 101 (was disabled) Feature 'new_dashboard' disabled for user 102 (was disabled)
Example 3: Job application tracking
# Track which jobs each user has applied to
def track_job_application(user_id, job_id):
    app_key = f"user_applications:{user_id}"
    already_applied = r.setbit(app_key, job_id, 1)
    
    if already_applied:
        print(f"User {user_id} had already applied to job {job_id}")
        return False  # Duplicate application
    else:
        print(f"User {user_id} successfully applied to job {job_id}")
        return True   # New application

# Track applications
track_job_application(500, 1001)
track_job_application(500, 1002)
track_job_application(500, 1001)  # Duplicate
User 500 successfully applied to job 1001 User 500 successfully applied to job 1002 User 500 had already applied to job 1001
Example 4: Weekly goal completion tracking
# Track daily goal completion for users (7 bits per user per week)
def mark_daily_goal_complete(user_id, day_of_week):
    """Mark daily goal as complete (0=Monday, 6=Sunday)"""
    week_key = f"weekly_goals:2025_week_34"
    
    # Each user gets 7 consecutive bits for the week
    bit_offset = user_id * 7 + day_of_week
    previous = r.setbit(week_key, bit_offset, 1)
    
    day_names = ["Monday", "Tuesday", "Wednesday", "Thursday", "Friday", "Saturday", "Sunday"]
    print(f"User {user_id} completed {day_names[day_of_week]} goal. Previously complete: {bool(previous)}")

# Track weekly goals
mark_daily_goal_complete(10, 0)  # Monday
mark_daily_goal_complete(10, 1)  # Tuesday  
mark_daily_goal_complete(11, 0)  # Different user, Monday
User 10 completed Monday goal. Previously complete: False User 10 completed Tuesday goal. Previously complete: False User 11 completed Monday goal. Previously complete: False
Example 5: Permission system with bitmaps
# Use bitmaps for granular permission management
PERMISSIONS = {
    'read_jobs': 0,
    'create_jobs': 1, 
    'edit_jobs': 2,
    'delete_jobs': 3,
    'manage_users': 4,
    'view_analytics': 5
}

def grant_permission(user_id, permission):
    if permission not in PERMISSIONS:
        print(f"Unknown permission: {permission}")
        return
    
    perm_key = f"user_permissions:{user_id}"
    bit_offset = PERMISSIONS[permission]
    previous = r.setbit(perm_key, bit_offset, 1)
    
    print(f"Granted '{permission}' to user {user_id}. Previously had: {bool(previous)}")

# Grant permissions
grant_permission(200, 'read_jobs')
grant_permission(200, 'create_jobs')
grant_permission(200, 'view_analytics')
Granted 'read_jobs' to user 200. Previously had: False Granted 'create_jobs' to user 200. Previously had: False Granted 'view_analytics' to user 200. Previously had: False

GETBIT

Docs: Redis GETBIT Documentation

Returns a bit value by offset.

Full description

GETBIT retrieves the value of a specific bit in a Redis bitmap at the given offset. Returns 0 if the bit is not set or if the key doesn't exist. Perfect for Django applications checking user states, feature flags, permissions, or any boolean property efficiently without loading entire datasets into memory.

Syntax

GETBIT key offset

Parameters

  • key: The bitmap key name.
  • offset: The bit position to retrieve (0-based).

Return value

Integer: The bit value at the specified offset (0 or 1).

Example 1: Check daily user activity
# Check if user was active today
user_id = 12345
today = datetime.now().strftime("%Y-%m-%d")
activity_key = f"daily_active:{today}"

is_active_today = r.getbit(activity_key, user_id)
print(f"User {user_id} active today: {bool(is_active_today)}")

# Check multiple days
yesterday = (datetime.now() - timedelta(days=1)).strftime("%Y-%m-%d")
was_active_yesterday = r.getbit(f"daily_active:{yesterday}", user_id)
print(f"User {user_id} active yesterday: {bool(was_active_yesterday)}")
User 12345 active today: True User 12345 active yesterday: False
Example 2: Feature flag checking
# Check if user has specific feature enabled
def user_has_feature(user_id, feature_name):
    flag_key = f"feature_flags:{feature_name}"
    has_feature = bool(r.getbit(flag_key, user_id))
    return has_feature

# Check feature access for different users
users_to_check = [100, 101, 102, 999]  # 999 is a new user
for user_id in users_to_check:
    has_dashboard = user_has_feature(user_id, "new_dashboard")
    print(f"User {user_id} has new dashboard: {has_dashboard}")

Example 4: Weekly goal progress check
# Check daily goal completion for a user
def check_weekly_progress(user_id):
    week_key = f"weekly_goals:2025_week_34"
    day_names = ["Mon", "Tue", "Wed", "Thu", "Fri", "Sat", "Sun"]
    completed_days = []
    
    for day in range(7):
        bit_offset = user_id * 7 + day
        completed = bool(r.getbit(week_key, bit_offset))
        status = "✅" if completed else "❌"
        completed_days.append(completed)
        print(f"  {day_names[day]}: {status}")
    
    total_completed = sum(completed_days)
    print(f"Progress: {total_completed}/7 days completed")

print("Weekly goal progress for user 10:")

BITCOUNT

Docs: Redis BITCOUNT Documentation

Counts the number of set bits (population counting) in a string.

Full description

BITCOUNT counts the number of set bits (1s) in a Redis bitmap, optionally within a specific byte range. This is extremely efficient for analytics queries like counting active users, feature adoption rates, goal completion statistics, or any aggregate boolean metrics in Django applications without scanning entire datasets.

Syntax

BITCOUNT key [start end] [BYTE|BIT]

Parameters

  • key: The bitmap key name.
  • start: Starting position for counting (optional).
  • end: Ending position for counting (optional).
  • BYTE|BIT: Unit of start/end positions (Redis 7.0+).

Return value

Integer: Number of set bits in the specified range.

Example 1: Count daily active users
# Count total active users for today
today = datetime.now().strftime("%Y-%m-%d")
activity_key = f"daily_active:{today}"

active_users_today = r.bitcount(activity_key)
print(f"Daily active users today ({today}): {active_users_today:,}")

# Compare with yesterday
yesterday = (datetime.now() - timedelta(days=1)).strftime("%Y-%m-%d")
active_users_yesterday = r.bitcount(f"daily_active:{yesterday}")
print(f"Daily active users yesterday: {active_users_yesterday:,}")

# Calculate growth
if active_users_yesterday > 0:
    growth = ((active_users_today - active_users_yesterday) / active_users_yesterday) * 100
    print(f"Growth: {growth:+.1f}%")
Daily active users today (2025-08-24): 1 Daily active users yesterday: 0 Growth: +100.0%
Example 2: Feature adoption analytics

def analyze_feature_adoption():

        # In practice, you might track this separately
        total_users = 1000  # Placeholder

        print(f"{feature}:")
        print(f"  Users: {users_with_feature:,}")
        print(f"  Adoption Rate: {adoption_rate:.1f}%")
        print()

Example 4: Permission distribution analysis
# Analyze permission distribution across users
def analyze_permissions():
    permission_stats = {}
    
    for perm_name, bit_offset in PERMISSIONS.items():
        # Count users with each permission
        # This is simplified - in practice you'd query across user permission keys
        sample_users = [200, 201, 202]  # Sample user IDs
        users_with_perm = 0
        
        for user_id in sample_users:
            perm_key = f"user_permissions:{user_id}"
            if r.getbit(perm_key, bit_offset):
                users_with_perm += 1
        
        permission_stats[perm_name] = users_with_perm
    
    print("Permission Distribution:")
    print("-" * 30)
    for perm, count in permission_stats.items():
        print(f"{perm}: {count} users")

Example 5: Application rate analytics
# Calculate job application rates
def application_analytics():
    # Sample user IDs to analyze
    sample_users = [500, 501, 502, 503, 504]
    
    total_applications = 0
    active_applicants = 0
    
    for user_id in sample_users:
        app_key = f"user_applications:{user_id}"
        user_applications = r.bitcount(app_key)
        
        if user_applications > 0:
            active_applicants += 1
            total_applications += user_applications
    
    print("Application Analytics:")
    print(f"Active applicants: {active_applicants}/{len(sample_users)} users")
    print(f"Total applications: {total_applications}")
    
    if active_applicants > 0:
        avg_applications = total_applications / active_applicants
        print(f"Average applications per active user: {avg_applications:.1f}")

application_analytics()
Application Analytics: Active applicants: 1/5 users Total applications: 2 Average applications per active user: 2.0

BITOP

Docs: Redis BITOP Documentation

Performs bitwise operations on multiple strings, and stores the result.

Full description

BITOP performs bitwise operations (AND, OR, XOR, NOT) between multiple bitmaps and stores the result in a destination key. Perfect for Django applications requiring complex analytics like finding users active on both weekdays AND weekends, combining feature flags, calculating user segments, or performing set operations on large boolean datasets efficiently.

Syntax

BITOP operation destkey key [key ...]

Parameters

  • operation: The bitwise operation (AND, OR, XOR, NOT).
  • destkey: The destination key to store the result.
  • key: One or more source bitmap keys.

Return value

Integer: The size of the longest input string in bytes.

Example 1: Find users active on both weekdays and weekends
# Set up sample data for weekday and weekend activity
weekday_key = "active_weekdays:2025_week_34"
weekend_key = "active_weekends:2025_week_34"

# Mark some users as active on weekdays
for user_id in [100, 101, 102, 105]:
    r.setbit(weekday_key, user_id, 1)

# Mark some users as active on weekends  
for user_id in [101, 102, 103, 104]:
    r.setbit(weekend_key, user_id, 1)

# Find users active on BOTH weekdays AND weekends
result_key = "active_both:2025_week_34"
result_size = r.bitop("AND", result_key, weekday_key, weekend_key)

# Count users active on both
both_active = r.bitcount(result_key)
print(f"Users active on both weekdays and weekends: {both_active}")

# Check specific users
test_users = [100, 101, 102, 103, 104, 105]
for user_id in test_users:
    is_both_active = bool(r.getbit(result_key, user_id))
    weekday_active = bool(r.getbit(weekday_key, user_id))
    weekend_active = bool(r.getbit(weekend_key, user_id))
    print(f"User {user_id}: Weekday({weekday_active}) & Weekend({weekend_active}) = Both({is_both_active})")
Users active on both weekdays and weekends: 2 User 100: Weekday(True) & Weekend(False) = Both(False) User 101: Weekday(True) & Weekend(True) = Both(True) User 102: Weekday(True) & Weekend(True) = Both(True) User 103: Weekday(False) & Weekend(True) = Both(False) User 104: Weekday(False) & Weekend(True) = Both(False) User 105: Weekday(True) & Weekend(False) = Both(False)
Example 2: Combine feature flags for advanced users
# Create advanced user segment with multiple feature requirements
beta_key = "feature_flags:beta_access"
premium_key = "feature_flags:premium_features"
analytics_key = "feature_flags:advanced_analytics"

premium_users = [201, 202, 204, 205]

    r.setbit(beta_key, user_id, 1)
for user_id in premium_users:
    r.setbit(premium_key, user_id, 1)
for user_id in analytics_users:
    r.setbit(analytics_key, user_id, 1)

# Find users with ALL advanced features (beta AND premium AND analytics)
advanced_users_key = "segment_advanced_users"
temp_key = "temp_beta_premium"

# First combine beta and premium
r.bitop("AND", temp_key, beta_key, premium_key)
# Then combine with analytics
r.bitop("AND", advanced_users_key, temp_key, analytics_key)

advanced_count = r.bitcount(advanced_users_key)
print(f"Users with all advanced features: {advanced_count}")

# Check which users qualify
test_users = [200, 201, 202, 203, 204, 205, 206]

    has_beta = bool(r.getbit(beta_key, user_id))
    has_premium = bool(r.getbit(premium_key, user_id))
    has_analytics = bool(r.getbit(analytics_key, user_id))
    is_advanced = bool(r.getbit(advanced_users_key, user_id))
    print(f"User {user_id}: Beta({has_beta}) & Premium({has_premium}) & Analytics({has_analytics}) = Advanced({is_advanced})")

# Cleanup temp key
r.delete(temp_key)

Example 3: User engagement segmentation
# Create user segments based on activity patterns
daily_active = "daily_active:2025-08-24"
job_applicants = "user_segment:applicants"
job_viewers = "user_segment:viewers"

# Set up sample data
for user_id in [300, 301, 302, 303]:
    r.setbit(daily_active, user_id, 1)
for user_id in [301, 302, 304, 305]:
    r.setbit(job_applicants, user_id, 1)
for user_id in [300, 302, 303, 305, 306]:
    r.setbit(job_viewers, user_id, 1)

# Find active users who view jobs OR apply to jobs
engaged_users = "segment_engaged_active"
r.bitop("OR", "temp_viewers_or_applicants", job_viewers, job_applicants)
r.bitop("AND", engaged_users, daily_active, "temp_viewers_or_applicants")

# Find active users who ONLY view but don't apply
viewers_only = "segment_viewers_only"
r.bitop("AND", "temp_active_viewers", daily_active, job_viewers)
r.bitop("NOT", "temp_not_applicants", job_applicants)
r.bitop("AND", viewers_only, "temp_active_viewers", "temp_not_applicants")

print(f"Engaged active users (view OR apply): {r.bitcount(engaged_users)}")
print(f"Viewers only (no applications): {r.bitcount(viewers_only)}")

# Analyze user segments
test_users = [300, 301, 302, 303, 304, 305, 306]
for user_id in test_users:
    active = bool(r.getbit(daily_active, user_id))
    views = bool(r.getbit(job_viewers, user_id))
    applies = bool(r.getbit(job_applicants, user_id))
    engaged = bool(r.getbit(engaged_users, user_id))
    viewer_only = bool(r.getbit(viewers_only, user_id))
    
    print(f"User {user_id}: Active({active}) Views({views}) Applies({applies}) -> Engaged({engaged}) ViewerOnly({viewer_only})")

# Cleanup temp keys
for key in ["temp_viewers_or_applicants", "temp_active_viewers", "temp_not_applicants"]:
    r.delete(key)

Example 4: A/B testing group analysis
# Analyze A/B test groups and conversion rates
test_group_a = "ab_test:new_ui:group_a"
test_group_b = "ab_test:new_ui:group_b"
conversions = "ab_test:conversions"

# Set up A/B test groups
group_a_users = [400, 401, 402, 403, 404]
group_b_users = [405, 406, 407, 408, 409]
converted_users = [401, 403, 406, 408]

for user_id in group_a_users:
    r.setbit(test_group_a, user_id, 1)
for user_id in group_b_users:
    r.setbit(test_group_b, user_id, 1)
for user_id in converted_users:
    r.setbit(conversions, user_id, 1)

# Calculate conversions for each group
group_a_conversions = "ab_test:group_a_conversions"
group_b_conversions = "ab_test:group_b_conversions"

r.bitop("AND", group_a_conversions, test_group_a, conversions)
r.bitop("AND", group_b_conversions, test_group_b, conversions)

# Calculate conversion rates
total_group_a = r.bitcount(test_group_a)
total_group_b = r.bitcount(test_group_b)
converted_group_a = r.bitcount(group_a_conversions)
converted_group_b = r.bitcount(group_b_conversions)

rate_a = (converted_group_a / total_group_a * 100) if total_group_a > 0 else 0
rate_b = (converted_group_b / total_group_b * 100) if total_group_b > 0 else 0

print("A/B Test Results:")
print(f"Group A: {converted_group_a}/{total_group_a} conversions ({rate_a:.1f}%)")
print(f"Group B: {converted_group_b}/{total_group_b} conversions ({rate_b:.1f}%)")
print(f"Winner: Group {'B' if rate_b > rate_a else 'A'} (+ {abs(rate_b - rate_a):.1f}pp)")
A/B Test Results: Group A: 2/5 conversions (40.0%) Group B: 2/5 conversions (40.0%) Winner: A (+ 0.0pp)
Example 5: User churn prediction segments
# Identify at-risk users using activity patterns
recent_login = "activity:recent_login"
job_activity = "activity:job_engagement"
profile_updates = "activity:profile_updates"

# Set up activity data

profile_active_users = [502, 503, 505, 506]

    r.setbit(recent_login, user_id, 1)

temp3 = "temp_not_job_or_profile"

    risk = bool(r.getbit(at_risk, user_id))
    
    print(f"User {user_id}: Login({login}) Job({job}) Profile({profile}) -> Engaged({engaged}) AtRisk({risk})")

# Cleanup
for key in [temp1, temp2, temp3]:
    r.delete(key)

BITPOS

Docs: Redis BITPOS Documentation

Finds the first set (1) or clear (0) bit in a string.

Full description

BITPOS finds the position of the first bit set to 1 or 0 in a Redis bitmap, optionally within a specific range. Perfect for Django applications that need to find the first available slot, the first inactive user, the next user ID to assign, or efficiently locate gaps in sequential data without scanning entire datasets.

Syntax

BITPOS key bit [start [end]] [BYTE|BIT]

Parameters

  • key: The bitmap key name.
  • bit: The bit value to find (0 or 1).
  • start: Starting position for search (optional).
  • end: Ending position for search (optional).
  • BYTE|BIT: Unit of start/end positions (Redis 7.0+).

Return value

Integer: Position of the first bit, or -1 if not found.

Example 1: Find next available user ID
# Find the first available user ID in registration system
user_ids_taken = "registered_user_ids"

# Simulate some registered user IDs
taken_ids = [0, 1, 2, 5, 7, 10, 15]
for user_id in taken_ids:
    r.setbit(user_ids_taken, user_id, 1)

# Find first available user ID
next_available_id = r.bitpos(user_ids_taken, 0)
print(f"Next available user ID: {next_available_id}")

# Find first available ID starting from a specific position
next_id_from_20 = r.bitpos(user_ids_taken, 0, 20)
print(f"Next available ID from position 20: {next_id_from_20}")

# Register a new user with the available ID
if next_available_id >= 0:
    r.setbit(user_ids_taken, next_available_id, 1)
    print(f"Registered new user with ID: {next_available_id}")
Next available user ID: 3 Next available ID from position 20: 20 Registered new user with ID: 3
Example 2: Find first inactive day in user activity
# Track user activity over 30 days and find first inactive day
user_id = 12345
activity_key = f"user_activity_30days:{user_id}"

# Simulate user activity (active on some days)
active_days = [0, 1, 2, 4, 5, 7, 8, 10, 12, 15, 20, 25]
for day in active_days:
    r.setbit(activity_key, day, 1)

# Find first inactive day
first_inactive_day = r.bitpos(activity_key, 0)
print(f"User {user_id} first inactive day: Day {first_inactive_day}")

# Find inactive days in specific week (days 7-13)
first_inactive_week2 = r.bitpos(activity_key, 0, 7, 13)
print(f"First inactive day in week 2 (days 7-13): Day {first_inactive_week2}")

# Check activity pattern
print(f"Activity pattern for first 15 days:")
for day in range(15):
    active = bool(r.getbit(activity_key, day))
    status = "✅" if active else "❌"
    print(f"Day {day:2d}: {status}", end="  ")
    if (day + 1) % 5 == 0:
        print()  # New line every 5 days

Example 4: Find first available appointment slot
# Find available appointment slots in a schedule
schedule_key = "appointment_slots_2025_08_24"

# Mark some slots as booked (slot IDs represent time slots)
booked_slots = [0, 1, 2, 5, 8, 9, 12, 15]  # 9 AM, 9:30 AM, 10 AM, etc.
for slot in booked_slots:
    r.setbit(schedule_key, slot, 1)

# Find first available slot
first_available = r.bitpos(schedule_key, 0)

# Convert slot ID to time
def slot_to_time(slot_id):
    start_hour = 9  # 9 AM start
    hours = start_hour + (slot_id // 2)
    minutes = 30 if slot_id % 2 else 0
    return f"{hours:02d}:{minutes:02d}"

print(f"First available appointment slot: #{first_available} ({slot_to_time(first_available)})")

# Find available slot in afternoon (after 1 PM = slot 8)
afternoon_available = r.bitpos(schedule_key, 0, 8)
if afternoon_available >= 8:
    print(f"First afternoon slot: #{afternoon_available} ({slot_to_time(afternoon_available)})")

# Show schedule for the day
print(f"\nSchedule for 2025-08-24:")
for slot in range(16):  # 9 AM to 5 PM (16 slots)
    booked = bool(r.getbit(schedule_key, slot))
    status = "BOOKED" if booked else "Available"
    print(f"{slot_to_time(slot)}: {status}")

    print(f"User {user_id}: {status}")

if first_without_permission >= 0:
    r.setbit(permission_key, first_without_permission, 1)

Cuckoo Filter Commands

Redis Cuckoo Filters are space-efficient probabilistic data structures that support both membership testing and deletion operations. Unlike Bloom Filters, Cuckoo Filters allow item removal while maintaining excellent performance and low false positive rates. Perfect for Django applications requiring dynamic sets with add/remove operations - like active user sessions, temporary access tokens, recent activity tracking, or any scenario where you need both fast lookups and the ability to clean up expired items.

🥒 Cuckoo Filter Commands Overview

ADD - Add single item to filter
ADDNX - Add item if not exists
COUNT - Count item occurrences
DEL - Delete item from filter
EXISTS - Check item existence
INFO - Get filter information
INSERT - Batch insert items
INSERTNX - Batch insert if not exists
LOADCHUNK - Restore filter chunk
MEXISTS - Check multiple items
RESERVE - Create new filter
SCANDUMP - Dump filter for backup
🔄 Key Advantage: Unlike Bloom Filters, Cuckoo Filters support deletion operations, making them ideal for dynamic datasets where items need to be removed (like session management, token expiration, or cache invalidation).

RESERVE

Docs: Redis CF.RESERVE Documentation

Creates a new Cuckoo Filter.

Full description

CF.RESERVE creates a new Cuckoo Filter with specified capacity and optional bucket size and maximum iterations. This allows you to optimize the filter for your specific Django application's performance requirements and memory constraints. Essential for applications that need predictable behavior and the ability to delete items from the filter.

Syntax

CF.RESERVE key capacity [BUCKETSIZE bucketsize] [MAXITERATIONS maxiterations] [EXPANSION expansion]

Parameters

  • key: The Cuckoo Filter key name.
  • capacity: Expected number of items to store.
  • BUCKETSIZE: Number of items per bucket (default: 2).
  • MAXITERATIONS: Maximum insertion attempts (default: 500).
  • EXPANSION: Growth factor when capacity is exceeded (default: 1).

Return value

String: "OK" if the filter was created successfully.

Example 1: Session management filter
import redis
r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Create filter for tracking active user sessions
result = r.execute_command(
    "CF.RESERVE", 
    "active_sessions",
    10000,  # Expected 10K concurrent sessions
    "BUCKETSIZE", 4,  # Higher bucket size for better performance
    "MAXITERATIONS", 1000  # More iterations for better insertion success
)
print(f"Session tracking filter created: {result}")
Session tracking filter created: OK
Example 2: API rate limiting with token expiration
# Create filter for API rate limiting tokens
result = r.execute_command(
    "CF.RESERVE",
    "api_rate_tokens",
    50000,  # Support 50K rate limit tokens
    "BUCKETSIZE", 2,  # Standard bucket size
    "EXPANSION", 2  # Double capacity when needed
)
print(f"API rate limiting filter created: {result}")
API rate limiting filter created: OK
Example 3: Recent activity tracking
# Filter for tracking recent user activities (with cleanup)
result = r.execute_command(
    "CF.RESERVE",
    "recent_activities",
    25000,  # Track 25K recent activities
    "BUCKETSIZE", 3,  # Medium bucket size
    "MAXITERATIONS", 800
)
print(f"Recent activity filter created: {result}")
Recent activity filter created: OK
Example 4: Job application deduplication
# Filter for preventing duplicate job applications
result = r.execute_command(
    "CF.RESERVE",
    "job_applications_dedup",
    100000,  # Handle 100K applications
    "BUCKETSIZE", 4,  # Higher performance
    "EXPANSION", 1  # No auto-expansion for predictable memory
)
print(f"Job application dedup filter created: {result}")

# Set expiration for the filter (24 hours)
r.expire("job_applications_dedup", 24 * 3600)
Job application dedup filter created: OK
Example 5: Cache invalidation tracking
# Filter for tracking cache keys that need invalidation
result = r.execute_command(
    "CF.RESERVE",
    "cache_invalidation_queue",
    15000,  # Track 15K cache invalidations
    "BUCKETSIZE", 2,
    "MAXITERATIONS", 600
)
print(f"Cache invalidation filter created: {result}")

# Create multiple filters for different cache types
cache_types = ["user_profiles", "job_listings", "search_results"]
for cache_type in cache_types:
    filter_key = f"invalidation_{cache_type}"
    result = r.execute_command("CF.RESERVE", filter_key, 5000)
    print(f"Cache invalidation filter for {cache_type}: {result}")
Cache invalidation filter created: OK Cache invalidation filter for user_profiles: OK Cache invalidation filter for job_listings: OK Cache invalidation filter for search_results: OK

ADD

Docs: Redis CF.ADD Documentation

Adds an item to a Cuckoo Filter.

Full description

CF.ADD adds a single item to an existing Cuckoo Filter. If the filter doesn't exist, it creates one with default parameters. Returns 1 if the item was successfully added. Perfect for Django applications tracking dynamic sets like active sessions, recent operations, temporary tokens, or any scenario where you need efficient membership testing with the ability to remove items later.

Syntax

CF.ADD key item

Parameters

  • key: The Cuckoo Filter key name.
  • item: The item to add to the filter.

Return value

Integer: 1 if item was successfully added.

Example 1: Track active user sessions
# Add user session to active sessions filter
import uuid
user_id = 12345
session_token = str(uuid.uuid4())
session_key = f"session_{user_id}_{session_token[:8]}"

added = r.execute_command("CF.ADD", "active_sessions", session_key)
print(f"Session {session_key} added to active sessions: {bool(added)}")

# Add multiple sessions for different users
session_data = [
    ("user_100", "sess_abc123"),
    ("user_101", "sess_def456"), 
    ("user_102", "sess_ghi789")
]

for user, session in session_data:
    added = r.execute_command("CF.ADD", "active_sessions", f"{user}_{session}")
    print(f"Session {user}_{session} added: {bool(added)}")
Session session_12345_4f8c2a1b added to active sessions: True Session user_100_sess_abc123 added: True Session user_101_sess_def456 added: True Session user_102_sess_ghi789 added: True
Example 2: API rate limiting token tracking
# Add API request tokens for rate limiting
import time
client_ip = "192.168.1.100"
current_minute = int(time.time() // 60)
request_token = f"{client_ip}_{current_minute}_req_1"

added = r.execute_command("CF.ADD", "api_rate_tokens", request_token)
print(f"API request token added: {bool(added)}")

# Simulate multiple requests from same IP
for req_num in range(2, 6):
    token = f"{client_ip}_{current_minute}_req_{req_num}"
    added = r.execute_command("CF.ADD", "api_rate_tokens", token)
    print(f"Request {req_num} token added: {bool(added)}")
API request token added: True Request 2 token added: True Request 3 token added: True Request 4 token added: True Request 5 token added: True

Example 4: Job application deduplication
# Prevent duplicate job applications
def submit_job_application(user_id, job_id):
    app_key = f"user_{user_id}_job_{job_id}"
    
    # Add to deduplication filter
    added = r.execute_command("CF.ADD", "job_applications_dedup", app_key)
    
    if added:
        print(f"✅ Application submitted: User {user_id} -> Job {job_id}")
        # Process the application here
        return True
    else:
        print(f"❌ Duplicate application detected: User {user_id} -> Job {job_id}")
        return False

    (600, 2001),
    (601, 2002),

    (602, 2003)
]

for user_id, job_id in applications:

Example 5: Cache invalidation queue
# Track cache keys that need invalidation
def queue_cache_invalidation(cache_type, cache_key):
    invalidation_key = f"{cache_type}:{cache_key}"
    added = r.execute_command("CF.ADD", "cache_invalidation_queue", invalidation_key)
    
    if added:
        print(f"🔄 Queued for invalidation: {invalidation_key}")
        return True
    else:
        print(f"⚠️ Already queued: {invalidation_key}")
        return False

# Queue various cache invalidations
cache_items = [
    ("user_profile", "user_123"),
    ("job_listing", "job_456"),
    ("search_results", "python_developer_nyc"),
    ("user_profile", "user_123"),  # Duplicate
    ("job_listing", "job_789")
]

for cache_type, key in cache_items:
    queue_cache_invalidation(cache_type, key)
🔄 Queued for invalidation: user_profile:user_123 🔄 Queued for invalidation: job_listing:job_456 🔄 Queued for invalidation: search_results:python_developer_nyc 🔄 Queued for invalidation: user_profile:user_123 🔄 Queued for invalidation: job_listing:job_789

EXISTS

Docs: Redis CF.EXISTS Documentation

Checks whether one or more items exist in a Cuckoo Filter.

Full description

CF.EXISTS tests if an item exists in a Cuckoo Filter. Returns 1 if the item exists, 0 if it doesn't. Like Bloom Filters, Cuckoo Filters can have false positives but no false negatives. Perfect for Django applications checking session validity, API rate limits, recent activity, or any fast membership testing where deletions are also needed.

Syntax

CF.EXISTS key item

Parameters

  • key: The Cuckoo Filter key name.
  • item: The item to check for existence.

Return value

Integer: 1 if item exists, 0 if item doesn't exist.

Example 1: Validate active sessions
# Check if user session is active
def is_session_active(user_id, session_token):
    session_key = f"session_{user_id}_{session_token[:8]}"
    exists = r.execute_command("CF.EXISTS", "active_sessions", session_key)
    return bool(exists)

# Check various session tokens
session_token = "4f8c2a1b-1234-5678-9abc-def012345678"
active = is_session_active(12345, session_token)
print(f"Session for user 12345 is active: {active}")

# Check multiple sessions
sessions_to_check = [
    ("user_100", "sess_abc123"),
    ("user_101", "sess_def456"),
    ("user_999", "sess_invalid")  # This one doesn't exist
]

for user, session in sessions_to_check:
    exists = r.execute_command("CF.EXISTS", "active_sessions", f"{user}_{session}")
    status = "✅ Active" if exists else "❌ Invalid"
    print(f"Session {user}_{session}: {status}")
Session for user 12345 is active: True Session user_100_sess_abc123: ✅ Active Session user_101_sess_def456: ✅ Active Session user_999_sess_invalid: ❌ Invalid
Example 2: API rate limit checking
# Check if API request is within rate limits
def check_rate_limit(client_ip, max_requests=10):
    current_minute = int(time.time() // 60)
    
    # Count existing requests in current minute
    request_count = 0
    for req_num in range(1, max_requests + 1):
        token = f"{client_ip}_{current_minute}_req_{req_num}"
        exists = r.execute_command("CF.EXISTS", "api_rate_tokens", token)
        if exists:
            request_count += 1
    
    is_allowed = request_count < max_requests
    return is_allowed, request_count

# Check rate limits for different IPs
test_ips = ["192.168.1.100", "192.168.1.200", "10.0.0.50"]

for ip in test_ips:
    allowed, count = check_rate_limit(ip, max_requests=10)
    status = "✅ Allowed" if allowed else "❌ Rate Limited"
    print(f"IP {ip}: {status} ({count}/10 requests)")
IP 192.168.1.100: ✅ Allowed (5/10 requests) IP 192.168.1.200: ✅ Allowed (0/10 requests) IP 10.0.0.50: ✅ Allowed (0/10 requests)
Example 3: Check recent activity
# Check if user performed specific activity recently
def has_recent_activity(user_id, activity_type, resource_id, time_window=300):
    """Check if user has activity within time window (default 5 minutes)"""
    current_time = int(time.time())
    
    # Check within time window
    for seconds_ago in range(0, time_window, 30):  # Check every 30 seconds
        check_time = current_time - seconds_ago
        activity_key = f"user_{user_id}_{activity_type}_{resource_id}_{check_time}"
        
        exists = r.execute_command("CF.EXISTS", "recent_activities", activity_key)
        if exists:
            return True, seconds_ago
    
    return False, -1

# Check recent activities
activity_checks = [
    (500, "view_job", 1001),
    (501, "apply_job", 1001),
    (999, "view_job", 9999)  # Non-existent
]

for user_id, activity, resource_id in activity_checks:
    has_activity, seconds_ago = has_recent_activity(user_id, activity, resource_id)
    if has_activity:
        print(f"✅ User {user_id} {activity} on {resource_id} (~{seconds_ago}s ago)")
    else:
        print(f"❌ No recent {activity} activity for user {user_id} on {resource_id}")
✅ User 500 view_job on 1001 (~0s ago) ✅ User 501 apply_job on 1001 (~0s ago) ❌ No recent view_job activity for user 999 on 9999
Example 4: Job application status check
# Check if user already applied to job

# Check application status before allowing new applications

# Check if cache key is queued for invalidation
def is_queued_for_invalidation(cache_type, cache_key):
    invalidation_key = f"{cache_type}:{cache_key}"
    exists = r.execute_command("CF.EXISTS", "cache_invalidation_queue", invalidation_key)

# Check cache invalidation status

DEL

Docs: Redis CF.DEL Documentation

Deletes an item from a Cuckoo Filter.

Full description

CF.DEL removes an item from a Cuckoo Filter. Returns 1 if the item was successfully deleted, 0 if the item wasn't found. This is the key advantage of Cuckoo Filters over Bloom Filters - the ability to remove items. Perfect for Django applications managing sessions, tokens, temporary data, or any dynamic set where items need to be removed when they expire or are no longer relevant.

Syntax

CF.DEL key item

Parameters

  • key: The Cuckoo Filter key name.
  • item: The item to delete from the filter.

Return value

Integer: 1 if item was successfully deleted, 0 if item wasn't found.

Example 1: Session logout/expiration
# Remove user session on logout or expiration
def logout_user(user_id, session_token):
    session_key = f"session_{user_id}_{session_token[:8]}"
    
    # Remove from active sessions
    deleted = r.execute_command("CF.DEL", "active_sessions", session_key)
    
    if deleted:
        print(f"✅ Session {session_key} successfully logged out")
        return True
    else:
        print(f"⚠️ Session {session_key} was not active")
        return False

# Test session logout
session_token = "4f8c2a1b-1234-5678-9abc-def012345678"
logout_user(12345, session_token)

# Logout multiple sessions
sessions_to_logout = [
    ("user_100", "sess_abc123"),
    ("user_101", "sess_def456"),
    ("user_999", "sess_invalid")  # This one doesn't exist
]

for user, session in sessions_to_logout:
    session_key = f"{user}_{session}"
    deleted = r.execute_command("CF.DEL", "active_sessions", session_key)
    status = "✅ Logged out" if deleted else "⚠️ Not found"
    print(f"Session {session_key}: {status}")
✅ Session session_12345_4f8c2a1b successfully logged out Session user_100_sess_abc123: ✅ Logged out Session user_101_sess_def456: ✅ Logged out Session user_999_sess_invalid: ⚠️ Not found
Example 2: API rate limit token cleanup
# Clean up expired API rate limit tokens
def cleanup_expired_tokens(client_ip, minutes_to_keep=5):
    current_minute = int(time.time() // 60)
    deleted_count = 0
    
    # Remove tokens older than specified minutes
    for minutes_ago in range(minutes_to_keep, minutes_to_keep + 10):
        old_minute = current_minute - minutes_ago
        for req_num in range(1, 11):  # Assume max 10 requests per minute
            old_token = f"{client_ip}_{old_minute}_req_{req_num}"
            deleted = r.execute_command("CF.DEL", "api_rate_tokens", old_token)
            if deleted:
                deleted_count += 1
    
    print(f"Cleaned up {deleted_count} expired tokens for IP {client_ip}")
    return deleted_count

# Cleanup expired tokens
cleanup_expired_tokens("192.168.1.100")
cleanup_expired_tokens("192.168.1.200")
Cleaned up 0 expired tokens for IP 192.168.1.100 Cleaned up 0 expired tokens for IP 192.168.1.200
Example 3: Process cache invalidation queue
# Process and remove items from cache invalidation queue
def process_cache_invalidations():
    # Sample cache keys that were queued for invalidation
    queued_items = [
        "user_profile:user_123",
        "job_listing:job_456", 
        "search_results:python_developer_nyc",
        "job_listing:job_789"
    ]
    
    processed_count = 0
    
    for item in queued_items:
        # Check if item is in queue
        exists = r.execute_command("CF.EXISTS", "cache_invalidation_queue", item)
        
        if exists:
            # Process the invalidation (simulate cache clearing)
            print(f"🔄 Invalidating cache: {item}")
            
            # Remove from queue after processing
            deleted = r.execute_command("CF.DEL", "cache_invalidation_queue", item)
            if deleted:
                processed_count += 1
                print(f"✅ Removed {item} from invalidation queue")
            else:
                print(f"⚠️ Failed to remove {item} from queue")
    
    print(f"Processed {processed_count} cache invalidations")
    return processed_count

process_cache_invalidations()

Example 4: Remove completed activities
# Clean up old activities from recent activity tracking
def cleanup_old_activities(max_age_seconds=3600):  # 1 hour
    current_time = int(time.time())
    cutoff_time = current_time - max_age_seconds
    
    # This is simplified - in practice you'd track timestamps separately
    # or use a more sophisticated cleanup mechanism
    
    sample_activities = [
        f"user_500_view_job_1001_{current_time - 3600}",  # 1 hour old
        f"user_500_save_job_1002_{current_time - 1800}",  # 30 minutes old
        f"user_501_apply_job_1001_{current_time - 300}",  # 5 minutes old
    ]
    
    cleaned_count = 0
    
    for activity in sample_activities:
        # Extract timestamp from activity key
        parts = activity.split('_')
        activity_time = int(parts[-1])
        
        if activity_time < cutoff_time:
            deleted = r.execute_command("CF.DEL", "recent_activities", activity)
            if deleted:
                cleaned_count += 1
                print(f"🧹 Cleaned old activity: {activity}")
            else:
                print(f"⚠️ Activity not found: {activity}")
        else:
            print(f"⏳ Keeping recent activity: {activity}")
    
    print(f"Cleaned up {cleaned_count} old activities")
    return cleaned_count

cleanup_old_activities()
🧹 Cleaned old activity: user_500_view_job_1001_1724553456 ⏳ Keeping recent activity: user_500_save_job_1002_1724555256 ⏳ Keeping recent activity: user_501_apply_job_1001_1724556756 Cleaned up 1 old activities
Example 5: Job application withdrawal
# Allow users to withdraw job applications
def withdraw_job_application(user_id, job_id):
    app_key = f"user_{user_id}_job_{job_id}"
    
    # Check if application exists
    exists = r.execute_command("CF.EXISTS", "job_applications_dedup", app_key)
    
    if not exists:
        print(f"❌ No application found for user {user_id} on job {job_id}")
        return False
    
    # Remove from deduplication filter
    deleted = r.execute_command("CF.DEL", "job_applications_dedup", app_key)
    
    if deleted:
        print(f"✅ Application withdrawn: User {user_id} -> Job {job_id}")
        # Here you would also remove from actual database
        return True
    else:
        print(f"⚠️ Failed to withdraw application: User {user_id} -> Job {job_id}")
        return False

# Test application withdrawal
withdrawals = [
    (600, 2001),  # Should exist
    (601, 2002),  # Should exist  
    (700, 2001),  # Doesn't exist
    (602, 2003)   # Should exist
]

for user_id, job_id in withdrawals:
    withdraw_job_application(user_id, job_id)
✅ Application withdrawn: User 600 -> Job 2001 ✅ Application withdrawn: User 601 -> Job 2002 ❌ No application found for user 700 on job 2001 ✅ Application withdrawn: User 602 -> Job 2003

COUNT

Docs: Redis CF.COUNT Documentation

Return the number of times an item might be in a Cuckoo Filter.

Full description

CF.COUNT returns the number of times an item appears in a Cuckoo Filter. This can be useful for tracking frequency or detecting if an item was added multiple times. Perfect for Django applications that need to track occurrence counts, detect duplicates, or implement frequency-based logic with the ability to remove items when needed.

Syntax

CF.COUNT key item

Parameters

  • key: The Cuckoo Filter key name.
  • item: The item to count occurrences for.

Return value

Integer: Number of times the item appears in the filter.

Example 1: Track multiple login attempts
# Track multiple login attempts for security monitoring
login_attempts_filter = "login_attempts_tracking"

# Simulate login attempts
def track_login_attempt(username, ip_address):
    attempt_key = f"{username}_from_{ip_address}"
    
    # Add the attempt
    r.execute_command("CF.ADD", login_attempts_filter, attempt_key)
    
    # Count total attempts
    count = r.execute_command("CF.COUNT", login_attempts_filter, attempt_key)
    
    print(f"Login attempt for {username} from {ip_address}: {count} total attempts")
    
    # Security check
    if count >= 3:
        print(f"⚠️ SECURITY ALERT: Multiple login attempts for {username}")
    
    return count

# Simulate multiple login attempts
attempts = [
    ("john_doe", "192.168.1.100"),
    ("john_doe", "192.168.1.100"),  # Same user, same IP
    ("jane_smith", "10.0.0.50"),
    ("john_doe", "192.168.1.100"),  # Third attempt - should trigger alert
    ("alice_brown", "172.16.0.25")
]

for username, ip in attempts:
    track_login_attempt(username, ip)
Login attempt for john_doe from 192.168.1.100: 1 total attempts Login attempt for john_doe from 192.168.1.100: 2 total attempts Login attempt for jane_smith from 10.0.0.50: 1 total attempts Login attempt for john_doe from 192.168.1.100: 3 total attempts ⚠️ SECURITY ALERT: Multiple login attempts for john_doe Login attempt for alice_brown from 172.16.0.25: 1 total attempts
Example 2: API request frequency monitoring
# Monitor API request frequency per endpoint
api_frequency_filter = "api_endpoint_frequency"

def track_api_request(client_id, endpoint):
    request_key = f"{client_id}_{endpoint.replace('/', '_')}"
    
    # Add request
    r.execute_command("CF.ADD", api_frequency_filter, request_key)
    
    # Count requests
    count = r.execute_command("CF.COUNT", api_frequency_filter, request_key)
    
    print(f"API request: {client_id} -> {endpoint} ({count} times)")
    
    # Rate limiting logic
    if count > 100:
        print(f"🚨 Rate limit exceeded for {client_id} on {endpoint}")
    elif count > 80:
        print(f"⚠️ Approaching rate limit for {client_id} on {endpoint}")
    
    return count

    ("client_123", "/api/jobs/search"),  # Third time
    ("client_789", "/api/applications/submit")
]

    track_api_request(client, endpoint)

List Commands

Redis lists are ordered collections of strings, perfect for implementing queues, stacks, activity feeds, and job processing systems. In Django applications, lists are ideal for managing task queues, user activity logs, recent items, and any ordered data. This section covers all list operations with practical examples using your JobPost models.

📝 List Commands Overview

LPUSH - Add to list start
RPUSH - Add to list end
LPOP - Remove from start
RPOP - Remove from end
LLEN - Get list length
LRANGE - Get range of elements
LINDEX - Get element by index
LSET - Set element by index
LINSERT - Insert before/after element
LREM - Remove elements
LTRIM - Trim to range
LPOS - Find element position
BLPOP - Blocking pop left
BRPOP - Blocking pop right
🚀 Perfect for Django: Lists excel at job queues, activity feeds, recent items tracking, and any scenario where order matters and you need efficient push/pop operations.

LPUSH

Docs: Redis LPUSH Documentation

Insert one or more elements at the beginning (left/head) of a list.

Full description

LPUSH prepends one or more elements to a list, making it perfect for implementing stacks, recent activity feeds, or any scenario where new items should appear first. In Django applications, this is ideal for activity logs, notification queues, or maintaining recent user actions where the most recent items need to be at the front.

Syntax

LPUSH key element [element ...]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the list.
  • element: The element(s) to prepend to the list.

Return value

Integer: The length of the list after the push operations.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: User activity feed
import redis
from datetime import datetime
r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Track user activity - most recent first
user_id = 1
activity_key = f"user:{user_id}:activity"

# User performs various actions
actions = [
    "Applied to Python Developer position",
    "Updated profile information", 
    "Saved Full Stack Developer job",
    "Viewed Data Scientist posting",
    "Logged in"
]

for action in actions:
    timestamp = datetime.now().strftime("%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S")
    activity_entry = f"{timestamp}: {action}"
    result = r.lpush(activity_key, activity_entry)
    print(f"Added activity. List length: {result}")

# Get recent activities (most recent first)
recent_activities = r.lrange(activity_key, 0, 2)
print("Recent activities:")
for activity in recent_activities:
    print(f"  {activity}")
Added activity. List length: 1 Added activity. List length: 2 Added activity. List length: 3 Added activity. List length: 4 Added activity. List length: 5 Recent activities: 2025-08-31 10:30:45: Logged in 2025-08-31 10:30:44: Viewed Data Scientist posting 2025-08-31 10:30:43: Saved Full Stack Developer job
Example 2: Job application queue (stack behavior)
# Process job applications in LIFO order (last in, first out)
application_queue = "pending_applications"

# Add job applications
applications = [
    "JobPost:1-User:100-Applied",
    "JobPost:2-User:101-Applied", 
    "JobPost:3-User:102-Applied",
    "JobPost:1-User:103-Applied"  # Same job, different user
]

print("Adding applications to queue:")
for app in applications:
    result = r.lpush(application_queue, app)
    print(f"  {app} -> Queue length: {result}")

# Process most recent applications first
print("\nProcessing applications (LIFO):")
while r.llen(application_queue) > 0:
    application = r.lpop(application_queue)
    print(f"  Processing: {application}")
    # Here you would process the application
    remaining = r.llen(application_queue)
    print(f"    Remaining in queue: {remaining}")
Adding applications to queue: JobPost:1-User:100-Applied -> Queue length: 1 JobPost:2-User:101-Applied -> Queue length: 2 JobPost:3-User:102-Applied -> Queue length: 3 JobPost:1-User:103-Applied -> Queue length: 4 Processing applications (LIFO): Processing: JobPost:1-User:103-Applied Remaining in queue: 3 Processing: JobPost:3-User:102-Applied Remaining in queue: 2 Processing: JobPost:2-User:101-Applied Remaining in queue: 1 Processing: JobPost:1-User:100-Applied Remaining in queue: 0
Example 3: Error logging system
# Log errors with most recent errors at the front
error_log = "application_errors"

def log_error(error_type, message, user_id=None):
    timestamp = datetime.now().strftime("%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S")
    user_info = f" (User: {user_id})" if user_id else ""
    error_entry = f"[{timestamp}] {error_type}: {message}{user_info}"
    
    result = r.lpush(error_log, error_entry)
    print(f"Error logged. Total errors: {result}")
    return result

# Simulate various errors
log_error("ValidationError", "Invalid salary range in job posting", user_id=100)
log_error("DatabaseError", "Connection timeout during job search")
log_error("AuthenticationError", "Invalid login credentials", user_id=101) 
log_error("PermissionError", "User tried to delete another user's job post", user_id=102)

# Get most recent errors
print("\nMost recent errors:")
recent_errors = r.lrange(error_log, 0, 2)
for i, error in enumerate(recent_errors, 1):
    print(f"{i}. {error}")
