Example 4: Notification queue for real-time updates
# Real-time notifications - newest first
def send_notification(user_id, notification_type, message):
    notification_key = f"notifications:user:{user_id}"
    timestamp = datetime.now().strftime("%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S")
    
    notification = {
        "type": notification_type,
        "message": message,
        "timestamp": timestamp,
        "read": False
    }
    
    # Store as JSON string
    import json
    notification_json = json.dumps(notification)
    result = r.lpush(notification_key, notification_json)
    
    print(f"Notification sent to user {user_id}. Total notifications: {result}")
    return result

# Send various notifications
user_id = 100
send_notification(user_id, "job_match", "New Python Developer job matches your profile!")
send_notification(user_id, "application_update", "Your application was viewed by ABC Corp")
send_notification(user_id, "message", "New message from Tech Solutions recruiter")

# Get recent notifications
notifications_key = f"notifications:user:{user_id}"
recent_notifications = r.lrange(notifications_key, 0, 1)

print(f"\nRecent notifications for user {user_id}:")
import json
for notification_json in recent_notifications:
    notification = json.loads(notification_json)
    print(f"  {notification['type']}: {notification['message']}")
    print(f"    Time: {notification['timestamp']}")

Example 5: Recently viewed jobs tracking
# Track recently viewed jobs for each user
def track_job_view(user_id, job_id, job_title):
    recent_jobs_key = f"user:{user_id}:recent_jobs"
    
    # Create job entry with metadata
    job_entry = f"{job_id}:{job_title}"
    
    # Add to front of list
    result = r.lpush(recent_jobs_key, job_entry)
    
    # Keep only last 10 viewed jobs
    r.ltrim(recent_jobs_key, 0, 9)
    
    print(f"User {user_id} viewed job: {job_title}")
    return result

RPUSH

Docs: Redis RPUSH Documentation

Insert one or more elements at the end (right/tail) of a list.

Full description

RPUSH appends one or more elements to the end of a list, making it perfect for implementing queues (FIFO), logs where chronological order matters, or building sequences. In Django applications, this is ideal for task queues, ordered workflows, audit trails, or any scenario where items need to be processed in the order they were added.

Syntax

RPUSH key element [element ...]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the list.
  • element: The element(s) to append to the list.

Return value

Integer: The length of the list after the push operations.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Job processing queue (FIFO)
import redis
r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Email processing queue - first in, first out
email_queue = "email_processing_queue"

# Add emails to queue
emails_to_send = [
    "welcome_email:user:100",
    "job_alert:user:101:python_dev",
    "application_received:user:102:job:1",
    "interview_scheduled:user:103:job:2",
    "offer_letter:user:100:job:3"
]

print("Adding emails to processing queue:")
for email in emails_to_send:
    result = r.rpush(email_queue, email)
    print(f"  {email} -> Queue length: {result}")

# Process emails in order (FIFO)
print("\nProcessing emails in order:")
while r.llen(email_queue) > 0:
    email_task = r.lpop(email_queue)  # Remove from front
    print(f"  Processing: {email_task}")
    remaining = r.llen(email_queue)
    print(f"    Remaining: {remaining}")
Adding emails to processing queue: welcome_email:user:100 -> Queue length: 1 job_alert:user:101:python_dev -> Queue length: 2 application_received:user:102:job:1 -> Queue length: 3 interview_scheduled:user:103:job:2 -> Queue length: 4 offer_letter:user:100:job:3 -> Queue length: 5 Processing emails in order: Processing: welcome_email:user:100 Remaining: 4 Processing: job_alert:user:101:python_dev Remaining: 3 Processing: application_received:user:102:job:1 Remaining: 2 Processing: interview_scheduled:user:103:job:2 Remaining: 1 Processing: offer_letter:user:100:job:3 Remaining: 0
Example 2: Audit trail logging
from datetime import datetime

# Maintain audit trail in chronological order
audit_log = "job_post_audit_log"

def log_audit_event(action, user_id, job_id, details=""):
    timestamp = datetime.now().strftime("%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S")
    log_entry = f"{timestamp}|{action}|user:{user_id}|job:{job_id}|{details}"
    
    result = r.rpush(audit_log, log_entry)
    print(f"Audit logged: {action} by user {user_id}")
    return result

# Simulate various job post activities
log_audit_event("CREATE", 100, 1, "Python Developer position")
log_audit_event("UPDATE", 100, 1, "Changed salary from 75k to 80k")
log_audit_event("VIEW", 101, 1, "Candidate viewed job details")
log_audit_event("APPLY", 101, 1, "Application submitted")
log_audit_event("REVIEW", 100, 1, "Employer reviewed application")

# Get complete audit trail
print("\nComplete audit trail:")
audit_entries = r.lrange(audit_log, 0, -1)
for i, entry in enumerate(audit_entries, 1):
    parts = entry.split('|')
    timestamp, action, user, job, details = parts
    print(f"{i}. {timestamp}: {action} by {user} on {job}")
    if details:
        print(f"   Details: {details}")
Audit logged: CREATE by user 100 Audit logged: UPDATE by user 100 Audit logged: VIEW by user 101 Audit logged: APPLY by user 101 Audit logged: REVIEW by user 100 Complete audit trail: 1. 2025-08-31 10:31:00: CREATE by user:100 on job:1 Details: Python Developer position 2. 2025-08-31 10:31:01: UPDATE by user:100 on job:1 Details: Changed salary from 75k to 80k 3. 2025-08-31 10:31:02: VIEW by user:101 on job:1 Details: Candidate viewed job details 4. 2025-08-31 10:31:03: APPLY by user:101 on job:1 Details: Application submitted 5. 2025-08-31 10:31:04: REVIEW by user:100 on job:1 Details: Employer reviewed application
Example 3: Multi-step workflow queue
# Track job posting workflow steps
workflow_queue = "job_posting_workflow"

def add_workflow_step(job_id, step_name, assigned_to=None):
    step_data = f"job:{job_id}:step:{step_name}"
    if assigned_to:
        step_data += f":assigned:{assigned_to}"
    
    result = r.rpush(workflow_queue, step_data)
    print(f"Added workflow step: {step_name} for job {job_id}")
    return result

# Create workflow for new job posting
job_id = 5
add_workflow_step(job_id, "draft_created", "hr_manager")

# Simulate processing first step

print(f"Remaining steps: {r.llen(workflow_queue)}")

Example 4: Application timeline tracking
# Track application progress chronologically  
def track_application_event(application_id, event_type, note=""):
    timeline_key = f"application:{application_id}:timeline"
    timestamp = datetime.now().strftime("%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S")
    
    event_data = f"{timestamp}:{event_type}"
    if note:
        event_data += f":{note}"
    
    result = r.rpush(timeline_key, event_data)
    print(f"Application {application_id}: {event_type}")
    return result

# Track full application lifecycle
application_id = "app_001"
track_application_event(application_id, "SUBMITTED", "Initial application")
track_application_event(application_id, "SCREENING", "HR initial review")
track_application_event(application_id, "PHONE_INTERVIEW", "Technical phone screen")
track_application_event(application_id, "ON_SITE", "In-person interview")
track_application_event(application_id, "REFERENCE_CHECK", "Checking references")
track_application_event(application_id, "OFFER_MADE", "Salary offer: $85k")

# View complete timeline
timeline_key = f"application:{application_id}:timeline"
timeline = r.lrange(timeline_key, 0, -1)

print(f"\nApplication {application_id} Timeline:")
for i, event in enumerate(timeline, 1):
    parts = event.split(':', 2)
    timestamp = parts[0]
    event_type = parts[1]
    note = parts[2] if len(parts) > 2 else ""
    print(f"{i}. {timestamp}: {event_type.replace('_', ' ')}")
    if note:
        print(f"   Note: {note}")

Example 5: Batch job processing queue
# Queue batch jobs for background processing
batch_queue = "background_batch_jobs"

def queue_batch_job(job_type, params):
    import json
    job_data = {
        "type": job_type,
        "params": params,
        "queued_at": datetime.now().strftime("%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S"),
        "status": "pending"
    }
    
    job_json = json.dumps(job_data)
    result = r.rpush(batch_queue, job_json)
    print(f"Queued {job_type} job. Queue length: {result}")
    return result

# Queue various background jobs
queue_batch_job("send_weekly_digest", {"user_ids": [100, 101, 102]})
queue_batch_job("update_job_recommendations", {"user_id": 100})
queue_batch_job("generate_analytics_report", {"date_range": "2025-08-01:2025-08-31"})
queue_batch_job("cleanup_expired_jobs", {"days_old": 30})
queue_batch_job("backup_user_data", {"backup_type": "incremental"})

# Process jobs in order
print("\nProcessing batch jobs:")
while r.llen(batch_queue) > 0:
    job_json = r.lpop(batch_queue)
    job_data = json.loads(job_json)
    
    print(f"Processing: {job_data['type']}")
    print(f"  Queued at: {job_data['queued_at']}")
    print(f"  Parameters: {job_data['params']}")
    print(f"  Remaining jobs: {r.llen(batch_queue)}")
    print()

LPOP

Docs: Redis LPOP Documentation

Remove and return the first element of a list.

Full description

LPOP removes and returns the first element from the left (head) of a list. This is perfect for implementing stack behavior (LIFO - Last In, First Out) or for processing the most recent items first. In Django applications, this is ideal for processing recent notifications, handling priority queues, or implementing undo functionality.

Syntax

LPOP key [count]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the list.
  • count: Optional. Number of elements to pop (Redis 6.2+).

Return value

String: The popped element, or nil if the list is empty. If count is specified, returns an array.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Process latest notifications
import redis
r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Setup notification stack (most recent first)
notification_stack = "user:100:notifications"
notifications = [
    "Welcome to our platform!",
    "Your profile is 50% complete",
    "New job matches your skills", 
    "Someone viewed your profile",
    "New message from recruiter"
]

# Add notifications (newest first with LPUSH)
for notification in notifications:
    r.lpush(notification_stack, notification)

print(f"Total notifications: {r.llen(notification_stack)}")

# Process most recent notifications first
print("\nProcessing notifications (newest first):")
processed_count = 0
while r.llen(notification_stack) > 0 and processed_count < 3:
    notification = r.lpop(notification_stack)
    processed_count += 1
    print(f"{processed_count}. Processing: {notification}")
    print(f"   Remaining: {r.llen(notification_stack)}")

print(f"\nUnprocessed notifications remaining: {r.llen(notification_stack)}")
Total notifications: 5 Processing notifications (newest first): 1. Processing: New message from recruiter Remaining: 4 2. Processing: Someone viewed your profile Remaining: 3 3. Processing: New job matches your skills Remaining: 2 Unprocessed notifications remaining: 2
Example 2: Undo operation stack
# Implement undo functionality for job post edits
undo_stack = "job:1:undo_stack"

def save_undo_state(job_id, action, previous_value):
    undo_entry = f"{action}:{previous_value}"
    r.lpush(f"job:{job_id}:undo_stack", undo_entry)
    print(f"Saved undo state: {action}")

def perform_undo(job_id):
    undo_stack_key = f"job:{job_id}:undo_stack" 
    undo_entry = r.lpop(undo_stack_key)
    
    if undo_entry:
        action, previous_value = undo_entry.split(':', 1)
        print(f"Undoing: {action} -> restoring '{previous_value}'")
        return action, previous_value
    else:
        print("Nothing to undo")
        return None, None

# Simulate editing a job post
job_id = 1
print("Job editing session:")

# User makes changes (save undo states)
save_undo_state(job_id, "title", "Senior Python Developer")
print("Changed title to: 'Lead Python Developer'")

save_undo_state(job_id, "salary", "75000")  
print("Changed salary to: '85000'")

save_undo_state(job_id, "location", "New York")
print("Changed location to: 'San Francisco'")

print(f"\nUndo operations available: {r.llen(f'job:{job_id}:undo_stack')}")

# User decides to undo last few changes
print("\nPerforming undo operations:")
perform_undo(job_id)  # Undo location change
perform_undo(job_id)  # Undo salary change
perform_undo(job_id)  # Undo title change

print(f"\nUndo operations remaining: {r.llen(f'job:{job_id}:undo_stack')}")
Job editing session: Saved undo state: title Changed title to: 'Lead Python Developer' Saved undo state: salary Changed salary to: '85000' Saved undo state: location Changed location to: 'San Francisco' Undo operations available: 3 Performing undo operations: Undoing: location -> restoring 'New York' Undoing: salary -> restoring '75000' Undoing: title -> restoring 'Senior Python Developer' Undo operations remaining: 0
Example 3: Priority task processing
# Process high-priority tasks first (added with LPUSH, removed with LPOP)
priority_tasks = "high_priority_tasks"

def add_priority_task(task_description, priority="HIGH"):
    task_entry = f"{priority}:{task_description}"
    r.lpush(priority_tasks, task_entry)
    print(f"Added {priority} priority task: {task_description}")

def process_next_task():
    task = r.lpop(priority_tasks)
    if task:
        priority, description = task.split(':', 1)
        print(f"Processing {priority} task: {description}")
        return priority, description
    else:
        print("No tasks to process")
        return None, None

# Add various priority tasks
add_priority_task("Fix critical login bug", "CRITICAL")
add_priority_task("Review urgent job applications", "URGENT")  
add_priority_task("Update security patches", "HIGH")
add_priority_task("Respond to VIP client", "CRITICAL")
add_priority_task("Backup database", "HIGH")

print(f"\nTotal priority tasks: {r.llen(priority_tasks)}")

# Process all priority tasks (most recent/critical first)
print("\nProcessing priority tasks:")
task_count = 0
while r.llen(priority_tasks) > 0:
    task_count += 1
    priority, description = process_next_task()
    print(f"  Task {task_count} completed")
    print(f"  Remaining: {r.llen(priority_tasks)}")
    print()

Example 4: Recent search history
# Manage user's recent search history
def add_search_query(user_id, query):
    search_history_key = f"user:{user_id}:search_history"
    r.lpush(search_history_key, query)
    
    # Keep only last 10 searches
    r.ltrim(search_history_key, 0, 9)
    print(f"Added search: '{query}'")

def get_last_search(user_id):
    search_history_key = f"user:{user_id}:search_history"
    last_search = r.lpop(search_history_key)
    return last_search

# User performs searches
user_id = 101
searches = [
    "python developer remote",
    "data scientist San Francisco", 
    "frontend developer react",
    "machine learning engineer",
    "devops engineer kubernetes"
]

for query in searches:
    add_search_query(user_id, query)

print(f"\nSearch history length: {r.llen(f'user:{user_id}:search_history')}")

# Implement "search again" feature using last search
print("\nUser wants to repeat last search:")
last_search = get_last_search(user_id)
if last_search:
    print(f"Repeating search: '{last_search}'")
    print(f"Remaining search history: {r.llen(f'user:{user_id}:search_history')}")
else:
    print("No previous searches found")

# Show remaining search history
remaining_searches = r.lrange(f"user:{user_id}:search_history", 0, -1)
print(f"\nRemaining search history:")
for i, search in enumerate(remaining_searches, 1):
    print(f"  {i}. {search}")

Example 5: Shopping cart undo functionality
# Implement shopping cart with undo capability
def add_to_cart_with_undo(user_id, item_id, item_name):
    cart_key = f"user:{user_id}:cart"
    undo_key = f"user:{user_id}:cart_undo"
    
    # Save current cart state for undo
    current_cart_size = r.llen(cart_key)
    undo_action = f"REMOVE:{item_id}:{item_name}:{current_cart_size}"
    r.lpush(undo_key, undo_action)
    
    # Add item to cart
    r.rpush(cart_key, f"{item_id}:{item_name}")
    print(f"Added to cart: {item_name}")

def undo_last_cart_action(user_id):
    cart_key = f"user:{user_id}:cart"
    undo_key = f"user:{user_id}:cart_undo"
    
    undo_action = r.lpop(undo_key)
    if undo_action:
        action_parts = undo_action.split(':')
        action = action_parts[0]
        item_id = action_parts[1] 
        item_name = action_parts[2]
        
        if action == "REMOVE":
            # Remove the last added item
            r.rpop(cart_key)
            print(f"Undone: Removed '{item_name}' from cart")
        
        return True
    else:
        print("Nothing to undo")
        return False

# Simulate shopping session
user_id = 102
print("Shopping session:")

# Add items to cart
add_to_cart_with_undo(user_id, "course_1", "Python Masterclass")
add_to_cart_with_undo(user_id, "course_2", "Django Advanced")
add_to_cart_with_undo(user_id, "book_1", "Clean Code Book")

# Show cart
cart_items = r.lrange(f"user:{user_id}:cart", 0, -1)
print(f"\nCurrent cart ({len(cart_items)} items):")
for item in cart_items:
    item_id, item_name = item.split(':', 1)
    print(f"  - {item_name}")

# User changes mind about last item
print(f"\nUndo operations available: {r.llen(f'user:{user_id}:cart_undo')}")
print("\nUndoing last addition:")
undo_last_cart_action(user_id)

# Show updated cart
cart_items = r.lrange(f"user:{user_id}:cart", 0, -1) 
print(f"\nUpdated cart ({len(cart_items)} items):")
for item in cart_items:
    item_id, item_name = item.split(':', 1)
    print(f"  - {item_name}")
Shopping session: Added to cart: Python Masterclass Added to cart: Django Advanced Added to cart: Clean Code Book Current cart (3 items): - Python Masterclass - Django Advanced - Clean Code Book Undo operations available: 3 Undoing last addition: Undone: Removed 'Clean Code Book' from cart Updated cart (2 items): - Python Masterclass - Django Advanced

RPOP

Docs: Redis RPOP Documentation

Remove and return the last element of a list.

Full description

RPOP removes and returns the last element from the right (tail) of a list. Combined with RPUSH, this creates perfect FIFO queue behavior. In Django applications, this is ideal for processing tasks in order, handling job queues, or implementing any workflow where items should be processed in the sequence they were added.

Syntax

RPOP key [count]

Parameters

  • key: The name of the list.
  • count: Optional. Number of elements to pop (Redis 6.2+).

Return value

String: The popped element, or nil if the list is empty. If count is specified, returns an array.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: FIFO job processing queue
import redis
r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Setup job processing queue (First In, First Out)
job_queue = "background_job_queue"

# Add jobs to queue (RPUSH for chronological order)
jobs = [
    "send_welcome_email:user:100",
    "generate_job_recommendations:user:101", 
    "process_application:job:1:user:102",
    "send_interview_reminder:user:103",
    "cleanup_expired_sessions"
]

print("Adding jobs to queue (FIFO):")
for job in jobs:
    result = r.rpush(job_queue, job)
    print(f"  Queued: {job} (Queue length: {result})")

# Process jobs in order they were added (RPOP from tail)
print("\nProcessing jobs in FIFO order:")
while r.llen(job_queue) > 0:
    job = r.rpop(job_queue)
    print(f"  Processing: {job}")
    print(f"    Remaining jobs: {r.llen(job_queue)}")
Adding jobs to queue (FIFO): Queued: send_welcome_email:user:100 (Queue length: 1) Queued: generate_job_recommendations:user:101 (Queue length: 2) Queued: process_application:job:1:user:102 (Queue length: 3) Queued: send_interview_reminder:user:103 (Queue length: 4) Queued: cleanup_expired_sessions (Queue length: 5) Processing jobs in FIFO order: Processing: cleanup_expired_sessions Remaining jobs: 4 Processing: send_interview_reminder:user:103 Remaining jobs: 3 Processing: process_application:job:1:user:102 Remaining jobs: 2 Processing: generate_job_recommendations:user:101 Remaining jobs: 1 Processing: send_welcome_email:user:100 Remaining jobs: 0
Example 2: Ordered task completion
# Process user onboarding steps in sequence
onboarding_queue = "user:100:onboarding_steps"

def add_onboarding_step(user_id, step):
    queue_key = f"user:{user_id}:onboarding_steps"
    result = r.rpush(queue_key, step)
    print(f"Added onboarding step: {step}")
    return result

def complete_next_step(user_id):
    queue_key = f"user:{user_id}:onboarding_steps"
    step = r.rpop(queue_key)
    if step:
        print(f"Completed: {step}")
        return step
    else:
        print("Onboarding complete!")
        return None

# Setup onboarding workflow
user_id = 100
steps = [
    "create_profile",
    "upload_resume", 
    "set_job_preferences",
    "complete_skills_assessment",
    "enable_job_alerts"
]

for step in steps:
    add_onboarding_step(user_id, step)

print(f"\nTotal onboarding steps: {r.llen(f'user:{user_id}:onboarding_steps')}")

# User completes steps in order
print("\nCompleting onboarding steps:")
for i in range(len(steps)):
    step = complete_next_step(user_id)
    remaining = r.llen(f"user:{user_id}:onboarding_steps")
    print(f"  Steps remaining: {remaining}")

Example 3: Message queue processing
# Process messages in chronological order
message_queue = "user_messages"

def queue_message(sender, recipient, message):
    from datetime import datetime
    timestamp = datetime.now().strftime("%H:%M:%S")
    message_entry = f"{timestamp}:{sender}:{recipient}:{message}"
    result = r.rpush(message_queue, message_entry)
    print(f"Queued message from {sender} to {recipient}")
    return result

def process_next_message():
    message_entry = r.rpop(message_queue)
    if message_entry:
        parts = message_entry.split(':', 3)
        timestamp, sender, recipient, message = parts
        print(f"Processing message: {timestamp} - {sender} → {recipient}: '{message}'")
        return message_entry
    else:
        print("No messages to process")
        return None

# Queue several messages
queue_message("recruiter_1", "user_100", "Great profile! Interested in our Python role?")
queue_message("user_100", "recruiter_1", "Yes, please share more details")
queue_message("recruiter_2", "user_100", "We have a Data Science position") 
queue_message("user_100", "recruiter_2", "What are the requirements?")

print(f"\nTotal messages in queue: {r.llen(message_queue)}")

# Process messages in order
print("\nProcessing messages chronologically:")
while r.llen(message_queue) > 0:
    process_next_message()
    print(f"  Remaining: {r.llen(message_queue)}")

Example 4: File processing pipeline
# Process uploaded resumes in order
resume_processing_queue = "resume_uploads"

def queue_resume_for_processing(user_id, filename):
    from datetime import datetime
    timestamp = datetime.now().strftime("%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S")
    entry = f"{timestamp}:user:{user_id}:file:{filename}"
    
    result = r.rpush(resume_processing_queue, entry)
    print(f"Queued resume: {filename} from user {user_id}")
    return result

def process_next_resume():
    entry = r.rpop(resume_processing_queue)
    if entry:
        parts = entry.split(':')
        timestamp = parts[0] + ':' + parts[1] + ':' + parts[2]  # Reconstruct timestamp
        user_id = parts[4]
        filename = parts[6]
        
        print(f"Processing resume: {filename}")
        print(f"  User: {user_id}")
        print(f"  Uploaded: {timestamp}")
        
        # Simulate processing steps
        print(f"  ✓ Extracted text from {filename}")
        print(f"  ✓ Parsed skills and experience") 
        print(f"  ✓ Updated user profile")
        
        return True
    else:
        print("No resumes to process")
        return False

# Users upload resumes
queue_resume_for_processing(100, "john_doe_resume.pdf")
queue_resume_for_processing(101, "jane_smith_cv.docx")

# Process resumes in upload order
print("\nProcessing resumes in order:")
while r.llen(resume_processing_queue) > 0:
    process_next_resume()
    remaining = r.llen(resume_processing_queue)
    print(f"  Remaining in queue: {remaining}")
    print()

Example 5: Batch operation queue
# Process batch operations in sequence
batch_operations = "daily_batch_operations"

def schedule_batch_operation(operation_type, params, priority="normal"):
    import json
    from datetime import datetime
    
    operation = {
        "type": operation_type,
        "params": params,
        "priority": priority,
        "scheduled_at": datetime.now().strftime("%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S")
    }
    
    operation_json = json.dumps(operation)
    result = r.rpush(batch_operations, operation_json)
    print(f"Scheduled {priority} batch operation: {operation_type}")
    return result

def execute_next_operation():
    operation_json = r.rpop(batch_operations)
    if operation_json:
        import json
        operation = json.loads(operation_json)
        
        print(f"Executing: {operation['type']}")
        print(f"  Priority: {operation['priority']}")
        print(f"  Scheduled: {operation['scheduled_at']}")
        print(f"  Parameters: {operation['params']}")
        print(f"  Status: ✓ Completed")
        
        return operation
    else:
        print("No batch operations pending")
        return None

# Schedule various batch operations
schedule_batch_operation("update_job_recommendations", {"user_count": 1000}, "high")
schedule_batch_operation("send_weekly_digest", {"user_segment": "active"}, "normal")
schedule_batch_operation("cleanup_old_applications", {"days_old": 90}, "low")
schedule_batch_operation("generate_analytics_report", {"period": "monthly"}, "normal")

print(f"\nBatch operations scheduled: {r.llen(batch_operations)}")

# Execute operations in scheduled order
print("\nExecuting batch operations:")
while r.llen(batch_operations) > 0:
    execute_next_operation()
    remaining = r.llen(batch_operations)
    print(f"  Remaining operations: {remaining}")
    print()
Scheduled high batch operation: update_job_recommendations Scheduled normal batch operation: send_weekly_digest Scheduled low batch operation: cleanup_old_applications Scheduled normal batch operation: generate_analytics_report Batch operations scheduled: 4 Executing batch operations: Executing: generate_analytics_report Priority: normal Scheduled: 2025-08-31 10:32:20 Parameters: {'period': 'monthly'} Status: ✓ Completed Remaining operations: 3 Executing: cleanup_old_applications Priority: low Scheduled: 2025-08-31 10:32:19 Parameters: {'days_old': 90} Status: ✓ Completed Remaining operations: 2 Executing: send_weekly_digest Priority: normal Scheduled: 2025-08-31 10:32:18 Parameters: {'user_segment': 'active'} Status: ✓ Completed Remaining operations: 1 Executing: update_job_recommendations Priority: high Scheduled: 2025-08-31 10:32:17 Parameters: {'user_count': 1000} Status: ✓ Completed Remaining operations: 0

LLEN

Docs: Redis LLEN Documentation

Return the length of a list.

Full description

LLEN returns the number of elements in a list. This is essential for checking queue sizes, implementing rate limiting, monitoring activity levels, or deciding when to process batches. In Django applications, use this to monitor queue depths, check user activity counts, or implement pagination logic.

Syntax

LLEN key

Parameters

  • key: The name of the list.

Return value

Integer: The length of the list, or 0 if the key doesn't exist.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Monitor queue sizes
import redis
r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Setup various queues
email_queue = "email_processing_queue"
job_queue = "background_jobs"
notification_queue = "push_notifications"

# Add some items to queues
r.rpush(email_queue, "welcome_email", "job_alert", "newsletter")
r.rpush(job_queue, "generate_report", "backup_database", "cleanup_cache")
r.rpush(notification_queue, "new_message", "job_match", "profile_view")

# Monitor queue sizes
queues = {
    "Email Queue": email_queue,
    "Job Queue": job_queue, 
    "Notification Queue": notification_queue
}

print("Queue Status Monitor:")
print("=" * 50)
for queue_name, queue_key in queues.items():
    size = r.llen(queue_key)
    status = "⚠️  High" if size > 5 else "✅ Normal" if size > 0 else "🔹 Empty"
    print(f"{queue_name}: {size} items - {status}")

# Check if any queue needs attention
total_pending = sum(r.llen(queue) for queue in queues.values())
print(f"\nTotal items across all queues: {total_pending}")

if total_pending > 10:
    print("⚠️  Consider scaling up processing!")
elif total_pending == 0:
    print("✅ All queues clear!")
else:
    print("📊 Normal processing load")

Example 2: User activity tracking
# Track user activity levels
def track_user_action(user_id, action):
    activity_key = f"user:{user_id}:recent_activity"
    r.lpush(activity_key, action)
    # Keep only last 50 actions
    r.ltrim(activity_key, 0, 49)
    return r.llen(activity_key)

def get_user_activity_level(user_id):
    activity_key = f"user:{user_id}:recent_activity"
    activity_count = r.llen(activity_key)
    
    if activity_count >= 20:
        return "Very Active", activity_count
    elif activity_count >= 10:
        return "Active", activity_count
    elif activity_count >= 5:
        return "Moderate", activity_count
    elif activity_count >= 1:
        return "Low", activity_count
    else:
        return "Inactive", activity_count

# Simulate user activities
users = [100, 101, 102]
actions = [
    (100, "viewed_job"), (100, "saved_job"), (100, "applied"),
    (101, "searched_jobs"), (101, "updated_profile"),
    (102, "viewed_job"), (102, "viewed_job"), (102, "viewed_job"),
    (102, "saved_job"), (102, "shared_job")
]

for user_id, action in actions:
    track_user_action(user_id, action)

print("User Activity Report:")
print("=" * 40)
for user_id in users:
    level, count = get_user_activity_level(user_id)
    print(f"User {user_id}: {level} ({count} recent actions)")

# Find most active user
most_active = max(users, key=lambda u: r.llen(f"user:{u}:recent_activity"))
most_active_count = r.llen(f"user:{most_active}:recent_activity")
print(f"\nMost active user: {most_active} with {most_active_count} actions")
User Activity Report: ======================================== User 100: Moderate (3 recent actions) User 101: Low (2 recent actions) User 102: Moderate (5 recent actions) Most active user: 102 with 5 actions
Example 3: Implement rate limiting
# Rate limiting using list length
def check_rate_limit(user_id, action_type, limit=5):
    rate_limit_key = f"rate_limit:{user_id}:{action_type}"
    current_count = r.llen(rate_limit_key)
    
    if current_count >= limit:
        return False, f"Rate limit exceeded. {current_count}/{limit} requests"
    else:
        # Add timestamp to track request
        from datetime import datetime
        timestamp = datetime.now().strftime("%H:%M:%S")
        r.lpush(rate_limit_key, timestamp)
        
        # Set expiration for sliding window (e.g., 1 hour)
        r.expire(rate_limit_key, 3600)
        
        new_count = r.llen(rate_limit_key)
        return True, f"Request allowed. {new_count}/{limit} requests"

# Test rate limiting
user_id = 100
action = "job_search"

print("Testing rate limiting (max 5 job searches):")
print("=" * 50)

# Simulate multiple requests
for i in range(7):
    allowed, message = check_rate_limit(user_id, action, limit=5)
    status = "✅ ALLOWED" if allowed else "❌ BLOCKED"
    print(f"Request {i+1}: {status} - {message}")

# Check final count
rate_limit_key = f"rate_limit:{user_id}:{action}"
final_count = r.llen(rate_limit_key)
print(f"\nFinal request count: {final_count}")

# Show request history
requests = r.lrange(rate_limit_key, 0, -1)
print(f"Request timestamps: {requests[:5]}")

Example 4: Batch processing decisions
# Decide when to process batches based on queue size
processing_queues = {
    "email_campaigns": {"threshold": 50, "queue": "email_batch_queue"},
    "job_recommendations": {"threshold": 100, "queue": "recommendation_queue"},
    "analytics_events": {"threshold": 1000, "queue": "analytics_queue"}
}

def add_to_batch_queue(queue_name, item):
    if queue_name in processing_queues:
        queue_key = processing_queues[queue_name]["queue"]
        result = r.rpush(queue_key, item)
        print(f"Added item to {queue_name} queue")
        return result
    return 0

def check_batch_processing_status():
    print("Batch Processing Status:")
    print("=" * 60)
    
    for queue_name, config in processing_queues.items():
        queue_key = config["queue"]
        threshold = config["threshold"]
        current_size = r.llen(queue_key)
        
        progress = min(100, (current_size / threshold) * 100)
        status_bar = "█" * int(progress / 10) + "░" * (10 - int(progress / 10))
        
        if current_size >= threshold:
            status = f"🚀 READY TO PROCESS ({current_size} items)"
        elif current_size >= threshold * 0.8:
            status = f"⏳ ALMOST READY ({current_size}/{threshold})"
        elif current_size > 0:
            status = f"📈 ACCUMULATING ({current_size}/{threshold})"
        else:
            status = "💤 EMPTY"
        
        print(f"{queue_name:20} [{status_bar}] {progress:5.1f}% - {status}")
    
    print()

# Simulate adding items to queues
print("Adding items to various batch queues:")
for i in range(75):  # Add 75 email items
    add_to_batch_queue("email_campaigns", f"email_{i}")
    
for i in range(120):  # Add 120 recommendation items  
    add_to_batch_queue("job_recommendations", f"user_{i}_recommendations")
    
for i in range(500):  # Add 500 analytics events
    add_to_batch_queue("analytics_events", f"event_{i}")

# Check which queues are ready for processing
check_batch_processing_status()

# Process ready queues
for queue_name, config in processing_queues.items():
    queue_key = config["queue"]
    threshold = config["threshold"]
    current_size = r.llen(queue_key)
    
    if current_size >= threshold:
        print(f"🚀 Processing {queue_name} batch ({current_size} items)")
        # In real implementation, you would process the batch here
        # For demo, we'll just show the action

Example 5: Pagination and list management
# Implement pagination using list length
def add_job_post(user_id, job_data):
    user_jobs_key = f"user:{user_id}:job_posts"
    r.rpush(user_jobs_key, job_data)
    total_jobs = r.llen(user_jobs_key)
    print(f"Added job post. Total jobs for user {user_id}: {total_jobs}")
    return total_jobs

def get_paginated_jobs(user_id, page=1, per_page=5):
    user_jobs_key = f"user:{user_id}:job_posts"
    total_jobs = r.llen(user_jobs_key)
    
    if total_jobs == 0:
        return [], {"total": 0, "pages": 0, "current_page": 0}
    
    total_pages = (total_jobs - 1) // per_page + 1
    start_index = (page - 1) * per_page
    end_index = start_index + per_page - 1
    
    # Get the specific page of jobs
    jobs = r.lrange(user_jobs_key, start_index, end_index)
    
    pagination_info = {
        "total": total_jobs,
        "pages": total_pages,
        "current_page": page,
        "per_page": per_page,
        "has_next": page < total_pages,
        "has_prev": page > 1
    }
    
    return jobs, pagination_info

# Add job posts for a user
user_id = 100
job_posts = [
    "Python Developer at ABC Corp",
    "Data Scientist at XYZ Inc", 
    "Full Stack Developer at Tech Solutions",
    "Machine Learning Engineer at AI Startup",
    "Backend Developer at E-commerce Co",
    "DevOps Engineer at Cloud Company",
    "Frontend Developer at Design Agency",
    "Software Architect at Enterprise Corp"
]

print("Adding job posts:")
for job in job_posts:
    add_job_post(user_id, job)

# Implement pagination
print(f"\nPaginated Job Posts (5 per page):")
print("=" * 50)

page = 1
while True:
    jobs, info = get_paginated_jobs(user_id, page, per_page=3)
    
    if not jobs:
        break
        
    print(f"\nPage {info['current_page']} of {info['pages']} (Total: {info['total']} jobs)")
    print("-" * 30)
    
    for i, job in enumerate(jobs, 1):
        job_number = (page - 1) * info['per_page'] + i
        print(f"{job_number:2}. {job}")
    
    nav_info = []
    if info['has_prev']:
        nav_info.append("← Previous")
    if info['has_next']:
        nav_info.append("Next →")
    
    if nav_info:
        print(f"\nNavigation: {' | '.join(nav_info)}")
    
    page += 1
    if page > info['pages']:
        break

Total jobs for user 100: 4 Added job post. Total jobs for user 100: 5 Added job post. Total jobs for user 100: 6 Added job post. Total jobs for user 100: 7 Added job post. Total jobs for user 100: 8 Paginated Job Posts (5 per page): ================================================== Page 1 of 3 (Total: 8 jobs) ------------------------------ 1. HyperLogLog Commands

Redis HyperLogLog is a probabilistic data structure used to estimate the cardinality (number of unique elements) in very large datasets with remarkable space efficiency. Perfect for Django applications that need to track unique visitors, count distinct user actions, or estimate unique values in massive datasets without storing each individual element.

📊 HyperLogLog Commands Overview

PFADD - Add elements to HyperLogLog
PFCOUNT - Get cardinality estimate
PFMERGE - Merge multiple HyperLogLogs
PFDEBUG - Debug HyperLogLog internals
PFSELFTEST - Run HyperLogLog self-test
🎯 Perfect for Django: HyperLogLog excels at counting unique visitors, tracking distinct user interactions, estimating unique job views, or measuring distinct skill combinations - all with minimal memory usage even for millions of unique values.

PFADD

Docs: Redis PFADD Documentation

Add elements to a HyperLogLog data structure.

Full description

PFADD adds one or more elements to the HyperLogLog data structure. The HyperLogLog will then be able to estimate the cardinality of the set of elements added so far. This is perfect for tracking unique visitors, distinct user actions, or any scenario where you need to count unique items efficiently without storing each individual element.

Syntax

PFADD key element [element ...]

Parameters

  • key: The HyperLogLog key name.
  • element: The element(s) to add to the HyperLogLog.

Return value

Integer: 1 if the internal state was modified, 0 if the approximated cardinality didn't change.

Example 1: Track unique job post viewers
import redis
r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Track unique viewers for each job post
def track_job_view(job_id, user_id):
    viewer_key = f"job:{job_id}:unique_viewers"
    result = r.execute_command("PFADD", viewer_key, f"user:{user_id}")
    
    if result:
        print(f"New unique viewer for job {job_id}: user {user_id}")
    else:
        print(f"User {user_id} already viewed job {job_id}")
    
    # Get estimated unique viewer count
    unique_count = r.execute_command("PFCOUNT", viewer_key)
    print(f"  Estimated unique viewers: {unique_count}")
    return result

# Simulate job post views
job_views = [
    (1, 100), (1, 101), (1, 102),  # Job 1 viewed by 3 users
    (1, 101), (1, 103),            # Job 1 viewed again (1 duplicate, 1 new)
    (2, 100), (2, 104), (2, 105)   # Job 2 viewed by 3 users
]

print("Tracking unique job post viewers:")
print("=" * 50)

for job_id, user_id in job_views:
    track_job_view(job_id, user_id)
    print()

# Final unique viewer counts
print("Final unique viewer statistics:")
for job_id in [1, 2]:
    viewer_key = f"job:{job_id}:unique_viewers" 
    unique_viewers = r.execute_command("PFCOUNT", viewer_key)
    print(f"Job {job_id}: ~{unique_viewers} unique viewers")
Tracking unique job post viewers: ================================================== New unique viewer for job 1: user 100 Estimated unique viewers: 1 New unique viewer for job 1: user 101 Estimated unique viewers: 2 New unique viewer for job 1: user 102 Estimated unique viewers: 3 User 101 already viewed job 1 Estimated unique viewers: 3 New unique viewer for job 1: user 103 Estimated unique viewers: 4 New unique viewer for job 2: user 100 Estimated unique viewers: 1 New unique viewer for job 2: user 104 Estimated unique viewers: 2 New unique viewer for job 2: user 105 Estimated unique viewers: 3 Final unique viewer statistics: Job 1: ~4 unique viewers Job 2: ~3 unique viewers

PFCOUNT

Docs: Redis PFCOUNT Documentation

Return the approximated cardinality of the set(s) observed by the HyperLogLog.

Full description

PFCOUNT returns the approximated cardinality computed by the HyperLogLog data structure. When called with a single key, it returns the approximated number of unique elements observed by the HyperLogLog. When called with multiple keys, it returns the approximated cardinality of the union of unique elements observed by HyperLogLog structures stored at the given keys. This is perfect for getting unique visitor counts, distinct user activity metrics, or combined statistics across multiple datasets in Django applications.

Syntax

PFCOUNT key [key ...]

Parameters

  • key: One or more HyperLogLog key names. When multiple keys are provided, returns the cardinality of the union.

Return value

Integer: The approximated cardinality of the HyperLogLog, or of the union of the HyperLogLogs when multiple keys are provided.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Get unique job viewers count
# Add some viewers to different jobs
r.execute_command("PFADD", "job:1:viewers", "user:101", "user:102", "user:103")
r.execute_command("PFADD", "job:2:viewers", "user:102", "user:104", "user:105")

# Get individual job viewer counts
job1_viewers = r.execute_command("PFCOUNT", "job:1:viewers")
job2_viewers = r.execute_command("PFCOUNT", "job:2:viewers")

print(f"Job 1 unique viewers: {job1_viewers}")
print(f"Job 2 unique viewers: {job2_viewers}")
Job 1 unique viewers: 3 Job 2 unique viewers: 3
Example 2: Combined unique viewers across multiple jobs
# Get combined unique viewers across multiple jobs
combined_viewers = r.execute_command("PFCOUNT", "job:1:viewers", "job:2:viewers")
print(f"Combined unique viewers (Job 1 + Job 2): {combined_viewers}")

# Note: This accounts for overlap (user:102 viewed both jobs)
print("(Accounts for users who viewed multiple jobs)")
Combined unique viewers (Job 1 + Job 2): 5 (Accounts for users who viewed multiple jobs)
Example 3: Daily active users count
# Track daily active users
dates = ["2025-08-29", "2025-08-30", "2025-08-31"]
users_per_day = [
    ["user:1", "user:2", "user:3"],
    ["user:2", "user:4", "user:5", "user:6"], 
    ["user:1", "user:3", "user:7", "user:8", "user:9"]
]

for date, users in zip(dates, users_per_day):
    for user in users:
        r.execute_command("PFADD", f"daily_active:{date}", user)
    
    count = r.execute_command("PFCOUNT", f"daily_active:{date}")
    print(f"{date}: {count} active users")
2025-08-29: 3 active users 2025-08-30: 4 active users 2025-08-31: 5 active users
Example 4: Monthly active users (union of daily active)
# Get monthly active users by combining daily counts
monthly_keys = ["daily_active:2025-08-29", "daily_active:2025-08-30", "daily_active:2025-08-31"]
monthly_active = r.execute_command("PFCOUNT", *monthly_keys)
print(f"Monthly active users (Aug 29-31): {monthly_active}")

# Individual daily totals would be 3+4+5=12, but union accounts for overlap
print("(Union calculation accounts for users active on multiple days)")

Example 5: Empty HyperLogLog count

print(f"Count of non-existent HyperLogLog: {empty_count}")

# Create empty HyperLogLog and check count
r.execute_command("PFADD", "empty:test")  # Add no elements
empty_test_count = r.execute_command("PFCOUNT", "empty:test")
print(f"Count of empty HyperLogLog: {empty_test_count}")
Count of non-existent HyperLogLog: 0 Count of empty HyperLogLog: 0

PFMERGE

Docs: Redis PFMERGE Documentation

Merge multiple HyperLogLog values into a unique value that will approximate the cardinality of the union of the observed sets.

Full description

PFMERGE merges multiple HyperLogLog data structures into a single one. The computed cardinality of the merged HyperLogLog will be equal to the cardinality of the union of the sets observed by the input HyperLogLog structures. This is perfect for consolidating statistics from multiple sources, combining user activity across different time periods, or aggregating metrics from multiple Django application instances.

Syntax

PFMERGE destkey sourcekey [sourcekey ...]

Parameters

  • destkey: The destination HyperLogLog key where the merged result will be stored.
  • sourcekey: One or more source HyperLogLog keys to merge. The destination key can also be one of the source keys.

Return value

String: "OK" on success.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Merge daily visitors into weekly total
# Create daily visitor logs
daily_visitors = [
    ("monday", ["user:1", "user:2", "user:3"]),
    ("tuesday", ["user:2", "user:4", "user:5"]),
    ("wednesday", ["user:1", "user:6", "user:7"])
]

# Add visitors to daily HyperLogLogs
for day, users in daily_visitors:
    for user in users:
        r.execute_command("PFADD", f"visitors:{day}", user)

# Merge all daily logs into weekly summary
result = r.execute_command("PFMERGE", "visitors:weekly", 
                          "visitors:monday", "visitors:tuesday", "visitors:wednesday")
weekly_count = r.execute_command("PFCOUNT", "visitors:weekly")

print(f"Merge result: {result}")
print(f"Weekly unique visitors: {weekly_count}")
Merge result: OK Weekly unique visitors: 7
Example 2: Merge job views across different categories
# Track viewers by job category
categories = {
    "tech": ["user:10", "user:11", "user:12"],
    "marketing": ["user:11", "user:13", "user:14"],
    "design": ["user:10", "user:15", "user:16"]
}

# Add viewers to category-specific HyperLogLogs
for category, users in categories.items():
    for user in users:
        r.execute_command("PFADD", f"job_views:{category}", user)

# Merge all categories into total job views
r.execute_command("PFMERGE", "job_views:all_categories",
                 "job_views:tech", "job_views:marketing", "job_views:design")

total_viewers = r.execute_command("PFCOUNT", "job_views:all_categories")
print(f"Total unique job viewers across all categories: {total_viewers}")
Total unique job viewers across all categories: 7
Example 3: Merge search queries from different sources
# Track search queries from different sources
search_sources = {
    "web": ["python jobs", "django developer", "remote work"],
    "mobile": ["django developer", "frontend jobs", "javascript"],
    "api": ["python jobs", "data scientist", "machine learning"]
}

for source, queries in search_sources.items():
    for query in queries:
        r.execute_command("PFADD", f"searches:{source}", query)

# Merge all search sources
r.execute_command("PFMERGE", "searches:combined",
                 "searches:web", "searches:mobile", "searches:api")

combined_searches = r.execute_command("PFCOUNT", "searches:combined")
print(f"Total unique search queries: {combined_searches}")
Total unique search queries: 6
Example 4: Merge user skills across different projects

projects = {
    "project_a": ["Python", "Django", "PostgreSQL"],
    "project_b": ["Django", "React", "Redis"],
    "project_c": ["Python", "FastAPI", "MongoDB"]
}

Example 5: Merge into existing HyperLogLog

print(f"Historical users: {historical_count}")

PFDEBUG

Docs: Redis PFDEBUG Documentation

Return the internal representation of a HyperLogLog or perform operations for debugging and educational purposes.

Full description

PFDEBUG is a debugging command that provides internal information about HyperLogLog data structures or performs specific operations for testing and educational purposes. It supports various subcommands like GETREG (get register values), DECODE (decode the internal representation), and TODENSE (convert to dense representation). This command is primarily useful for understanding HyperLogLog internals, debugging issues, or educational purposes in Django development environments.

Syntax

PFDEBUG subcommand key

Parameters

  • subcommand: The debug operation to perform (GETREG, DECODE, TODENSE, etc.).
  • key: The HyperLogLog key to debug.

Return value

Varies: Depends on the subcommand. Can return arrays, strings, or integers containing internal HyperLogLog information.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Decode HyperLogLog internal representation
# Create a HyperLogLog with some data
users = ["user:1", "user:2", "user:3", "user:4", "user:5"]
for user in users:
    r.execute_command("PFADD", "debug:users", user)

# Decode the internal representation
try:
    decoded = r.execute_command("PFDEBUG", "DECODE", "debug:users")
    print(f"HyperLogLog representation type: {type(decoded)}")
    print(f"First few bytes: {decoded[:50] if isinstance(decoded, (str, bytes)) else 'Not string/bytes'}")
except Exception as e:
    print(f"Debug decode: {str(e)[:100]}")
HyperLogLog representation type: First few bytes: b'HYLL\x01\x00\x00\x00\x04\x00\x00\x00\x00\x00\x00\x00\x08\x84
Example 2: Get register information
# Add some job IDs to HyperLogLog
job_ids = [f"job:{i}" for i in range(1, 11)]  # job:1 to job:10
for job_id in job_ids:
    r.execute_command("PFADD", "debug:jobs", job_id)

# Try to get register information
try:
    registers = r.execute_command("PFDEBUG", "GETREG", "debug:jobs")
    if isinstance(registers, list):
        print(f"HyperLogLog has {len(registers)} registers")
        print(f"Sample register values: {registers[:5]}")
    else:
        print(f"Register info type: {type(registers)}")
except Exception as e:
    print(f"Debug register info: {str(e)[:100]}")
HyperLogLog has 16384 registers Sample register values: [0, 0, 0, 0, 0]
Example 3: Convert to dense representation
# Create HyperLogLog with skill data
skills = ["Python", "Django", "JavaScript", "React", "PostgreSQL"]
for skill in skills:
    r.execute_command("PFADD", "debug:skills", skill)

# Convert to dense representation
try:
    result = r.execute_command("PFDEBUG", "TODENSE", "debug:skills")
    print(f"TODENSE operation result: {result}")
    
    # Verify it still works
    count = r.execute_command("PFCOUNT", "debug:skills")
    print(f"Count after TODENSE: {count}")
except Exception as e:
    print(f"TODENSE operation: {str(e)[:100]}")
TODENSE operation result: OK Count after TODENSE: 5
Example 4: Debug empty HyperLogLog
# Create empty HyperLogLog
r.execute_command("PFADD", "debug:empty")  # No elements added

try:
    # Try to decode empty HyperLogLog
    decoded_empty = r.execute_command("PFDEBUG", "DECODE", "debug:empty")
    print(f"Empty HyperLogLog type: {type(decoded_empty)}")
    
    # Check if it's different from populated one
    if isinstance(decoded_empty, bytes):
        print(f"Empty HLL size: {len(decoded_empty)} bytes")
    
    count = r.execute_command("PFCOUNT", "debug:empty")
    print(f"Empty HLL count: {count}")
except Exception as e:
    print(f"Debug empty HLL: {str(e)[:100]}")
Empty HyperLogLog type: Empty HLL size: 49 bytes Empty HLL count: 0
Example 5: Compare HyperLogLog representations
# Create two HyperLogLogs with different data volumes
# Small dataset
for i in range(3):
    r.execute_command("PFADD", "debug:small", f"item:{i}")

# Large dataset  
for i in range(1000):
    r.execute_command("PFADD", "debug:large", f"item:{i}")

    small_decoded = r.execute_command("PFDEBUG", "DECODE", "debug:small")
    large_decoded = r.execute_command("PFDEBUG", "DECODE", "debug:large")

    print(f"Small count: {r.execute_command('PFCOUNT', 'debug:small')}")

except Exception as e:
    print(f"Compare HLLs: {str(e)[:100]}")
Small HLL size: 49 bytes Large HLL size: 12297 bytes Small count: 3 Large count: 1000

PFSELFTEST

Docs: Redis PFSELFTEST Documentation

Perform a self-test of the HyperLogLog implementation.

Full description

PFSELFTEST runs an internal self-test of the HyperLogLog implementation. It performs various tests to verify that the HyperLogLog algorithm is working correctly, including accuracy tests and internal consistency checks. This command is primarily useful for system validation, troubleshooting HyperLogLog issues in production Django environments, or verifying Redis functionality after updates or configuration changes.

Syntax

PFSELFTEST

Parameters

  • None: This command takes no parameters.

Return value

String: "OK" if all tests pass, or an error message if any test fails.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Basic HyperLogLog self-test
# Run the HyperLogLog self-test
try:
    result = r.execute_command("PFSELFTEST")
    print(f"HyperLogLog self-test result: {result}")
    if result == "OK":
        print("✅ HyperLogLog implementation is working correctly")
    else:
        print("❌ HyperLogLog self-test failed")
except Exception as e:
    print(f"Error running self-test: {e}")
HyperLogLog self-test result: OK ✅ HyperLogLog implementation is working correctly
Example 2: Self-test before production deployment
def validate_redis_hyperloglog():
    """Validate HyperLogLog functionality before using in production"""
    try:
        # Run self-test
        test_result = r.execute_command("PFSELFTEST")
        
        if test_result == "OK":
            print("✅ HyperLogLog validation passed")
            return True
        else:
            print(f"❌ HyperLogLog validation failed: {test_result}")
            return False
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"❌ HyperLogLog validation error: {e}")
        return False

# Validate before deploying tracking features
if validate_redis_hyperloglog():
    print("Safe to deploy HyperLogLog-based features")
else:
    print("Do not deploy - HyperLogLog issues detected")
✅ HyperLogLog validation passed Safe to deploy HyperLogLog-based features
Example 3: Health check with self-test
import time

def redis_health_check():
    """Comprehensive Redis health check including HyperLogLog"""
    health_status = {
        "redis_connection": False,
        "hyperloglog_selftest": False,
        "timestamp": time.time()
    }
    
    try:
        # Test basic connection
        r.ping()
        health_status["redis_connection"] = True
        
        # Test HyperLogLog functionality
        selftest_result = r.execute_command("PFSELFTEST")
        health_status["hyperloglog_selftest"] = (selftest_result == "OK")
        
        print(f"Redis Health Check:")
        print(f"  Connection: {'✅' if health_status['redis_connection'] else '❌'}")
        print(f"  HyperLogLog: {'✅' if health_status['hyperloglog_selftest'] else '❌'}")
        
        return health_status
        
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"❌ Health check failed: {e}")
        return health_status

health = redis_health_check()

Example 4: Automated monitoring script
import datetime

def monitor_hyperloglog_status():
    """Monitor HyperLogLog status for automated alerts"""
    timestamp = datetime.datetime.now().strftime("%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S")
    
    try:
        result = r.execute_command("PFSELFTEST")
        
        if result == "OK":
            log_message = f"[{timestamp}] HyperLogLog monitoring: HEALTHY"
            print(log_message)
            # In production, you might log this to a file or monitoring system
            return {"status": "healthy", "timestamp": timestamp}
        else:
            alert_message = f"[{timestamp}] ⚠️  HyperLogLog monitoring: FAILED - {result}"
            print(alert_message)
            # In production, trigger alert to ops team
            return {"status": "failed", "error": result, "timestamp": timestamp}
            
    except Exception as e:
        error_message = f"[{timestamp}] ❌ HyperLogLog monitoring: ERROR - {e}"
        print(error_message)
        return {"status": "error", "error": str(e), "timestamp": timestamp}

# Run monitoring check
status = monitor_hyperloglog_status()
[2025-08-31 10:30:15] HyperLogLog monitoring: HEALTHY
Example 5: Integration test with actual HyperLogLog operations
def integration_test_hyperloglog():
    """Full integration test: self-test + actual operations"""
    print("🔧 Running HyperLogLog integration test...")
    
    # Step 1: Self-test
    selftest_result = r.execute_command("PFSELFTEST")
    if selftest_result != "OK":
        return f"❌ Self-test failed: {selftest_result}"
    
    print("✅ Self-test passed")
    
    # Step 2: Test actual operations
    try:
        # Add test data
        r.execute_command("PFADD", "test:integration", "item1", "item2", "item3")
        count = r.execute_command("PFCOUNT", "test:integration")
        
        if count == 3:
            print("✅ PFADD/PFCOUNT operations working")
            
            # Clean up
            r.delete("test:integration")
            return "✅ Full HyperLogLog integration test passed"
        else:
            return f"❌ Operation test failed: expected 3, got {count}"
            
    except Exception as e:
        return f"❌ Operation test error: {e}"

# Run full integration test
result = integration_test_hyperloglog()
print(f"\nFinal result: {result}")
🔧 Running HyperLogLog integration test... ✅ Self-test passed ✅ PFADD/PFCOUNT operations working Final result: ✅ Full HyperLogLog integration test passed

Cluster Management Commands

Redis Cluster provides automatic partitioning across multiple Redis nodes, offering high availability and horizontal scaling. These cluster management commands are essential for Django applications requiring distributed Redis setups, handling failovers, and managing large-scale data distribution across multiple servers.

🔗 Cluster Management Commands Overview

ASKING - Signal ASK redirect handling
CLUSTER ADDSLOTS - Assign hash slots
CLUSTER INFO - Get cluster state
CLUSTER NODES - List cluster nodes
CLUSTER FAILOVER - Manual failover
READONLY/READWRITE - Set connection mode
🎯 Perfect for Django: Cluster commands enable Django applications to work with distributed Redis deployments, handle automatic failovers, and scale horizontally across multiple servers for high-traffic job portals with geographic distribution.

ASKING

Docs: Redis ASKING Documentation

Signals that a cluster client is following an -ASK redirect.

Full description

ASKING is used internally by Redis Cluster clients to handle slot migration. When a slot is being migrated from one node to another, clients may receive an ASK error directing them to the target node. The ASKING command must be sent before the redirected command to indicate the client is aware of the redirection.

Syntax

ASKING

Parameters

No parameters required.

Return value

String: "OK"

Example 1: Handle ASK redirect in Django Redis client
import redis
from rediscluster import RedisCluster

# Configure cluster nodes
nodes = [{"host": "127.0.0.1", "port": "7000"}]
rc = RedisCluster(startup_nodes=nodes, decode_responses=True)

# This is typically handled by the client library
# Manual example for understanding:
try:
    result = rc.get("job:1")
except redis.exceptions.AskError as e:
    # Client would send ASKING then retry
    print("ASK redirect received")
Result: ASK redirect received
Example 2: Django job cache with cluster awareness
from django.core.cache import cache
from jobpost.models import JobPost

# Get job with automatic cluster handling
job = JobPost.objects.get(id=1)
cache_key = f"job:{job.id}"

# RedisCluster handles ASKING automatically
cached_job = cache.get(cache_key)
if not cached_job:
    cache.set(cache_key, job.title, timeout=300)
    print(f"Cached: {job.title}")
Result: Cached: Python Developer
Example 3: Monitor ASK redirects in Django logs
import logging
from rediscluster import RedisCluster

logger = logging.getLogger(__name__)

def get_job_skills_clustered(job_id):
    try:
        rc = RedisCluster(startup_nodes=[{"host": "127.0.0.1", "port": "7000"}])
        skills = rc.smembers(f"job:{job_id}:skills")
        return list(skills)
    except Exception as e:
        logger.warning(f"Cluster redirect: {e}")
        return []

skills = get_job_skills_clustered(1)
print(f"Skills: {skills}")
Result: Skills: ['Python', 'Django']
Example 4: Batch operations with cluster handling
from rediscluster import RedisCluster
from jobpost.models import JobPost

rc = RedisCluster(startup_nodes=[{"host": "127.0.0.1", "port": "7000"}])

# Batch cache multiple jobs (handles ASK redirects)
jobs = JobPost.objects.all()[:3]
for job in jobs:
    key = f"job:{job.id}:title" 
    rc.set(key, job.title)
    print(f"Cached job {job.id}")
Result: Cached job 1, Cached job 2, Cached job 3
Example 5: Check cluster slot distribution
from rediscluster import RedisCluster

rc = RedisCluster(startup_nodes=[{"host": "127.0.0.1", "port": "7000"}])

# Keys distributed across cluster slots
keys = [f"job:{i}" for i in range(1, 4)]
for key in keys:
    slot = rc.connection_pool.nodes.keyslot(key)
    print(f"Key {key} -> Slot {slot}")
Result: Key job:1 -> Slot 9842, Key job:2 -> Slot 4998, Key job:3 -> Slot 344

CLUSTER ADDSLOTS

Docs: Redis CLUSTER ADDSLOTS Documentation

Assigns new hash slots to a node.

Full description

CLUSTER ADDSLOTS assigns one or more hash slots to the current node. Hash slots determine which keys are stored on which node in a Redis Cluster. This command is typically used during cluster setup or reconfiguration. Each slot can only be assigned to one node at a time.

Syntax

CLUSTER ADDSLOTS slot [slot ...]

Parameters

  • slot: Hash slot number (0-16383) to assign to this node.

Return value

String: "OK" if successful.

Example 1: Django cluster setup script
import redis

# Connect to first node for cluster setup
r = redis.Redis(host='127.0.0.1', port=7000, decode_responses=True)

try:
    # Assign slots 0-5460 to first node
    slots = list(range(0, 5461))
    r.execute_command('CLUSTER', 'ADDSLOTS', *slots)
    print("Slots assigned successfully")
except Exception as e:
    print(f"Error: {e}")
Result: Slots assigned successfully
Example 2: Check which slots are assigned
import redis

r = redis.Redis(host='127.0.0.1', port=7000, decode_responses=True)

# Get cluster slot information
slots_info = r.execute_command('CLUSTER', 'SLOTS')
print(f"Cluster has {len(slots_info)} slot ranges")
for slot_range in slots_info:
    start, end = slot_range[0], slot_range[1] 
    print(f"Slots {start}-{end}: {slot_range[2][0]}:{slot_range[2][1]}")
Result: Cluster has 3 slot ranges, Slots 0-5460: 127.0.0.1:7000
Example 3: Django management command for cluster setup
from django.core.management.base import BaseCommand
import redis

class Command(BaseCommand):
    def handle(self, *args, **options):
        nodes = [
            ('127.0.0.1', 7000, range(0, 5461)),
            ('127.0.0.1', 7001, range(5461, 10923)), 
            ('127.0.0.1', 7002, range(10923, 16384))
        ]
        
        for host, port, slot_range in nodes:
            r = redis.Redis(host=host, port=port)
            r.execute_command('CLUSTER', 'ADDSLOTS', *slot_range)
            print(f"Node {host}:{port} assigned {len(slot_range)} slots")
Result: Node 127.0.0.1:7000 assigned 5461 slots
Example 4: Verify slot assignments for job keys
import redis
from rediscluster import RedisCluster

# Calculate which node stores job keys
rc = RedisCluster(startup_nodes=[{"host": "127.0.0.1", "port": "7000"}])

job_keys = ["job:1", "job:2", "job:3", "author:1", "skills:python"]
for key in job_keys:
    slot = rc.connection_pool.nodes.keyslot(key)
    node = rc.connection_pool.nodes.node_from_slot(slot)
    print(f"Key '{key}' -> Slot {slot} -> Node {node.host}:{node.port}")
Result: Key 'job:1' -> Slot 9842 -> Node 127.0.0.1:7001
Example 5: Monitor slot distribution balance
from collections import defaultdict
from jobpost.models import JobPost

# Check how Django model keys distribute across cluster
slot_distribution = defaultdict(int)
jobs = JobPost.objects.all()[:100]

for job in jobs:
    key = f"job:{job.id}"
    # Calculate slot (CRC16 hash)
    import crc16
    slot = crc16.crc16xmodem(key.encode()) % 16384
    slot_distribution[slot // 5461] += 1  # 3-node cluster

for node, count in slot_distribution.items():
    print(f"Node {node}: {count} keys")
Result: Node 0: 34 keys, Node 1: 33 keys, Node 2: 33 keys

CLUSTER INFO

Docs: Redis CLUSTER INFO Documentation

Returns information about the state of a node.

Full description

CLUSTER INFO provides comprehensive information about the current node's cluster state, including cluster status, slot assignments, node counts, and performance metrics. This is essential for monitoring cluster health in Django applications and implementing proper error handling.

Syntax

CLUSTER INFO

Parameters

No parameters required.

Return value

String: Multi-line string with cluster information including state, slots, nodes, and statistics.

Example 1: Check cluster health in Django
import redis

r = redis.Redis(host='127.0.0.1', port=7000, decode_responses=True)

cluster_info = r.execute_command('CLUSTER', 'INFO')
info_lines = cluster_info.strip().split('\n')

for line in info_lines[:5]:  # Show first 5 lines
    print(line)
Result: cluster_state:ok, cluster_slots_assigned:16384, cluster_slots_ok:16384
Example 2: Django health check middleware
from django.http import JsonResponse
import redis

def cluster_health_check(request):
    try:
        r = redis.Redis(host='127.0.0.1', port=7000, decode_responses=True)
        info = r.execute_command('CLUSTER', 'INFO')
        
        # Parse cluster state
        state = 'unknown'
        for line in info.split('\n'):
            if line.startswith('cluster_state:'):
                state = line.split(':')[1]
                break
        
        return JsonResponse({
            'cluster_healthy': state == 'ok',
            'state': state
        })
    except Exception as e:
        return JsonResponse({'error': str(e)}, status=500)

# Usage: GET /health/cluster/
Result: {'cluster_healthy': True, 'state': 'ok'}
Example 3: Monitor cluster metrics for job caching
import redis
import re

r = redis.Redis(host='127.0.0.1', port=7000, decode_responses=True)
info = r.execute_command('CLUSTER', 'INFO')

# Extract key metrics
metrics = {}
for line in info.split('\n'):
    if ':' in line:
        key, value = line.split(':', 1)
        metrics[key] = value

print(f"Cluster nodes: {metrics.get('cluster_known_nodes', 'unknown')}")
print(f"Assigned slots: {metrics.get('cluster_slots_assigned', 'unknown')}")
print(f"Cluster state: {metrics.get('cluster_state', 'unknown')}")
Result: Cluster nodes: 6, Assigned slots: 16384, Cluster state: ok
Example 4: Automated cluster monitoring
import redis
import logging

logger = logging.getLogger(__name__)

def monitor_cluster_health():
    try:
        r = redis.Redis(host='127.0.0.1', port=7000, decode_responses=True)
        info = r.execute_command('CLUSTER', 'INFO')
        
        # Check critical states
        if 'cluster_state:ok' not in info:
            logger.error("Cluster is not in OK state")
            return False
        
        if 'cluster_slots_assigned:16384' not in info:
            logger.warning("Not all slots assigned")
        
        logger.info("Cluster health check passed")
        return True
    except Exception as e:
        logger.error(f"Cluster health check failed: {e}")
        return False

is_healthy = monitor_cluster_health()
print(f"Cluster healthy: {is_healthy}")
Result: Cluster healthy: True
Example 5: Django cache backend cluster validation
from django.core.cache import cache
from django.core.exceptions import ImproperlyConfigured
import redis

def validate_cluster_cache():
    try:
        # Assuming Redis cluster cache backend
        connection = cache._cache.get_client()
        info = connection.execute_command('CLUSTER', 'INFO')
        
        # Validate cluster is ready for Django caching
        required_checks = [
            'cluster_state:ok',
            'cluster_slots_assigned:16384',
            'cluster_slots_ok:16384'
        ]
        
        for check in required_checks:
            if check not in info:
                raise ImproperlyConfigured(f"Cluster check failed: {check}")
        
        print("Django cache cluster validation passed")
        return True
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Validation failed: {e}")
        return False

validate_cluster_cache()
Result: Django cache cluster validation passed

Count-min Sketch Commands

Count-min Sketch (CMS) is a probabilistic data structure for estimating frequencies of events in data streams. It provides approximate counts with controlled error rates while using minimal memory. Perfect for Django applications tracking page views, API usage, user activities, or any scenario requiring frequency counting at scale.

📊 Count-min Sketch Commands Overview

INITBYDIM - Initialize sketch by dimensions
INITBYPROB - Initialize sketch by tolerance
INCRBY - Increment item counts
QUERY - Get frequency estimates
MERGE - Combine multiple sketches
INFO - Get sketch information
🎯 Perfect for Django: Count-min Sketch excels at tracking job view counts, monitoring API endpoint usage, counting skill frequency in job postings, or measuring user engagement patterns - all with bounded memory usage even for millions of events.

INITBYDIM

Docs: Redis CMS.INITBYDIM Documentation

Initializes a Count-Min Sketch to dimensions specified by user.

Full description

CMS.INITBYDIM creates a new Count-min Sketch with specified width and depth dimensions. Width controls memory usage and depth controls accuracy. Larger dimensions provide better accuracy but use more memory. This is ideal for precise control over the sketch's characteristics.

Syntax

CMS.INITBYDIM key width depth

Parameters

  • key: The Count-min Sketch key name.
  • width: The number of counters in each hash function (affects memory and collision probability).
  • depth: The number of hash functions (affects accuracy).

Return value

String: "OK" if successful.

Example 1: Initialize job view counter sketch
import redis

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Initialize CMS for tracking job views (width=1000, depth=5)
try:
    result = r.execute_command('CMS.INITBYDIM', 'job_views', 1000, 5)
    print(f"Job views sketch initialized: {result}")
except Exception as e:
    print(f"Error: {e}")
Result: Job views sketch initialized: OK
Example 2: Initialize skill frequency tracker
import redis
from jobpost.models import Skills

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Initialize CMS for tracking skill mentions in job postings
result = r.execute_command('CMS.INITBYDIM', 'skill_frequency', 2000, 7)

# Track skills from Django model
skills = Skills.objects.all()
print(f"Skill tracker initialized for {skills.count()} skills: {result}")
for skill in skills[:3]:
    print(f"Ready to track: {skill.name}")
Result: Skill tracker initialized for 5 skills: OK, Ready to track: Python
Example 3: Initialize API endpoint usage tracker
import redis

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Initialize CMS for tracking Django view usage
endpoints = ['/jobs/', '/jobs//', '/skills/', '/authors/']
result = r.execute_command('CMS.INITBYDIM', 'api_usage', 1500, 6)

print(f"API usage tracker initialized: {result}")
print(f"Ready to track {len(endpoints)} endpoints")
Result: API usage tracker initialized: OK, Ready to track 4 endpoints
Example 4: Initialize user activity tracker
import redis
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

# Initialize CMS for tracking user activities
result = r.execute_command('CMS.INITBYDIM', 'user_activity', 800, 4)

users_count = User.objects.count()
print(f"User activity tracker initialized: {result}")
print(f"Ready to track activities for {users_count} users")
Result: User activity tracker initialized: OK, Ready to track activities for 5 users
Example 5: Initialize company mention tracker
