Result: Company tracker initialized: OK, Companies to track: ['ABC Corp', 'XYZ Inc', '123 Industries']

INCRBY

Docs: Redis CMS.INCRBY Documentation

Increases the count of one or more items by increment.

Full description

CMS.INCRBY adds specified increments to the frequency count of one or more items in the Count-min Sketch. This is the primary command for tracking events, page views, user actions, or any countable activity in your Django application.

Syntax

CMS.INCRBY key item increment [item increment ...]

Parameters

  • key: The Count-min Sketch key name.
  • item: The item to increment (e.g., job ID, user ID, skill name).
  • increment: The amount to add to the item's count.

Return value

Array: List of updated counts for each item (approximate values).

Example 1: Track job post views
import redis
from jobpost.models import JobPost

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Initialize sketch first
r.execute_command('CMS.INITBYDIM', 'job_views', 1000, 5)

# Track views for job posts
jobs = JobPost.objects.all()[:3]
for job in jobs:
    count = r.execute_command('CMS.INCRBY', 'job_views', f'job:{job.id}', 1)
    print(f"Job {job.id} ({job.title}): {count[0]} views")
Result: Job 1 (Python Developer): 1 views, Job 2 (Frontend Developer): 1 views
Example 2: Track skill mentions in bulk
import redis
from jobpost.models import JobPost

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)
r.execute_command('CMS.INITBYDIM', 'skill_frequency', 2000, 7)

# Bulk increment skill counts from job skills
jobs = JobPost.objects.prefetch_related('skills').all()
skill_increments = []

for job in jobs[:2]:  # Process first 2 jobs
    for skill in job.skills.all():
        skill_increments.extend([skill.name, 1])

if skill_increments:
    counts = r.execute_command('CMS.INCRBY', 'skill_frequency', *skill_increments)
    print(f"Updated {len(counts)} skill frequencies")
Result: Updated 4 skill frequencies
Example 3: Track API endpoint usage
import redis

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)
r.execute_command('CMS.INITBYDIM', 'api_usage', 1500, 6)

# Simulate API usage tracking
endpoints = ['/jobs/', '/jobs/1/', '/skills/']
increments = []

for endpoint in endpoints:
    # Simulate different usage levels
    usage = 5 if endpoint == '/jobs/' else 1
    increments.extend([endpoint, usage])

counts = r.execute_command('CMS.INCRBY', 'api_usage', *increments)
for i, endpoint in enumerate(endpoints):
    print(f"{endpoint}: {counts[i]} requests")
Result: /jobs/: 5 requests, /jobs/1/: 1 requests, /skills/: 1 requests
Example 4: Track user activities
import redis
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)
r.execute_command('CMS.INITBYDIM', 'user_activity', 800, 4)

# Track multiple user activities
users = User.objects.all()[:3]
activities = ['login', 'view_job', 'apply']

for user in users:
    for activity in activities[:2]:  # Track 2 activities per user
        count = r.execute_command('CMS.INCRBY', 'user_activity', 
                                 f'{user.username}:{activity}', 1)
        print(f"{user.username} {activity}: {count[0]}")
Result: user1 login: 1, user1 view_job: 1, user2 login: 1
Example 5: Track company popularity
import redis
from jobpost.models import JobPost

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)
r.execute_command('CMS.INITBYDIM', 'company_popularity', 1000, 5)

# Track company mentions from job posts
jobs = JobPost.objects.select_related('author').all()
company_data = []

for job in jobs[:3]:
    if job.author and job.author.company:
        company_data.extend([job.author.company, 1])

if company_data:
    counts = r.execute_command('CMS.INCRBY', 'company_popularity', *company_data)
    companies = company_data[::2]  # Extract company names
    for i, company in enumerate(companies):
        print(f"{company}: {counts[i]} mentions")
Result: ABC Corp: 1 mentions, XYZ Inc: 1 mentions, 123 Industries: 1 mentions

QUERY

Docs: Redis CMS.QUERY Documentation

Returns the count for one or more items in a sketch.

Full description

CMS.QUERY retrieves the estimated frequency count for specified items from the Count-min Sketch. The returned values are approximate but guaranteed to never underestimate the true count. This is essential for reading analytics data in Django applications.

Syntax

CMS.QUERY key item [item ...]

Parameters

  • key: The Count-min Sketch key name.
  • item: The item(s) to query for frequency count.

Return value

Array: List of estimated counts for each queried item.

Example 1: Get job view counts for analytics
import redis
from jobpost.models import JobPost

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Setup data first
r.execute_command('CMS.INITBYDIM', 'job_views', 1000, 5)
jobs = JobPost.objects.all()[:3]

# Add some view counts
for i, job in enumerate(jobs, 1):
    r.execute_command('CMS.INCRBY', 'job_views', f'job:{job.id}', i * 2)

# Query view counts
job_keys = [f'job:{job.id}' for job in jobs]
counts = r.execute_command('CMS.QUERY', 'job_views', *job_keys)

for i, job in enumerate(jobs):
    print(f"{job.title}: {counts[i]} views")
Result: Python Developer: 2 views, Frontend Developer: 4 views, Full Stack Developer: 6 views
Example 2: Analyze skill popularity
import redis
from jobpost.models import Skills

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Setup skill frequency data
r.execute_command('CMS.INITBYDIM', 'skill_frequency', 2000, 7)
skills = Skills.objects.all()

# Add frequency data
skill_names = []
for skill in skills:
    # Simulate different frequencies
    freq = len(skill.name)  # Use name length as frequency
    r.execute_command('CMS.INCRBY', 'skill_frequency', skill.name, freq)
    skill_names.append(skill.name)

# Query frequencies
counts = r.execute_command('CMS.QUERY', 'skill_frequency', *skill_names)
for i, skill_name in enumerate(skill_names[:3]):
    print(f"{skill_name}: {counts[i]} mentions")
Result: Python: 6 mentions, JavaScript: 10 mentions, React: 5 mentions
Example 3: Check API endpoint usage stats
import redis

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Setup API usage tracking
r.execute_command('CMS.INITBYDIM', 'api_usage', 1500, 6)
endpoints = ['/jobs/', '/jobs/1/', '/skills/', '/authors/']

# Add usage data
for i, endpoint in enumerate(endpoints, 1):
    r.execute_command('CMS.INCRBY', 'api_usage', endpoint, i * 3)

# Query usage statistics
counts = r.execute_command('CMS.QUERY', 'api_usage', *endpoints)

print("API Endpoint Usage Report:")
for i, endpoint in enumerate(endpoints):
    print(f"{endpoint}: {counts[i]} requests")
Result: API Endpoint Usage Report:, /jobs/: 3 requests, /jobs/1/: 6 requests
Example 4: User activity analytics
import redis
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Setup user activity tracking  
r.execute_command('CMS.INITBYDIM', 'user_activity', 800, 4)
users = User.objects.all()[:2]
activities = ['login', 'view_job', 'apply']

# Add activity data
for user in users:
    for i, activity in enumerate(activities, 1):
        r.execute_command('CMS.INCRBY', 'user_activity', 
                         f'{user.username}:{activity}', i)

# Query user activity
query_keys = []
for user in users:
    for activity in activities[:2]:  # Query first 2 activities
        query_keys.append(f'{user.username}:{activity}')

counts = r.execute_command('CMS.QUERY', 'user_activity', *query_keys)
print(f"Activity counts: {counts[:4]}")
Result: Activity counts: [1, 2, 1, 2]
Example 5: Compare company popularity
import redis
from jobpost.models import Author

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Setup company tracking
r.execute_command('CMS.INITBYDIM', 'company_mentions', 1000, 5)
companies = Author.objects.values_list('company', flat=True).distinct()

# Add company data with different weights
for i, company in enumerate(companies, 1):
    r.execute_command('CMS.INCRBY', 'company_mentions', company, i * 2)

# Query company popularity
counts = r.execute_command('CMS.QUERY', 'company_mentions', *companies)

print("Company Popularity Ranking:")
for i, company in enumerate(companies[:3]):
    print(f"{i+1}. {company}: {counts[i]} mentions")
Result: Company Popularity Ranking:, 1. ABC Corp: 2 mentions, 2. XYZ Inc: 4 mentions

Connection Management Commands

Redis connection management commands control client connections, authentication, and communication settings. These commands are essential for Django applications to maintain secure, efficient connections to Redis servers, handle authentication, manage connection pooling, and control client behavior for optimal performance.

🔌 Connection Management Commands Overview

AUTH - Authenticate connection
CLIENT CACHING - Control key tracking
CLIENT ID - Get unique client ID
CLIENT INFO - Get connection info
CLIENT LIST - List all connections
CLIENT SETNAME - Set connection name
HELLO - Handshake with server
PING - Test connection liveliness
SELECT - Switch database
RESET - Reset connection state
🎯 Perfect for Django: Connection management commands enable Django applications to maintain secure Redis connections, implement connection pooling, monitor client sessions, and troubleshoot connectivity issues in production environments.

AUTH

Docs: Redis AUTH Documentation

Authenticates the connection.

Full description

AUTH authenticates the current connection using a password or username/password combination. This is essential for secure Redis deployments where authentication is required. Django applications should use AUTH to establish authenticated connections before executing any commands on secured Redis instances.

Syntax

AUTH [username] password

Parameters

  • username: Optional username for Redis 6+ ACL authentication.
  • password: The password for authentication.

Return value

String: "OK" if authentication successful.

Example 1: Basic password authentication
import redis

# Connect with password authentication
try:
    r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, password='mypassword', decode_responses=True)
    
    # Test authentication by pinging
    result = r.ping()
    print(f"Authentication successful: {result}")
    
except redis.exceptions.AuthenticationError as e:
    print(f"Authentication failed: {e}")
Result: Authentication successful: True
Example 2: Django cache with Redis authentication
from django.core.cache import cache
from jobpost.models import JobPost

# Django settings would have:
# CACHES = {
#     'default': {
#         'BACKEND': 'django_redis.cache.RedisCache',
#         'LOCATION': 'redis://localhost:6379/1',
#         'OPTIONS': {'PASSWORD': 'mypassword'}
#     }
# }

try:
    job = JobPost.objects.first()
    cache_key = f"job:{job.id}"
    cache.set(cache_key, job.title, timeout=300)
    
    cached_title = cache.get(cache_key)
    print(f"Cached job title: {cached_title}")
    
except Exception as e:
    print(f"Cache operation failed: {e}")
Result: Cached job title: Python Developer
Example 3: Manual AUTH command
import redis

# Manual authentication using AUTH command
r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

try:
    # Authenticate manually
    auth_result = r.execute_command('AUTH', 'mypassword')
    print(f"Manual auth: {auth_result}")
    
    # Now can execute commands
    r.set('test_key', 'authenticated')
    value = r.get('test_key')
    print(f"Retrieved: {value}")
    
except Exception as e:
    print(f"Authentication error: {e}")
Result: Manual auth: OK, Retrieved: authenticated
Example 4: Username/password authentication (Redis 6+)
import redis

# Redis 6+ ACL authentication
try:
    r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, username='django_user', password='django_pass', decode_responses=True)
    
    # Test with job data
    from jobpost.models import Skills
    skills = Skills.objects.all()
    
    for skill in skills[:2]:
        key = f"skill:{skill.id}"
        r.set(key, skill.name)
        print(f"Stored skill: {skill.name}")
        
except Exception as e:
    print(f"ACL authentication failed: {e}")
Result: Stored skill: Python, Stored skill: JavaScript
Example 5: Connection pool with authentication
import redis
from django.conf import settings

# Create authenticated connection pool
def get_redis_connection():
    pool = redis.ConnectionPool(
        host='localhost',
        port=6379,
        password='mypassword',
        max_connections=20,
        decode_responses=True
    )
    return redis.Redis(connection_pool=pool)

# Use in Django view
def cache_user_activity(user_id, activity):
    r = get_redis_connection()
    
    key = f"user:{user_id}:activity"
    r.set(key, activity, ex=3600)  # Expire in 1 hour
    
    print(f"Cached activity for user {user_id}: {activity}")

# Test the function
cache_user_activity(1, 'viewed_job_posting')
Result: Cached activity for user 1: viewed_job_posting

PING

Docs: Redis PING Documentation

Returns the server's liveliness response.

Full description

PING tests the connection to the Redis server and returns a response to confirm the server is alive and responding. This is commonly used in Django applications for health checks, connection validation, and monitoring Redis server availability.

Syntax

PING [message]

Parameters

  • message: Optional message to echo back (Redis 2.8+).

Return value

String: "PONG" if no message provided, or the message if provided.

Example 1: Basic connection test
import redis

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

# Simple ping test
try:
    result = r.ping()
    print(f"Redis connection: {'OK' if result else 'FAILED'}")
    
except redis.exceptions.ConnectionError as e:
    print(f"Connection failed: {e}")
Result: Redis connection: OK
Example 2: Django health check view
from django.http import JsonResponse
from django.core.cache import cache
import redis

def health_check(request):
    """Health check endpoint for Django app"""
    try:
        # Test Redis connection
        connection = cache._cache.get_client()
        ping_result = connection.ping()
        
        # Test basic operations
        cache.set('health_check', 'ok', timeout=10)
        cache_test = cache.get('health_check')
        
        return JsonResponse({
            'redis_ping': ping_result,
            'cache_test': cache_test == 'ok',
            'status': 'healthy'
        })
        
    except Exception as e:
        return JsonResponse({
            'error': str(e),
            'status': 'unhealthy'
        }, status=500)

# Usage: GET /health/
Result: {'redis_ping': True, 'cache_test': True, 'status': 'healthy'}
Example 3: Ping with custom message
import redis

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

# Ping with custom message
messages = ['Django-App', 'JobPost-Service', 'Cache-Check']

for msg in messages:
    response = r.execute_command('PING', msg)
    print(f"Ping '{msg}': {response}")
Result: Ping 'Django-App': Django-App, Ping 'JobPost-Service': JobPost-Service
Example 4: Connection monitoring in Django management command
from django.core.management.base import BaseCommand
import redis
import time

class Command(BaseCommand):
    help = 'Monitor Redis connection status'
    
    def handle(self, *args, **options):
        r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)
        
        for i in range(5):  # Check 5 times
            try:
                start_time = time.time()
                result = r.ping()
                latency = (time.time() - start_time) * 1000
                
                self.stdout.write(
                    f"Ping {i+1}: {'OK' if result else 'FAIL'} "
                    f"(latency: {latency:.2f}ms)"
                )
                time.sleep(1)
                
            except Exception as e:
                self.stdout.write(f"Ping {i+1}: FAILED - {e}")

# Run with: python manage.py monitor_redis
Result: Ping 1: OK (latency: 0.15ms), Ping 2: OK (latency: 0.12ms)
Example 5: Redis sentinel monitoring
import redis
from redis.sentinel import Sentinel

def check_redis_cluster_health():
    """Check Redis Sentinel cluster health"""
    sentinel = Sentinel([('localhost', 26379)], socket_timeout=0.1)
    
    try:
        # Get master connection
        master = sentinel.master_for('mymaster', socket_timeout=0.1)
        
        # Ping master
        master_ping = master.ping()
        print(f"Master ping: {'OK' if master_ping else 'FAILED'}")
        
        # Get slave connection  
        slave = sentinel.slave_for('mymaster', socket_timeout=0.1)
        slave_ping = slave.ping()
        print(f"Slave ping: {'OK' if slave_ping else 'FAILED'}")
        
        return master_ping and slave_ping
        
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Cluster health check failed: {e}")
        return False

health_status = check_redis_cluster_health()
print(f"Cluster healthy: {health_status}")
Result: Master ping: OK, Slave ping: OK, Cluster healthy: True

SELECT

Docs: Redis SELECT Documentation

Changes the selected database.

Full description

SELECT changes the current database for the connection. Redis has 16 databases (0-15) by default. This is useful in Django applications for separating different types of data (cache, sessions, queues) or different environments (development, testing, staging) on the same Redis instance.

Syntax

SELECT index

Parameters

  • index: Database index (0-15 by default).

Return value

String: "OK" if successful.

Example 1: Switch between databases
import redis

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

# Store job data in database 0 (default)
from jobpost.models import JobPost
job = JobPost.objects.first()

r.set(f"job:{job.id}", job.title)
print(f"DB 0 - Stored: {job.title}")

# Switch to database 1 for cache
r.execute_command('SELECT', 1)
r.set('cache:job_count', JobPost.objects.count())
print(f"DB 1 - Cached job count: {r.get('cache:job_count')}")

# Switch back to database 0
r.execute_command('SELECT', 0)
retrieved = r.get(f"job:{job.id}")
print(f"DB 0 - Retrieved: {retrieved}")
Result: DB 0 - Stored: Python Developer, DB 1 - Cached job count: 5, DB 0 - Retrieved: Python Developer
Example 2: Django settings with multiple databases
# In Django settings.py
CACHES = {
    'default': {  # Database 0 for general cache
        'BACKEND': 'django_redis.cache.RedisCache',
        'LOCATION': 'redis://127.0.0.1:6379/0',
    },
    'sessions': {  # Database 1 for sessions
        'BACKEND': 'django_redis.cache.RedisCache', 
        'LOCATION': 'redis://127.0.0.1:6379/1',
    },
    'queue': {  # Database 2 for task queue
        'BACKEND': 'django_redis.cache.RedisCache',
        'LOCATION': 'redis://127.0.0.1:6379/2',
    }
}

# Usage in views
from django.core.cache import caches

def use_multiple_databases(request):
    default_cache = caches['default']
    session_cache = caches['sessions'] 
    queue_cache = caches['queue']
    
    # Store in different databases
    default_cache.set('job_data', 'Python Developer')
    session_cache.set('user_session', 'active')
    queue_cache.set('pending_tasks', 5)
    
    print("Data stored across multiple Redis databases")
Result: Data stored across multiple Redis databases
Example 3: Environment-based database selection
import redis
import os

def get_redis_db():
    """Get Redis database based on environment"""
    env = os.getenv('DJANGO_ENV', 'development')
    
    db_mapping = {
        'development': 0,
        'testing': 1, 
        'staging': 2,
        'production': 0  # Production uses dedicated instance
    }
    
    return db_mapping.get(env, 0)

# Connect to environment-specific database
r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=get_redis_db(), decode_responses=True)

# Store environment-specific data
from jobpost.models import Skills
skills = Skills.objects.all()

for skill in skills[:3]:
    key = f"skill:{skill.id}"
    r.set(key, f"{skill.name}-{os.getenv('DJANGO_ENV', 'dev')}")
    print(f"Stored in DB {get_redis_db()}: {key}")
Result: Stored in DB 0: skill:1, Stored in DB 0: skill:2, Stored in DB 0: skill:3
Example 4: Database isolation for testing
import redis
from django.test import TestCase

class RedisTestCase(TestCase):
    def setUp(self):
        # Use database 15 for tests to avoid conflicts
        self.redis = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=15, decode_responses=True)
        self.redis.flushdb()  # Clean test database
        
    def test_job_caching(self):
        from jobpost.models import JobPost
        
        # Test job caching in isolated database
        job = JobPost.objects.first()
        cache_key = f"test:job:{job.id}"
        
        # Store test data
        self.redis.set(cache_key, job.title, ex=300)
        
        # Verify storage
        cached_title = self.redis.get(cache_key)
        self.assertEqual(cached_title, job.title)
        print(f"Test DB 15 - Cached: {cached_title}")
        
    def tearDown(self):
        # Clean up test database
        self.redis.flushdb()
Result: Test DB 15 - Cached: Python Developer
Example 5: Database monitoring and stats
import redis

def monitor_database_usage():
    """Monitor usage across multiple Redis databases"""
    r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)
    
    for db_num in range(3):  # Check databases 0, 1, 2
        r.execute_command('SELECT', db_num)
        
        # Get database info
        info = r.info('keyspace')
        db_key = f'db{db_num}'
        
        if db_key in info:
            keys = info[db_key]['keys']
            expires = info[db_key]['expires']
            print(f"DB {db_num}: {keys} keys, {expires} with expiration")
        else:
            print(f"DB {db_num}: empty")
    
    # Switch back to default database
    r.execute_command('SELECT', 0)

# Monitor database usage
monitor_database_usage()
Result: DB 0: 15 keys, 5 with expiration, DB 1: 8 keys, 2 with expiration, DB 2: empty

Geospatial Indices Commands

Redis Geospatial Indices provide efficient storage and querying of location-based data using longitude/latitude coordinates. These commands enable Django applications to build location-aware features like proximity searches, distance calculations, and geographic filtering - perfect for job boards with location-based job matching, delivery services, social networks, and any application requiring spatial queries.

🌍 Geospatial Indices Commands Overview

GEOADD - Add locations with coordinates
GEODIST - Calculate distance between locations
GEORADIUS - Find locations within radius
GEOSEARCH - Advanced spatial queries
GEOPOS - Get coordinates of locations
GEOHASH - Get geohash representation
GEORADIUSBYMEMBER - Radius search from member
GEOSEARCHSTORE - Store search results
🎯 Perfect for Django: Geospatial commands enable Django applications to implement location-based job searches, calculate commute distances, find nearby offices, and build sophisticated geographic filtering - all with high performance spatial indexing.

GEOADD

Docs: Redis GEOADD Documentation

Adds one or more members to a geospatial index. The key is created if it doesn't exist.

Full description

GEOADD adds geospatial items (longitude, latitude, member name) to a Redis key. This creates a sorted set with special geospatial capabilities. Essential for Django applications that need to store and query location data efficiently, such as job locations, office addresses, user positions, or delivery points.

Syntax

GEOADD key longitude latitude member [longitude latitude member ...]

Parameters

  • key: The geospatial index key name.
  • longitude: Longitude coordinate (-180 to 180).
  • latitude: Latitude coordinate (-85.05112878 to 85.05112878).
  • member: The location identifier/name.

Return value

Integer: Number of elements added to the sorted set (not including elements already existing).

Example 1: Add job locations to geospatial index
import redis
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Location

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

# Add job locations to geospatial index
jobs = JobPost.objects.select_related('location').all()

for job in jobs[:3]:
    if job.location:
        # Using city coordinates (simplified)
        coords = {
            'New York': (-74.006, 40.7128),
            'Los Angeles': (-118.2437, 34.0522),  
            'Chicago': (-87.6298, 41.8781)
        }
        
        if job.location.city in coords:
            lon, lat = coords[job.location.city]
            result = r.execute_command('GEOADD', 'job_locations', lon, lat, f'job:{job.id}')
            print(f"Added job {job.id} in {job.location.city}: {result}")
Result: Added job 1 in New York: 1, Added job 2 in Los Angeles: 1, Added job 3 in Chicago: 1
Example 2: Add company office locations
import redis
from jobpost.models import Author

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

# Add company offices with real coordinates
office_locations = [
    (-122.4194, 37.7749, 'office:abc_corp:sf'),      # San Francisco
    (-87.6298, 41.8781, 'office:xyz_inc:chicago'),   # Chicago  
    (-118.2437, 34.0522, 'office:123_ind:la')        # Los Angeles
]

# Batch add office locations

for lon, lat, office_id in office_locations:
    coords.extend([lon, lat, office_id])

result = r.execute_command('GEOADD', 'office_locations', *coords)
print(f"Added {result} office locations")

# Verify with company data
companies = Author.objects.values_list('company', flat=True).distinct()
print(f"Companies: {list(companies)[:3]}")
Result: Added 3 office locations, Companies: ['ABC Corp', 'XYZ Inc', '123 Industries']

    {'id': 2, 'name': 'Mall Store', 'lon': -118.2437, 'lat': 34.0522},

GEODIST

Docs: Redis GEODIST Documentation

Returns the distance between two members of a geospatial index.

Full description

GEODIST calculates the distance between two members in a geospatial index. The distance is calculated using the Haversine formula and can be returned in different units (meters, kilometers, miles, feet). Perfect for Django applications calculating commute distances, delivery ranges, or proximity between locations.

Syntax

GEODIST key member1 member2 [unit]

Parameters

  • key: The geospatial index key name.
  • member1: First location member.
  • member2: Second location member.
  • unit: Distance unit - m (meters), km (kilometers), mi (miles), ft (feet). Default: meters.

Return value

String: Distance between the two members, or null if one of the members is missing.

Example 1: Calculate distance between job locations
import redis

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

# First add some job locations (from previous example)
r.execute_command('GEOADD', 'job_locations', 
                 -74.006, 40.7128, 'job:1',      # New York
                 -118.2437, 34.0522, 'job:2',    # Los Angeles  
                 -87.6298, 41.8781, 'job:3')     # Chicago

# Calculate distances between jobs
distances = [
    ('job:1', 'job:2', 'mi'),  # NY to LA in miles
    ('job:1', 'job:3', 'km'),  # NY to Chicago in km
    ('job:2', 'job:3', 'mi')   # LA to Chicago in miles
]

for job1, job2, unit in distances:
    distance = r.execute_command('GEODIST', 'job_locations', job1, job2, unit)
    if distance:
        print(f"Distance {job1} to {job2}: {float(distance):.1f} {unit}")
Result: Distance job:1 to job:2: 2445.6 mi, Distance job:1 to job:3: 1144.8 km
Example 2: Django view for commute distance calculation
from django.http import JsonResponse
import redis

def calculate_commute_distance(request, job_id):
    """Calculate distance from user location to job location"""
    r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)
    
    # Get user location (from session or profile)
    user_location = request.session.get('user_location', 'user:1')
    job_location = f'job:{job_id}'
    
    try:
        # Calculate distance in miles
        distance = r.execute_command('GEODIST', 'locations', user_location, job_location, 'mi')
        
        if distance:
            distance_miles = float(distance)
            return JsonResponse({
                'job_id': job_id,
                'distance_miles': round(distance_miles, 1),
                'commute_time': round(distance_miles / 30 * 60, 0)  # Assume 30mph avg
            })
        else:
            return JsonResponse({'error': 'Location not found'}, status=404)
            
    except Exception as e:
        return JsonResponse({'error': str(e)}, status=500)

# Usage: GET /jobs/1/distance/
Result: {'job_id': '1', 'distance_miles': 15.2, 'commute_time': 30}
Example 3: Delivery range validation
import redis

def check_delivery_feasible(store_id, customer_location):
    """Check if delivery is within range"""
    r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)
    
    # Add sample data
    r.execute_command('GEOADD', 'delivery_network',
                     -74.006, 40.7128, f'store:{store_id}',
                     -74.0200, 40.7200, customer_location)
    
    # Calculate distance
    distance = r.execute_command('GEODIST', 'delivery_network', 
                               f'store:{store_id}', customer_location, 'km')
    
    if distance:
        distance_km = float(distance)
        max_delivery_range = 15  # 15km max delivery range
        
        feasible = distance_km <= max_delivery_range
        print(f"Distance: {distance_km:.1f}km, Feasible: {feasible}")
        
        return {
            'feasible': feasible,
            'distance_km': distance_km,
            'max_range': max_delivery_range
        }
    
    return {'error': 'Could not calculate distance'}

# Test delivery feasibility
result = check_delivery_feasible(1, 'customer:123')
print(f"Delivery feasible: {result.get('feasible')}")
Result: Distance: 2.1km, Feasible: True, Delivery feasible: True

Example 5: Distance-based job recommendations
import redis
from jobpost.models import JobPost

def get_nearby_job_distances(user_location, max_distance_miles=25):
    """Get distances to nearby jobs within max range"""
    r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)
    
    # Add user location
    r.execute_command('GEOADD', 'job_search', -74.006, 40.7128, user_location)
    
    # Add sample job locations
    job_locations = [
        (-74.0200, 40.7200, 'job:1'),
        (-73.9850, 40.7580, 'job:2'), 
        (-74.1000, 40.6500, 'job:3')
    ]
    
    coords = []
    for lon, lat, job in job_locations:
        coords.extend([lon, lat, job])
    r.execute_command('GEOADD', 'job_search', *coords)
    
    # Calculate distances to each job
    nearby_jobs = []
    jobs = JobPost.objects.all()[:3]
    
    for i, job in enumerate(jobs, 1):
        distance = r.execute_command('GEODIST', 'job_search', user_location, f'job:{i}', 'mi')
        if distance:
            dist_miles = float(distance)
            if dist_miles <= max_distance_miles:
                nearby_jobs.append({
                    'job_id': job.id,
                    'title': job.title,
                    'distance_miles': round(dist_miles, 1)
                })
    
    return nearby_jobs

# Find jobs within 25 miles
nearby = get_nearby_job_distances('user:current', 25)
for job in nearby[:2]:
    print(f"Job: {job['title']}, Distance: {job['distance_miles']} miles")
Result: Job: Python Developer, Distance: 1.5 miles, Job: Frontend Developer, Distance: 4.2 miles

GEORADIUS

Docs: Redis GEORADIUS Documentation

Queries a geospatial index for members within a distance from a coordinate, optionally stores the result.

Full description

GEORADIUS returns members from a geospatial index that are within a specified radius of given coordinates. This is essential for Django applications implementing proximity searches, location-based filtering, and "find nearby" functionality for jobs, stores, or services.

Syntax

GEORADIUS key longitude latitude radius unit [WITHCOORD] [WITHDIST] [WITHHASH] [COUNT count] [ASC|DESC] [STORE key] [STOREDIST key]

Parameters

  • key: The geospatial index key name.
  • longitude: Center longitude coordinate.
  • latitude: Center latitude coordinate.
  • radius: Search radius value.
  • unit: Radius unit (m, km, mi, ft).
  • WITHCOORD: Include coordinates in results.
  • WITHDIST: Include distances in results.
  • COUNT: Limit number of results.

Return value

Array: List of members within the specified radius, with optional coordinates and distances.

Example 1: Find jobs within radius
import redis
from jobpost.models import JobPost

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

# Setup job locations
job_locations = [
    (-74.006, 40.7128, 'job:1'),    # New York
    (-74.0200, 40.7200, 'job:2'),   # Close to NY  
    (-118.2437, 34.0522, 'job:3'),  # Los Angeles (far)
]

coords = []
for lon, lat, job_id in job_locations:
    coords.extend([lon, lat, job_id])
r.execute_command('GEOADD', 'job_locations', *coords)

# Find jobs within 10 miles of Manhattan
manhattan_lon, manhattan_lat = -74.006, 40.7128
nearby_jobs = r.execute_command('GEORADIUS', 'job_locations', 
                              manhattan_lon, manhattan_lat, 10, 'mi',
                              'WITHDIST', 'COUNT', 5)

print("Jobs within 10 miles of Manhattan:")
for i in range(0, len(nearby_jobs), 2):
    job_id = nearby_jobs[i]
    distance = nearby_jobs[i+1] if i+1 < len(nearby_jobs) else 'N/A'
    print(f"  {job_id}: {distance} miles")
Result: Jobs within 10 miles of Manhattan:, job:1: 0.0000 miles, job:2: 1.5000 miles
Example 2: Django location-based job search view
from django.http import JsonResponse
from jobpost.models import JobPost
import redis

def search_nearby_jobs(request):
    """API endpoint for location-based job search"""
    r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)
    
    # Get search parameters
    lat = float(request.GET.get('lat', 40.7128))
    lon = float(request.GET.get('lon', -74.006))
    radius = int(request.GET.get('radius', 25))  # miles
    limit = int(request.GET.get('limit', 10))
    
    try:
        # Search for nearby jobs
        results = r.execute_command('GEORADIUS', 'job_locations',
                                  lon, lat, radius, 'mi',
                                  'WITHDIST', 'COUNT', limit, 'ASC')
        
        # Format results
        nearby_jobs = []
        for i in range(0, len(results), 2):
            if i+1 < len(results):
                job_key = results[i]
                distance = float(results[i+1])
                
                # Extract job ID from key
                job_id = job_key.split(':')[1] if ':' in job_key else job_key
                
                try:
                    job = JobPost.objects.get(id=job_id)
                    nearby_jobs.append({
                        'id': job.id,
                        'title': job.title,
                        'distance_miles': round(distance, 1)
                    })
                except JobPost.DoesNotExist:
                    continue
        
        return JsonResponse({
            'jobs': nearby_jobs,
            'search_center': {'lat': lat, 'lon': lon},
            'radius_miles': radius
        })
        
    except Exception as e:
        return JsonResponse({'error': str(e)}, status=500)

# Usage: GET /jobs/nearby/?lat=40.7128&lon=-74.006&radius=25
Result: {'jobs': [{'id': 1, 'title': 'Python Developer', 'distance_miles': 0.0}], 'radius_miles': 25}

Example 4: Store locator with sorting
import redis

def find_nearest_stores(customer_lat, customer_lon, max_distance=20):
    """Find stores within distance, sorted by proximity"""
    r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)
    
    # Add sample store locations
    stores = [
        (-74.0059, 40.7128, 'store:downtown'),
        (-74.0200, 40.7300, 'store:uptown'),
        (-73.9850, 40.7000, 'store:brooklyn'),
        (-74.1500, 40.6500, 'store:jersey')  # Farther away
    ]
    
    coords = []
    for lon, lat, store in stores:
        coords.extend([lon, lat, store])
    r.execute_command('GEOADD', 'store_network', *coords)
    
    # Find nearest stores (sorted ascending by distance)
    nearest = r.execute_command('GEORADIUS', 'store_network',
                              customer_lon, customer_lat, max_distance, 'mi',
                              'WITHDIST', 'ASC')
    
    store_list = []
    for i in range(0, len(nearest), 2):
        if i+1 < len(nearest):
            store_name = nearest[i]
            distance = float(nearest[i+1])
            store_list.append({
                'name': store_name.replace('store:', '').title(),
                'distance_miles': round(distance, 1)
            })
    
    return store_list

# Find stores near customer location
customer_location = (40.7200, -74.0100)  # Customer coordinates
nearby_stores = find_nearest_stores(customer_location[0], customer_location[1])

print("Nearest stores:")
for store in nearby_stores[:3]:
    print(f"  {store['name']}: {store['distance_miles']} miles")
Result: Nearest stores:, Downtown: 0.8 miles, Uptown: 0.6 miles, Brooklyn: 1.2 miles
Example 5: Emergency services radius search
import redis

def find_emergency_services(incident_lat, incident_lon, service_type='hospital'):
    """Find emergency services within critical radius"""
    r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)
    
    # Add emergency service locations
    services = {
        'hospital': [
            (-74.006, 40.7128, 'hospital:nyc_general'),
            (-74.0200, 40.7300, 'hospital:mount_sinai'),
            (-73.9850, 40.7000, 'hospital:brooklyn_medical')
        ],
        'fire_station': [
            (-74.0100, 40.7150, 'fire:station_1'),
            (-74.0250, 40.7250, 'fire:station_2')
        ]
    }
    
    if service_type in services:
        coords = []
        for lon, lat, service_id in services[service_type]:
            coords.extend([lon, lat, service_id])
        r.execute_command('GEOADD', f'{service_type}_network', *coords)
        
        # Find services within 5 miles (critical radius)
        nearby = r.execute_command('GEORADIUS', f'{service_type}_network',
                                 incident_lon, incident_lat, 5, 'mi',
                                 'WITHDIST', 'COUNT', 3, 'ASC')
        
        emergency_services = []
        for i in range(0, len(nearby), 2):
            if i+1 < len(nearby):
                service_id = nearby[i]
                distance = float(nearby[i+1])
                emergency_services.append({
                    'service': service_id,
                    'distance_miles': round(distance, 1),
                    'eta_minutes': round(distance * 2, 0)  # Estimate 2 min/mile
                })
        
        return emergency_services
    
    return []

# Emergency incident at coordinates
incident_coords = (40.7200, -74.0050)
hospitals = find_emergency_services(incident_coords[0], incident_coords[1], 'hospital')

print("Nearest hospitals:")
for hospital in hospitals[:2]:
    print(f"  {hospital['service']}: {hospital['distance_miles']} mi (ETA: {hospital['eta_minutes']} min)")
Result: Nearest hospitals:, hospital:nyc_general: 0.8 mi (ETA: 2 min)

JSON Commands

Redis JSON module provides native JSON document storage and manipulation capabilities, enabling powerful document-based operations directly within Redis. Perfect for Django applications that need to store, query, and manipulate complex JSON data structures like user profiles, job metadata, search filters, or any schema-flexible data without requiring a separate document database.

🗂️ JSON Commands Overview

JSON.SET - Set JSON document
JSON.GET - Get JSON values
JSON.MGET - Get multiple JSON values
JSON.MSET - Set multiple JSON values
JSON.DEL - Delete JSON paths
JSON.FORGET - Alias for JSON.DEL
JSON.TYPE - Get value type
JSON.CLEAR - Clear arrays/objects
JSON.MERGE - Merge JSON values
JSON.ARRAPPEND - Append to arrays
JSON.ARRINDEX - Find array index
JSON.ARRINSERT - Insert into arrays
JSON.ARRLEN - Get array length
JSON.ARRPOP - Pop from arrays
JSON.ARRTRIM - Trim arrays
JSON.OBJKEYS - Get object keys
JSON.OBJLEN - Get object length
JSON.STRAPPEND - Append to strings
JSON.STRLEN - Get string length
JSON.NUMINCRBY - Increment numbers
JSON.NUMMULTBY - Multiply numbers
JSON.TOGGLE - Toggle booleans
JSON.DEBUG - Debug commands
JSON.RESP - Get RESP format
🎯 Perfect for Django: JSON commands excel at storing user profiles, job filters, search preferences, metadata, or any flexible schema data. Store complete Django model instances as JSON for caching, maintain complex configuration objects, or implement document-based features alongside your relational database.

JSON.SET

Docs: Redis JSON.SET Documentation

Sets or updates the JSON value at a path.

Full description

JSON.SET stores a JSON document at a specified key and path. If the path doesn't exist, it creates it. If it exists, it updates the value. This is perfect for storing Django model data as JSON documents, caching complex objects, or maintaining flexible schemas. Supports JSONPath expressions for precise targeting within nested structures.

Syntax

JSON.SET key path value [NX|XX]

Parameters

  • key: The Redis key to store the JSON document
  • path: JSONPath to the location within the document (use $ for root)
  • value: The JSON value to set (must be valid JSON)
  • NX: Only set if path doesn't exist
  • XX: Only set if path already exists

Return value

String: "OK" on success, null if conditions (NX/XX) not met.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Store user profile as JSON document
import json
import redis

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

# Create user profile document
user_profile = {
    "user_id": 1,
    "username": "john_dev",
    "email": "john@example.com",
    "preferences": {
        "job_alerts": True,
        "remote_only": False,
        "salary_min": 80000
    },
    "skills": ["Python", "Django", "React"],
    "experience_years": 5
}

result = r.execute_command("JSON.SET", "user:profile:1", "$", json.dumps(user_profile))
print(f"User profile stored: {result}")
User profile stored: OK
Example 2: Update nested preference
# Update just the salary preference
result = r.execute_command("JSON.SET", "user:profile:1", "$.preferences.salary_min", "90000")
print(f"Salary preference updated: {result}")

# Verify the update
updated_salary = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "user:profile:1", "$.preferences.salary_min")
print(f"New salary preference: {updated_salary}")
Salary preference updated: OK New salary preference: [90000]
Example 3: Store job post metadata
# Store complex job metadata
job_metadata = {
    "job_id": 42,
    "analytics": {
        "views": 0,
        "applications": 0,
        "saves": 0
    },
    "seo_data": {
        "title_variations": ["Python Developer", "Django Developer", "Backend Python Developer"],
        "keywords": ["python", "django", "backend", "api"]
    },
    "flags": {
        "featured": False,
        "urgent": False,
        "remote_friendly": True
    }
}

result = r.execute_command("JSON.SET", "job:meta:42", "$", json.dumps(job_metadata))
print(f"Job metadata stored: {result}")
Job metadata stored: OK
Example 4: Conditional set with NX (only if not exists)
# Try to set user preferences only if they don't exist
new_user_prefs = {
    "theme": "dark",
    "notifications": True,
    "language": "en"
}

# This will fail if preferences already exist
result1 = r.execute_command("JSON.SET", "user:profile:1", "$.preferences", 
                           json.dumps(new_user_prefs), "NX")

# Set preferences for a new user
result2 = r.execute_command("JSON.SET", "user:profile:99", "$.preferences", 
                           json.dumps(new_user_prefs), "NX")

print(f"Existing user preferences: {result1}")
print(f"New user preferences: {result2}")
Existing user preferences: None New user preferences: OK
Example 5: Store search filters as JSON
# Store complex search filters
search_filters = {
    "location": {
        "city": "New York",
        "remote": True,
        "radius_miles": 25
    },
    "salary": {
        "min": 75000,
        "max": 120000,
        "currency": "USD"
    },
    "requirements": {
        "experience_years": {"min": 2, "max": 8},
        "education": ["Bachelor", "Master"],
        "skills": ["Python", "Django", "PostgreSQL"]
    },
    "filters": {
        "job_type": ["full-time", "contract"],
        "company_size": ["startup", "mid-size"]
    }
}

result = r.execute_command("JSON.SET", "search:filters:user123", "$", json.dumps(search_filters))
print(f"Search filters stored: {result}")

# Quick verification
location = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "search:filters:user123", "$.location.city")
print(f"Search location: {location}")
Search filters stored: OK Search location: ["New York"]

JSON.GET

Docs: Redis JSON.GET Documentation

Gets the value at one or more paths in JSON serialized form.

Full description

JSON.GET retrieves JSON data from specified paths within a stored document. You can fetch the entire document, specific fields, or multiple paths simultaneously. Perfect for loading Django model data from JSON cache, retrieving user preferences, or getting specific portions of complex documents without transferring unnecessary data.

Syntax

JSON.GET key [INDENT indent] [NEWLINE newline] [SPACE space] [path ...]

Parameters

  • key: The Redis key containing the JSON document
  • path: One or more JSONPath expressions (default: $ for root)
  • INDENT: String to indent with (for pretty printing)
  • NEWLINE: String for newlines (for pretty printing)
  • SPACE: String for spaces (for pretty printing)

Return value

String: JSON representation of the values at specified paths.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Get complete user profile
# Retrieve entire user profile
profile_json = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "user:profile:1")
print(f"Full profile: {profile_json[:100]}...")

# Parse JSON back to Python dict
import json
profile_data = json.loads(profile_json)
print(f"Username: {profile_data['username']}")
print(f"Skills: {profile_data['skills']}")
Full profile: {"user_id":1,"username":"john_dev","email":"john@example.com","preferences":{"job_alerts":t... Username: john_dev Skills: ['Python', 'Django', 'React']
Example 2: Get specific nested values
# Get specific user preferences
preferences = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "user:profile:1", "$.preferences")
salary_min = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "user:profile:1", "$.preferences.salary_min")
skills = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "user:profile:1", "$.skills")

print(f"User preferences: {preferences}")
print(f"Minimum salary: {salary_min}")
print(f"User skills: {skills}")
User preferences: [{"job_alerts":true,"remote_only":false,"salary_min":90000}] Minimum salary: [90000] User skills: [["Python","Django","React"]]
Example 3: Get multiple paths simultaneously
# Get username and email in one call
user_basics = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "user:profile:1", 
                               "$.username", "$.email", "$.experience_years")
print(f"User basics: {user_basics}")

# Parse the multi-path result
basics_data = json.loads(user_basics)
print(f"Parsed data: {basics_data}")
User basics: {"$.username":["john_dev"],"$.email":["john@example.com"],"$.experience_years":[5]} Parsed data: {'$.username': ['john_dev'], '$.email': ['john@example.com'], '$.experience_years': [5]}
Example 4: Pretty formatted JSON output
# Get job metadata with pretty formatting
job_meta = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "job:meta:42", "$", 
                            "INDENT", "  ", "NEWLINE", "\n", "SPACE", " ")
print("Pretty formatted job metadata:")
print(job_meta)
Pretty formatted job metadata: [ { "job_id": 42, "analytics": { "views": 0, "applications": 0, "saves": 0 }, "seo_data": { "title_variations": [ "Python Developer", "Django Developer", "Backend Python Developer" ], "keywords": [ "python", "django", "backend", "api" ] }, "flags": { "featured": false, "urgent": false, "remote_friendly": true } } ]
Example 5: Get search filter components
# Retrieve specific filter components for a search form
location_filter = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "search:filters:user123", "$.location")
salary_range = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "search:filters:user123", "$.salary")

print(f"Location filter: {location_filter}")
print(f"Salary range: {salary_range}")
print(f"Required skills: {required_skills}")

# Parse for use in Django forms
location_data = json.loads(location_filter)[0]
print(f"City: {location_data['city']}, Remote: {location_data['remote']}")
Location filter: [{"city":"New York","remote":true,"radius_miles":25}] Salary range: [{"min":75000,"max":120000,"currency":"USD"}] Required skills: [["Python","Django","PostgreSQL"]] City: New York, Remote: True

JSON.DEL

Docs: Redis JSON.DEL Documentation

Deletes a value.

Full description

JSON.DEL removes values at specified paths from a JSON document. You can delete entire documents, specific objects, array elements, or individual fields. Perfect for removing deprecated user preferences, clearing cache entries, removing sensitive data, or cleaning up job listings. When deleting array elements, indices are automatically adjusted.

Syntax

JSON.DEL key [path]

Parameters

  • key: The Redis key containing the JSON document
  • path: JSONPath to the value to delete (default: $ deletes entire document)

Return value

Integer: Number of paths deleted.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Delete specific user preference
# Remove deprecated preference
deleted_count = r.execute_command("JSON.DEL", "user:profile:1", "$.preferences.legacy_setting")
print(f"Deleted {deleted_count} legacy settings")

# Remove email notifications preference
email_deleted = r.execute_command("JSON.DEL", "user:profile:1", "$.preferences.email_notifications")
print(f"Email notifications setting deleted: {email_deleted}")

# Verify preferences structure
prefs = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "user:profile:1", "$.preferences")
print(f"Remaining preferences: {prefs}")
Deleted 0 legacy settings Email notifications setting deleted: 0 Remaining preferences: [{"job_alerts":true,"remote_only":false,"salary_min":90000}]
Example 2: Remove skill from user profile
# First, let's see current skills
current_skills = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "user:profile:1", "$.skills")
print(f"Current skills: {current_skills}")

# Remove a specific skill (if it exists at index 1)
# Note: For array element removal, we'll add the skill back first
r.execute_command("JSON.SET", "user:profile:1", "$.skills", '["Python", "Django", "React", "JavaScript"]')

# Now remove React (index 2)
r.execute_command("JSON.DEL", "user:profile:1", "$.skills[2]")

# Check updated skills
updated_skills = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "user:profile:1", "$.skills")
print(f"Skills after deletion: {updated_skills}")
Current skills: [["Python","Django","React"]] Skills after deletion: [["Python","Django","JavaScript"]]
Example 3: Delete job analytics data
# Remove analytics data while keeping other job metadata
analytics_deleted = r.execute_command("JSON.DEL", "job:meta:42", "$.analytics")
print(f"Analytics data deleted: {analytics_deleted}")

# Verify what remains
remaining_meta = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "job:meta:42")
remaining_data = json.loads(remaining_meta)
print(f"Remaining metadata keys: {list(remaining_data.keys())}")
print(f"SEO keywords: {remaining_data['seo_data']['keywords']}")
Analytics data deleted: 1 Remaining metadata keys: ['job_id', 'seo_data', 'flags'] SEO keywords: ['python', 'django', 'backend', 'api']
Example 4: Clear sensitive data from user session
# Store user session with sensitive data
session_data = {
    "user_id": 123,
    "login_time": "2025-08-31T10:00:00",
    "temp_password": "temp123",
    "csrf_token": "abc123xyz",
    "preferences": {"theme": "dark"}
}

r.execute_command("JSON.SET", "session:temp:123", "$", json.dumps(session_data))

# Remove sensitive data
r.execute_command("JSON.DEL", "session:temp:123", "$.temp_password")
r.execute_command("JSON.DEL", "session:temp:123", "$.csrf_token")

# Verify cleanup
cleaned_session = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "session:temp:123")
print(f"Cleaned session: {cleaned_session}")
Cleaned session: {"user_id":123,"login_time":"2025-08-31T10:00:00","preferences":{"theme":"dark"}}
Example 5: Delete entire document
# Check if document exists
exists_before = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "session:temp:123", "$")
print(f"Document exists before deletion: {exists_before is not None}")

# Delete entire document
deleted_count = r.execute_command("JSON.DEL", "session:temp:123")
print(f"Deleted {deleted_count} documents")

# Verify deletion
try:
    exists_after = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "session:temp:123")
    print(f"Document exists after deletion: {exists_after is not None}")
except:
    print("Document no longer exists")
Document exists before deletion: True Deleted 1 documents Document no longer exists

JSON.ARRAPPEND

Docs: Redis JSON.ARRAPPEND Documentation

Append one or more JSON values into the array at path after the last element in it.

Full description

JSON.ARRAPPEND adds one or more JSON values to the end of an array at a specified path. If the array doesn't exist, it creates one. This is perfect for adding skills to user profiles, appending job applications, adding items to shopping lists, or maintaining activity logs in Django applications. All appended values must be valid JSON.

Syntax

JSON.ARRAPPEND key path value [value ...]

Parameters

  • key: The Redis key containing the JSON document
  • path: JSONPath to the array to append to
  • value: One or more JSON values to append to the array

Return value

Array: The new length of each array that was modified.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Add skills to user profile
# Add new skills to user's skill array
new_length = r.execute_command("JSON.ARRAPPEND", "user:profile:1", "$.skills", '"PostgreSQL"', '"Docker"')
print(f"New skills array length: {new_length}")

# Verify updated skills
skills = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "user:profile:1", "$.skills")
skills_list = json.loads(skills)[0]
print(f"Updated skills: {skills_list}")
New skills array length: [5] Updated skills: ['Python', 'Django', 'JavaScript', 'PostgreSQL', 'Docker']
Example 2: Add job applications to tracking list
# Create job applications tracking
applications_data = {
    "job_id": 42,
    "applications": []
}
r.execute_command("JSON.SET", "job:applications:42", "$", json.dumps(applications_data))

# Add new applications
application1 = json.dumps({
    "user_id": 101,
    "applied_at": "2025-08-31T10:00:00",
    "status": "pending"
})

application2 = json.dumps({
    "user_id": 102,
    "applied_at": "2025-08-31T10:15:00",
    "status": "pending"
})

new_length = r.execute_command("JSON.ARRAPPEND", "job:applications:42", "$.applications", 
                             application1, application2)
print(f"Applications array now has {new_length[0]} items")
Applications array now has 2 items
Example 3: Append search keywords to SEO data
# Add more SEO keywords to job posting

                                '"redis"', '"caching"', '"performance"')
print(f"SEO keywords array length: {new_keywords}")

# Check updated keywords
keywords = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "job:meta:42", "$.seo_data.keywords")
keyword_list = json.loads(keywords)[0]
print(f"SEO keywords: {keyword_list}")
SEO keywords array length: [7] SEO keywords: ['python', 'django', 'backend', 'api', 'redis', 'caching', 'performance']
Example 4: Add activity log entries
# Create user activity log
activity_log = {"user_id": 1, "activities": []}
r.execute_command("JSON.SET", "user:activity:1", "$", json.dumps(activity_log))

# Add multiple activities
activity1 = json.dumps({
    "action": "job_view",
    "job_id": 42,
    "timestamp": "2025-08-31T10:30:00"
})

activity2 = json.dumps({
    "action": "skill_update", 
    "changes": ["added PostgreSQL"],
    "timestamp": "2025-08-31T10:45:00"
})

                          activity1, activity2)
print(f"Activity log entries: {length[0]}")

# Show recent activities
recent = r.execute_command("JSON.GET", "user:activity:1", "$.activities[-2:]")
print(f"Recent activities: {len(json.loads(recent)[0])} entries")
Activity log entries: 2 Recent activities: 2 entries
Example 5: Add education entries to user profile
# Initialize education array in user profile

# Add education entries

    "degree": "Bachelor of Science",
    "field": "Computer Science",

    "year": 2018

Pub/Sub Commands

Redis Pub/Sub (Publish/Subscribe) provides a powerful messaging pattern for real-time communication between different parts of your Django application. Perfect for implementing live notifications, chat systems, activity feeds, real-time job updates, or any scenario where components need to communicate asynchronously without direct coupling.

📡 Pub/Sub Commands Overview

PUBLISH - Send messages to channels
SUBSCRIBE - Listen to channels
UNSUBSCRIBE - Stop channel subscriptions
PSUBSCRIBE - Pattern-based subscriptions
PUNSUBSCRIBE - Stop pattern subscriptions
PUBSUB CHANNELS - List active channels
PUBSUB NUMSUB - Count channel subscribers
PUBSUB NUMPAT - Count pattern subscriptions
SPUBLISH - Shard channel publishing
SSUBSCRIBE - Shard channel subscriptions
SUNSUBSCRIBE - Stop shard subscriptions
PUBSUB SHARDCHANNELS - List shard channels
PUBSUB SHARDNUMSUB - Count shard subscribers
🎯 Perfect for Django: Use Pub/Sub for real-time job notifications, user activity broadcasting, cache invalidation signals, or any event-driven communication. Ideal for decoupling microservices, implementing websocket backends, or building live dashboards that respond to model changes.

PUBLISH

Docs: Redis PUBLISH Documentation

Posts a message to a channel.

Full description

PUBLISH sends a message to all subscribers of a specified channel. Messages are not stored - they are delivered only to active subscribers at the time of publishing. This is perfect for real-time notifications in Django applications, such as broadcasting job updates, user activity, or system events to connected clients or services.

Syntax

PUBLISH channel message

Parameters

  • channel: The channel name to publish to (string). Use namespaces like "jobs:updates", "user:notifications", or "system:alerts".
  • message: The message content to send (string). Can be plain text, JSON, or any serialized data.

Return value

Integer: The number of subscribers that received the message.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Broadcast new job posting
import redis
import json
from jobs.models import JobPost

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# When a new job is created
job = JobPost.objects.first()
message = json.dumps({
    "type": "job_created",
    "job_id": job.id,
    "title": job.title,
    "company": job.author.company if job.author else None,
    "salary": job.salary,
    "timestamp": job.date.isoformat()
})

subscribers = r.publish("jobs:updates", message)
print(f"Message sent to {subscribers} subscribers")
Message sent to 3 subscribers
Example 2: User activity notification
# Notify when user updates profile
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

user = User.objects.first()
activity_data = {
    "type": "profile_updated",
    "user_id": user.id,
    "username": user.username,
    "changes": ["bio", "skills"],
    "timestamp": "2024-08-31T15:30:00Z"
}

result = r.publish(f"user:{user.id}:activity", json.dumps(activity_data))
print(f"Activity broadcasted to {result} listeners")
Activity broadcasted to 2 listeners
Example 3: System alert broadcast
# Send system-wide maintenance alert
alert = {
    "type": "maintenance",
    "message": "System maintenance scheduled for 2AM UTC",
    "severity": "info",
    "scheduled_time": "2024-09-01T02:00:00Z"
}

count = r.publish("system:alerts", json.dumps(alert))
print(f"Alert sent to {count} connected services")
Alert sent to 5 connected services
Example 4: Job application notification
# Notify job author about new application
job = JobPost.objects.first()
application_data = {
    "type": "new_application",
    "job_id": job.id,
    "job_title": job.title,
    "applicant_count": 10,  # Could be from related model
    "message": f"New application received for {job.title}"
}

subscribers = r.publish(f"job:{job.id}:applications", json.dumps(application_data))
print(f"Notification sent to {subscribers} watchers")
Notification sent to 1 watchers
Example 5: Cache invalidation signal
# Signal cache invalidation after model update
from jobs.models import Skills

skill = Skills.objects.first()
invalidation_signal = {
    "type": "cache_invalidate",
    "model": "skills",
    "object_id": skill.id,
    "action": "updated",
    "keys_to_invalidate": [
        f"skill:{skill.id}",
        "skills:list",
        "skills:popular"
    ]
}

services = r.publish("cache:invalidation", json.dumps(invalidation_signal))
print(f"Cache invalidation sent to {services} cache managers")
Cache invalidation sent to 4 cache managers

SUBSCRIBE

Docs: Redis SUBSCRIBE Documentation

Listens for messages published to channels.

Full description

SUBSCRIBE starts listening for messages on one or more channels. The client enters a special mode where it only receives messages and cannot send other Redis commands (except other Pub/Sub commands). Perfect for Django background services, real-time processors, or any component that needs to react to published events.

Syntax

SUBSCRIBE channel [channel ...]

Parameters

  • channel [channel ...]: One or more channel names to subscribe to. Once subscribed, you'll receive all messages published to these channels.

Return value

Array: Subscription confirmation messages, then a stream of published messages.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Job updates listener
import redis
import json

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Subscribe to job updates channel
pubsub = r.pubsub()
pubsub.subscribe("jobs:updates")

print("Listening for job updates...")
for message in pubsub.listen():
    if message['type'] == 'message':
        data = json.loads(message['data'])
        print(f"New job: {data['title']} at {data['company']}")
        # Process job update in Django view/task
        # send_email_notification(data)
        # update_cache(data)
        break  # Remove in real implementation
Listening for job updates... New job: Python Developer at ABC Corp
Example 2: Multiple channel subscription
# Listen to multiple related channels
pubsub = r.pubsub()
pubsub.subscribe("jobs:updates", "jobs:applications", "system:alerts")

print("Monitoring job system...")
for message in pubsub.listen():
    if message['type'] == 'message':
        channel = message['channel']
        data = json.loads(message['data'])
        
        if channel == "jobs:updates":
            print(f"Job update: {data['title']}")
        elif channel == "jobs:applications":
            print(f"New application for job {data['job_id']}")
        elif channel == "system:alerts":
            print(f"System alert: {data['message']}")
        break
Monitoring job system... Job update: Frontend Developer
Example 3: User-specific notifications
# Subscribe to user-specific activity
user_id = 1
pubsub = r.pubsub()
pubsub.subscribe(f"user:{user_id}:notifications")

print(f"Listening for user {user_id} notifications...")
for message in pubsub.listen():
    if message['type'] == 'message':
        notification = json.loads(message['data'])
        print(f"Notification: {notification['message']}")
        # Update Django user notification model
        # UserNotification.objects.create(user_id=user_id, ...)
        break
Listening for user 1 notifications... Notification: Your job application was viewed
Example 4: Cache invalidation listener
# Service that handles cache invalidation
pubsub = r.pubsub()
pubsub.subscribe("cache:invalidation")

print("Cache manager listening...")
for message in pubsub.listen():
    if message['type'] == 'message':
        invalidation = json.loads(message['data'])
        keys_to_clear = invalidation['keys_to_invalidate']
        
        print(f"Invalidating {len(keys_to_clear)} cache keys")
        for key in keys_to_clear:
            r.delete(key)
            print(f"Cleared cache key: {key}")
        break
Cache manager listening... Invalidating 3 cache keys Cleared cache key: skill:1 Cleared cache key: skills:list Cleared cache key: skills:popular
Example 5: Background task processor
# Background service processing job-related tasks
pubsub = r.pubsub()
pubsub.subscribe("tasks:background")

print("Background task processor ready...")
for message in pubsub.listen():
    if message['type'] == 'message':
        task = json.loads(message['data'])
        task_type = task['type']
        
        if task_type == 'send_email':
            print(f"Sending email: {task['subject']}")
            # send_email_task.delay(task['data'])
        elif task_type == 'generate_report':
            print(f"Generating report: {task['report_type']}")
            # generate_report_task.delay(task['params'])
        break
Background task processor ready... Sending email: New Job Application Received

PSUBSCRIBE

Docs: Redis PSUBSCRIBE Documentation

Listens for messages published to channels that match one or more patterns.

Full description

PSUBSCRIBE allows you to subscribe to channels using glob-style patterns with wildcards. This is extremely powerful for Django applications where you want to listen to related channels without knowing their exact names in advance. Use * for any sequence of characters and ? for single characters.

Syntax

PSUBSCRIBE pattern [pattern ...]

Parameters

  • pattern [pattern ...]: One or more glob patterns. Wildcards: * (any chars), ? (single char), [abc] (character class).

Return value

Array: Pattern subscription confirmations, then matching messages with pattern info.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Listen to all job events
import redis
import json

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Subscribe to all job-related channels
pubsub = r.pubsub()
pubsub.psubscribe("jobs:*")

print("Listening to all job channels...")
for message in pubsub.listen():
    if message['type'] == 'pmessage':
        pattern = message['pattern']
        channel = message['channel'] 
        data = json.loads(message['data'])
        
        print(f"Channel {channel} (pattern {pattern}): {data['type']}")
        break
Listening to all job channels... Channel jobs:updates (pattern jobs:*): job_created
Example 2: User activity monitoring
# Monitor all user activity across the platform
pubsub = r.pubsub()
pubsub.psubscribe("user:*:activity")

print("Monitoring all user activities...")
for message in pubsub.listen():
    if message['type'] == 'pmessage':
        channel = message['channel']
        # Extract user ID from channel name: user:123:activity
        user_id = channel.split(':')[1]
        activity = json.loads(message['data'])
        
        print(f"User {user_id} activity: {activity['type']}")
        # Log to Django analytics model
        # UserActivity.objects.create(user_id=user_id, ...)
        break
Monitoring all user activities... User 1 activity: profile_updated
Example 3: Application notifications by job
# Listen to application events for all jobs
pubsub = r.pubsub()
pubsub.psubscribe("job:*:applications")

print("Monitoring job applications...")
for message in pubsub.listen():
    if message['type'] == 'pmessage':
        channel = message['channel']
        job_id = channel.split(':')[1]  # Extract from job:123:applications
        app_data = json.loads(message['data'])
        
        print(f"Job {job_id}: {app_data['type']}")
        # Update job stats in Django
        # JobPost.objects.filter(id=job_id).update(application_count=...)
        break
Monitoring job applications... Job 1: new_application
Example 4: System-wide event monitoring
# Monitor all system events with pattern matching
pubsub = r.pubsub()
pubsub.psubscribe("system:*", "cache:*", "email:*")

print("System monitoring active...")
for message in pubsub.listen():
    if message['type'] == 'pmessage':
        pattern = message['pattern']
        channel = message['channel']
        event = json.loads(message['data'])
        
        print(f"{pattern} -> {channel}: {event['type']}")
        
        # Route to appropriate handler based on pattern
        if pattern == "system:*":
            handle_system_event(event)
        elif pattern == "cache:*":
            handle_cache_event(event)
        break
System monitoring active... cache:* -> cache:invalidation: cache_invalidate
Example 5: Environment-specific monitoring
# Monitor events for specific environment (dev, staging, prod)
environment = "prod"
pubsub = r.pubsub()
pubsub.psubscribe(f"{environment}:*:errors", f"{environment}:*:alerts")

print(f"Monitoring {environment} environment...")
for message in pubsub.listen():
    if message['type'] == 'pmessage':
        channel = message['channel']
        error_data = json.loads(message['data'])
        
        # Extract service name from pattern like prod:api:errors
        service = channel.split(':')[1]
        print(f"Service {service} {error_data['level']}: {error_data['message']}")
        
        # Send to Django error tracking
        # ErrorLog.objects.create(service=service, ...)
        break
Monitoring prod environment... Service api critical: Database connection failed

UNSUBSCRIBE

Docs: Redis UNSUBSCRIBE Documentation

Stops listening to messages posted to channels.

Full description

UNSUBSCRIBE stops the client from receiving messages on specified channels. If no channels are specified, it unsubscribes from all channels. This is essential for graceful cleanup in Django services, preventing memory leaks, and managing dynamic subscriptions based on user activity or service state.

Syntax

UNSUBSCRIBE [channel [channel ...]]

Parameters

  • [channel [channel ...]]: Optional channel names to unsubscribe from. If omitted, unsubscribes from all channels.

Return value

Array: Unsubscription confirmations showing remaining subscription count.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Graceful service shutdown
import redis
import signal

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)
pubsub = r.pubsub()
pubsub.subscribe("jobs:updates", "user:notifications")

def graceful_shutdown(signum, frame):
    print("Shutting down gracefully...")
    pubsub.unsubscribe()  # Unsubscribe from all channels
    pubsub.close()
    print("Unsubscribed from all channels")

# Set up signal handler for clean shutdown
signal.signal(signal.SIGTERM, graceful_shutdown)

print("Service running... (Ctrl+C to stop)")
# In real app, this would be your main processing loop
Service running... (Ctrl+C to stop) Shutting down gracefully... Unsubscribed from all channels
Example 2: Dynamic subscription management
# Manage subscriptions based on user activity
user_id = 1
pubsub = r.pubsub()

# Subscribe to user's job interests
pubsub.subscribe(f"jobs:tech", f"jobs:remote", f"user:{user_id}:notifications")
print("Subscribed to 3 channels")

# User changes preferences - unsubscribe from some channels
pubsub.unsubscribe("jobs:remote")
print("Unsubscribed from remote jobs")

# Subscribe to new interests
pubsub.subscribe("jobs:fulltime")
print("Subscribed to fulltime jobs")

# Check current subscriptions
channels = pubsub.channels
print(f"Currently subscribed to: {list(channels.keys())}")
Subscribed to 3 channels Unsubscribed from remote jobs Subscribed to fulltime jobs Currently subscribed to: ['jobs:tech', 'user:1:notifications', 'jobs:fulltime']
Example 3: Conditional unsubscription
# Unsubscribe based on business logic
from jobs.models import JobPost

pubsub = r.pubsub()
job_channels = []

# Subscribe to active job application channels
for job in JobPost.objects.filter(expiry__gte=timezone.now()):
    channel = f"job:{job.id}:applications"
    pubsub.subscribe(channel)
    job_channels.append(channel)

print(f"Subscribed to {len(job_channels)} job channels")

# Later: unsubscribe from expired jobs
expired_jobs = JobPost.objects.filter(expiry__lt=timezone.now())
for job in expired_jobs:
    channel = f"job:{job.id}:applications"
    if channel in job_channels:
        pubsub.unsubscribe(channel)
        print(f"Unsubscribed from expired job {job.id}")

remaining = len([ch for ch in pubsub.channels if ch.startswith('job:')])
print(f"Still monitoring {remaining} active jobs")
Subscribed to 5 job channels Unsubscribed from expired job 2 Unsubscribed from expired job 4 Still monitoring 3 active jobs

    f"user:{user_id}:job_alerts",
    f"user:{user_id}:messages",

Example 5: Performance-based unsubscription
# Monitor and unsubscribe from high-traffic channels if needed
import time

pubsub = r.pubsub()
high_traffic_channels = ["global:activity", "jobs:all_updates", "system:logs"]

for channel in high_traffic_channels:
    pubsub.subscribe(channel)

message_count = 0
start_time = time.time()

# Monitor message rate (simplified for example)
print("Monitoring message rate...")
for message in pubsub.listen():
    if message['type'] == 'message':
        message_count += 1
        
        # Check rate every 10 messages
        if message_count % 10 == 0:
            elapsed = time.time() - start_time
            rate = message_count / elapsed
            
            if rate > 100:  # More than 100 messages per second
                print(f"High traffic detected ({rate:.1f} msg/sec)")
                # Unsubscribe from noisiest channels
                pubsub.unsubscribe("system:logs", "global:activity") 
                print("Unsubscribed from high-traffic channels")
                break

print("Monitoring continues with reduced load")
Monitoring message rate... High traffic detected (142.3 msg/sec) Unsubscribed from high-traffic channels Monitoring continues with reduced load

PUBSUB CHANNELS

Docs: Redis PUBSUB CHANNELS Documentation

Returns the active channels.

Full description

PUBSUB CHANNELS lists all currently active channels (channels that have at least one subscriber). You can optionally provide a pattern to filter channels. This is invaluable for monitoring your Django application's pub/sub usage, debugging subscription issues, and understanding communication patterns.

Syntax

PUBSUB CHANNELS [pattern]

Parameters

  • [pattern]: Optional glob pattern to filter channel names. If omitted, returns all active channels.

Return value

Array: List of active channel names matching the pattern.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Monitor all active channels
import redis

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# First, create some subscriptions (in separate processes/threads)
# pubsub1.subscribe("jobs:updates")
# pubsub2.subscribe("user:1:notifications") 
# pubsub3.subscribe("system:alerts")

# Check all active channels
active_channels = r.pubsub_channels()
print(f"Active channels ({len(active_channels)}):")
for channel in sorted(active_channels):
    print(f"  - {channel}")

# Use for Django admin dashboard or monitoring
Active channels (3): - jobs:updates - system:alerts - user:1:notifications
Example 2: Filter job-related channels

job_channels = r.pubsub_channels("jobs:*")

for channel in job_channels:
    # Extract job info from channel name
    if ":" in channel:
        parts = channel.split(":")
        if len(parts) >= 2:
            category = parts[1]
            print(f"  - {channel} (category: {category})")

# Useful for job platform monitoring dashboard

    parts = channel.split(":")
    if len(parts) >= 2 and parts[1].isdigit():

Example 5: Channel usage analytics
# Analyze channel usage patterns for optimization

# Categorize channels by namespace
categories = {}
for channel in all_channels:
    namespace = channel.split(":")[0] if ":" in channel else "uncategorized"
    if namespace not in categories:
        categories[namespace] = []
    categories[namespace].append(channel)

for namespace, channels in sorted(categories.items()):
    print(f"  {namespace}: {len(channels)} channels")
    
    # Show specific channels for smaller categories
    if len(channels) <= 3:
        for ch in channels:
            print(f"    - {ch}")

if total_channels > 100:
    print("⚠️  Consider optimizing channel usage")
