Each provides specific functionality for managing subscriptions, monitoring usage, and handling shard-based messaging for high-performance scenarios.

Scripting and Functions Commands

Redis scripting capabilities allow you to execute server-side Lua scripts and functions, providing atomic operations, complex business logic, and enhanced performance by reducing network round-trips. Perfect for Django applications that need guaranteed atomic operations, complex data transformations, or custom business logic that benefits from server-side execution.

⚡ Scripting Commands Overview

EVAL - Execute Lua scripts
EVAL_RO - Execute read-only scripts
EVALSHA - Execute by SHA1 digest
EVALSHA_RO - Execute read-only by SHA
FCALL - Invoke functions
FCALL_RO - Invoke read-only functions
FUNCTION LOAD - Create function libraries
FUNCTION DELETE - Delete libraries
FUNCTION LIST - List all libraries
FUNCTION FLUSH - Delete all libraries
FUNCTION DUMP - Export libraries
FUNCTION RESTORE - Import libraries
SCRIPT LOAD - Cache Lua scripts
SCRIPT EXISTS - Check script cache
SCRIPT FLUSH - Clear script cache
SCRIPT KILL - Terminate scripts
SCRIPT DEBUG - Debug mode
🎯 Perfect for Django: Use scripting for atomic job application processing, complex user score calculations, batch operations on related models, custom caching logic, or any scenario where you need guaranteed consistency and reduced network overhead between Django and Redis.

EVAL

Docs: Redis EVAL Documentation

Executes a server-side Lua script.

Full description

EVAL executes a Lua script on the Redis server, providing atomic operations and reducing network round-trips. Scripts have access to all Redis commands and can perform complex operations atomically. Perfect for Django applications that need to ensure data consistency across multiple Redis operations or implement custom business logic server-side.

Syntax

EVAL script numkeys [key ...] [arg ...]

Parameters

  • script: The Lua script code to execute (string).
  • numkeys: Number of key arguments that follow (integer).
  • [key ...]: Keys that the script will access (available as KEYS[] in Lua).
  • [arg ...]: Additional arguments (available as ARGV[] in Lua).

Return value

Mixed: The value returned by the Lua script, can be string, number, array, or nil.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Atomic job application with counter
import redis

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Lua script for atomic job application
application_script = """
local job_key = KEYS[1]
local counter_key = KEYS[2] 
local applicant_id = ARGV[1]
local max_applications = tonumber(ARGV[2])

-- Get current application count
local current_count = redis.call('GET', counter_key) or 0
current_count = tonumber(current_count)

-- Check if limit reached
if current_count >= max_applications then
    return {false, current_count, "Application limit reached"}
end

-- Add application and increment counter atomically
redis.call('SADD', job_key, applicant_id)
local new_count = redis.call('INCR', counter_key)

return {true, new_count, "Application successful"}
"""

# Apply for job with limit check
job_id = 1
result = r.eval(application_script, 2, 
               f"job:{job_id}:applicants", 
               f"job:{job_id}:count",
               "user123", 
               "50")

success, count, message = result
print(f"Success: {success}, Count: {count}, Message: {message}")
Success: True, Count: 15, Message: Application successful
Example 2: Complex user skill scoring
# Lua script for calculating weighted skill scores
skill_scoring_script = """
local user_key = KEYS[1]
local skill_weights_key = KEYS[2]
local skills = cjson.decode(ARGV[1])

local total_score = 0
local skill_count = 0

-- Get skill weights
local weights = redis.call('HGETALL', skill_weights_key)
local weight_map = {}
for i = 1, #weights, 2 do
    weight_map[weights[i]] = tonumber(weights[i + 1])
end

-- Calculate weighted score
for _, skill in ipairs(skills) do
    local weight = weight_map[skill] or 1
    total_score = total_score + weight
    skill_count = skill_count + 1
end

-- Store user score
local final_score = skill_count > 0 and (total_score / skill_count) or 0
redis.call('HSET', user_key, 'skill_score', final_score, 'skill_count', skill_count)

return {final_score, skill_count, total_score}
"""

import json

# Calculate user skill score
skills_list = ["Python", "Django", "JavaScript", "React"]
result = r.eval(skill_scoring_script, 2,
               "user:1:profile",
               "skills:weights", 
               json.dumps(skills_list))

score, count, total = result
print(f"Average Score: {score:.2f}, Skills: {count}, Total: {total}")
Average Score: 4.25, Skills: 4, Total: 17
Example 3: Batch job status update
# Batch update job statuses with logging
batch_update_script = """
local job_ids = cjson.decode(ARGV[1])
local new_status = ARGV[2]
local timestamp = ARGV[3]

local updated = 0
local errors = {}

for _, job_id in ipairs(job_ids) do
    local job_key = "job:" .. job_id
    
    -- Check if job exists
    if redis.call('EXISTS', job_key) == 1 then
        -- Update status and add to history
        redis.call('HSET', job_key, 'status', new_status, 'updated_at', timestamp)
        redis.call('LPUSH', job_key .. ':history', 
                  timestamp .. ':' .. new_status)
        updated = updated + 1
    else
        table.insert(errors, job_id)
    end
end

return {updated, errors}
"""

# Batch update multiple job statuses
job_ids = [1, 2, 3, 5, 8]
timestamp = "2024-08-31T15:30:00Z"

result = r.eval(batch_update_script, 0,
               json.dumps(job_ids),
               "closed", 
               timestamp)

updated, errors = result
print(f"Updated {updated} jobs, Errors for jobs: {errors}")
Updated 4 jobs, Errors for jobs: [8]
Example 4: Smart cache with TTL management
# Smart caching with automatic TTL adjustment
smart_cache_script = """
local cache_key = KEYS[1]
local data = ARGV[1]
local base_ttl = tonumber(ARGV[2])
local access_count_key = cache_key .. ':access_count'

-- Get access count for TTL calculation
local access_count = redis.call('GET', access_count_key) or 0
access_count = tonumber(access_count)

-- Calculate dynamic TTL based on popularity
local popularity_multiplier = math.min(access_count / 10, 5) -- Max 5x multiplier
local dynamic_ttl = math.floor(base_ttl * (1 + popularity_multiplier))

-- Store data with dynamic TTL
redis.call('SETEX', cache_key, dynamic_ttl, data)

-- Increment access count with its own TTL
redis.call('INCR', access_count_key)
redis.call('EXPIRE', access_count_key, dynamic_ttl)

return {dynamic_ttl, access_count + 1, popularity_multiplier}
"""

# Cache job data with smart TTL
from jobs.models import JobPost
job = JobPost.objects.first()
job_data = json.dumps({
    "title": job.title,
    "company": job.author.company if job.author else None,
    "salary": job.salary
})

result = r.eval(smart_cache_script, 1,
               f"job:{job.id}:cached",
               job_data,
               "300")  # 5 minutes base TTL

ttl, access_count, multiplier = result
print(f"Cached with TTL: {ttl}s, Access count: {access_count}, Multiplier: {multiplier:.1f}x")
Cached with TTL: 450s, Access count: 8, Multiplier: 0.7x
Example 5: Atomic user activity tracking
# Track user activity with rate limiting
activity_tracking_script = """
local user_id = ARGV[1]
local activity_type = ARGV[2]
local timestamp = ARGV[3]
local rate_limit = tonumber(ARGV[4])

local activity_key = "user:" .. user_id .. ":activity:" .. activity_type
local rate_key = activity_key .. ":rate"

-- Check rate limit (activities per minute)
local current_minute = math.floor(tonumber(timestamp) / 60)
local rate_window_key = rate_key .. ":" .. current_minute

local current_rate = redis.call('GET', rate_window_key) or 0
current_rate = tonumber(current_rate)

if current_rate >= rate_limit then
    return {false, current_rate, "Rate limit exceeded"}
end

-- Log activity and update rate counter
redis.call('LPUSH', activity_key, timestamp)
redis.call('LTRIM', activity_key, 0, 99)  -- Keep last 100 activities

redis.call('INCR', rate_window_key)
redis.call('EXPIRE', rate_window_key, 60)  -- 1 minute window

-- Update user stats
local stats_key = "user:" .. user_id .. ":stats"
redis.call('HINCRBY', stats_key, activity_type .. "_count", 1)
redis.call('HSET', stats_key, "last_activity", timestamp)

return {true, current_rate + 1, "Activity logged"}
"""

import time

# Track user job view activity
user_id = 1
timestamp = str(int(time.time()))

result = r.eval(activity_tracking_script, 0,
               str(user_id),
               "job_view",
               timestamp,
               "10")  # Max 10 views per minute

success, rate, message = result
print(f"Success: {success}, Current rate: {rate}, Message: {message}")
Success: True, Current rate: 3, Message: Activity logged

EVALSHA

Docs: Redis EVALSHA Documentation

Executes a server-side Lua script by SHA1 digest.

Full description

EVALSHA executes a previously loaded Lua script using its SHA1 hash instead of sending the full script code. This is more efficient for scripts that are used repeatedly, as it reduces network bandwidth and improves performance. Perfect for Django applications with frequently used business logic scripts.

Syntax

EVALSHA sha1 numkeys [key ...] [arg ...]

Parameters

  • sha1: SHA1 hash of the script to execute (string).
  • numkeys: Number of key arguments that follow (integer).
  • [key ...]: Keys that the script will access.
  • [arg ...]: Additional arguments for the script.

Return value

Mixed: The value returned by the Lua script.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Load and execute job ranking script
import redis
import hashlib

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Job ranking script
ranking_script = """
local job_key = KEYS[1]
local user_id = ARGV[1]
local view_weight = tonumber(ARGV[2])
local apply_weight = tonumber(ARGV[3])

-- Get job stats
local views = redis.call('HGET', job_key, 'views') or 0
local applications = redis.call('HGET', job_key, 'applications') or 0

-- Calculate ranking score
local score = (tonumber(views) * view_weight) + (tonumber(applications) * apply_weight)

-- Update job ranking
redis.call('ZADD', 'jobs:ranking', score, job_key)

return score
"""

# Load script and get SHA
script_sha = r.script_load(ranking_script)
print(f"Script SHA: {script_sha}")

# Execute using SHA
job_id = 1
score = r.evalsha(script_sha, 1, f"job:{job_id}", "user1", "1", "5")
print(f"Job ranking score: {score}")
Script SHA: a42b4c3d5e6f7890abcdef1234567890abcdef12 Job ranking score: 85
Example 2: Cached user preference script
# User preference matching script (reusable)
preference_script = """
local user_key = KEYS[1]
local job_key = KEYS[2]

-- Get user preferences
local prefs = redis.call('HGETALL', user_key)
local user_prefs = {}
for i = 1, #prefs, 2 do
    user_prefs[prefs[i]] = prefs[i + 1]
end

-- Get job details
local job = redis.call('HGETALL', job_key)
local job_details = {}
for i = 1, #job, 2 do
    job_details[job[i]] = job[i + 1]
end

-- Calculate match score
local matches = 0
local total_criteria = 0

-- Check salary match
if user_prefs.min_salary and job_details.salary then
    total_criteria = total_criteria + 1
    if tonumber(job_details.salary) >= tonumber(user_prefs.min_salary) then
        matches = matches + 1
    end
end

-- Check location match
if user_prefs.preferred_location and job_details.location then
    total_criteria = total_criteria + 1
    if job_details.location == user_prefs.preferred_location then
        matches = matches + 1
    end
end

local match_percentage = total_criteria > 0 and (matches / total_criteria * 100) or 0
return {match_percentage, matches, total_criteria}
"""

# Load script once
pref_sha = r.script_load(preference_script)

# Use multiple times efficiently
job_ids = [1, 2, 3]
for job_id in job_ids:
    result = r.evalsha(pref_sha, 2, "user:1:preferences", f"job:{job_id}")
    percentage, matches, total = result
    print(f"Job {job_id}: {percentage}% match ({matches}/{total})")
Job 1: 75% match (3/4) Job 2: 50% match (2/4) Job 3: 100% match (4/4)
Example 3: Performance comparison EVAL vs EVALSHA
import time

# Simple increment script for performance testing
increment_script = """
local key = KEYS[1]
local increment = tonumber(ARGV[1])

return redis.call('INCRBY', key, increment)
"""

# Load script
inc_sha = r.script_load(increment_script)

# Performance test
def time_operation(operation_func, iterations=100):
    start = time.time()
    for i in range(iterations):
        operation_func(i)
    return time.time() - start

# Test EVAL
def test_eval(i):
    return r.eval(increment_script, 1, "test:counter:eval", str(i))

# Test EVALSHA  
def test_evalsha(i):
    return r.evalsha(inc_sha, 1, "test:counter:evalsha", str(i))

eval_time = time_operation(test_eval, 50)
evalsha_time = time_operation(test_evalsha, 50)

print(f"EVAL time: {eval_time:.3f}s")
print(f"EVALSHA time: {evalsha_time:.3f}s") 
print(f"EVALSHA is {eval_time/evalsha_time:.1f}x faster")
EVAL time: 0.234s EVALSHA time: 0.156s EVALSHA is 1.5x faster
Example 4: Error handling with EVALSHA
# Safe EVALSHA with fallback to EVAL
def safe_evalsha(redis_client, script_code, script_sha, keys, args):
    try:
        # Try EVALSHA first
        return redis_client.evalsha(script_sha, len(keys), *keys, *args)
    except redis.exceptions.NoScriptError:
        # Script not in cache, use EVAL and reload
        print("Script not in cache, reloading...")
        new_sha = redis_client.script_load(script_code)
        return redis_client.evalsha(new_sha, len(keys), *keys, *args)

# Usage with error handling
cleanup_script = """
local pattern = ARGV[1]
local limit = tonumber(ARGV[2])

local keys = redis.call('KEYS', pattern)
local deleted = 0

for i = 1, math.min(#keys, limit) do
    redis.call('DEL', keys[i])
    deleted = deleted + 1
end

return deleted
"""

cleanup_sha = "nonexistent_sha_for_demo"  # Simulate missing script

# This will fallback to EVAL automatically
deleted = safe_evalsha(r, cleanup_script, cleanup_sha, 
                      [], ["temp:*", "10"])
print(f"Deleted {deleted} temporary keys")
Script not in cache, reloading... Deleted 7 temporary keys
Example 5: Script versioning and management
# Script version management
class ScriptManager:
    def __init__(self, redis_client):
        self.redis = redis_client
        self.scripts = {}
    
    def load_script(self, name, code, version="1.0"):
        script_key = f"{name}:v{version}"
        sha = self.redis.script_load(code)
        self.scripts[script_key] = {
            'sha': sha,
            'code': code,
            'version': version
        }
        return sha
    
    def execute(self, name, version, keys, args):
        script_key = f"{name}:v{version}"
        if script_key not in self.scripts:
            raise ValueError(f"Script {script_key} not loaded")
        
        script_info = self.scripts[script_key]
        return self.redis.evalsha(script_info['sha'], len(keys), *keys, *args)

# Initialize script manager
sm = ScriptManager(r)

# Load different versions of the same script
job_matcher_v1 = """
-- Simple matching logic
local score = tonumber(ARGV[1]) or 50
return score
"""

job_matcher_v2 = """
-- Enhanced matching with multiple factors
local base_score = tonumber(ARGV[1]) or 50
local experience_bonus = tonumber(ARGV[2]) or 0
local skill_bonus = tonumber(ARGV[3]) or 0
return base_score + experience_bonus + skill_bonus
"""

# Load both versions
sm.load_script("job_matcher", job_matcher_v1, "1.0")
sm.load_script("job_matcher", job_matcher_v2, "2.0")

# Use different versions
score_v1 = sm.execute("job_matcher", "1.0", [], ["75"])
score_v2 = sm.execute("job_matcher", "2.0", [], ["75", "10", "15"])

print(f"Score v1.0: {score_v1}")
print(f"Score v2.0: {score_v2}")
Score v1.0: 75 Score v2.0: 100
💡 Implementation Note: The remaining Scripting and Functions commands (EVAL_RO, EVALSHA_RO, FCALL, FCALL_RO, FUNCTION DELETE, FUNCTION DUMP, FUNCTION FLUSH, FUNCTION KILL, FUNCTION LIST, FUNCTION RESTORE, FUNCTION STATS, SCRIPT DEBUG, SCRIPT EXISTS, SCRIPT FLUSH, SCRIPT KILL, SCRIPT LOAD) follow similar patterns. Each provides specific functionality for script/function lifecycle management, debugging, performance optimization, and advanced execution control.

Redis Query Engine Commands

Redis Query Engine (RediSearch) provides powerful full-text search, secondary indexing, and complex aggregations directly within Redis. Perfect for Django applications that need advanced search capabilities, real-time analytics, and complex queries without additional search infrastructure like Elasticsearch.

🔍 Query Engine Commands Overview

FT.CREATE - Create search indexes
FT.SEARCH - Execute search queries
FT.AGGREGATE - Run aggregation queries
FT.INFO - Get index information
FT.DROPINDEX - Delete indexes
FT.ALTER - Modify index schema
FT.ALIASADD - Add index aliases
FT.ALIASDEL - Delete index aliases
FT.ALIASUPDATE - Update index aliases
FT.SPELLCHECK - Spelling correction
FT.DICTADD - Add dictionary terms
FT.DICTDEL - Delete dictionary terms
FT.DICTDUMP - Export dictionary
FT.TAGVALS - Get tag values
FT.SYNUPDATE - Update synonyms
FT.SYNDUMP - Export synonyms
FT.PROFILE - Query profiling
FT.EXPLAIN - Query execution plan
FT._LIST - List all indexes
🎯 Perfect for Django: Use RediSearch for job search functionality, user profile matching, content discovery, real-time analytics dashboards, and any scenario requiring complex queries, full-text search, or aggregations without additional search infrastructure.

FT.CREATE

Docs: Redis FT.CREATE Documentation

Creates an index with the given spec.

Full description

FT.CREATE creates a search index on Redis data structures like hashes or JSON documents. You define fields to index with their types (text, numeric, tag, geo, vector) and search capabilities. Essential for Django applications that need to search across job postings, user profiles, or any complex data without external search engines.

Syntax

FT.CREATE index_name [ON HASH | JSON] [PREFIX count prefix ...] SCHEMA field_name field_type [options] ...

Parameters

  • index_name: Name of the index to create (string).
  • [ON HASH | JSON]: Index Redis hashes or JSON documents.
  • [PREFIX count prefix ...]: Key prefixes to index (e.g., "job:", "user:").
  • SCHEMA: Field definitions with types and options.

Return value

String: "OK" if the index was created successfully.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Create job search index
import redis
from redisearch import IndexDefinition, TextField, NumericField, TagField

r = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, decode_responses=True)

# Create comprehensive job search index
create_job_index = """
FT.CREATE job_search_idx 
ON HASH 
PREFIX 1 job: 
SCHEMA 
    title TEXT WEIGHT 2.0 SORTABLE
    description TEXT 
    company TEXT WEIGHT 1.5 SORTABLE
    location TEXT SORTABLE
    salary NUMERIC SORTABLE
    type TAG SORTABLE
    skills TAG SEPARATOR ','
    posted_date NUMERIC SORTABLE
    status TAG
"""

try:
    result = r.execute_command(*create_job_index.strip().split())
    print(f"Job index created: {result}")
    
    # Index some sample job data
    job_data = {
        "title": "Senior Python Developer",
        "description": "Join our team to build scalable Django applications with Redis integration",
        "company": "TechCorp", 
        "location": "San Francisco",
        "salary": "120000",
        "type": "Full Time",
        "skills": "Python,Django,Redis,PostgreSQL",
        "posted_date": "1693497600",
        "status": "active"
    }
    
    r.hset("job:1", mapping=job_data)
    print("Sample job indexed")
    
except Exception as e:
    print(f"Index creation result: OK (already exists)")
    print("Sample data added")
Job index created: OK Sample job indexed

FT.SEARCH

Docs: Redis FT.SEARCH Documentation

Searches the index with a textual query, returning either documents or just ids.

Full description

FT.SEARCH performs full-text searches with support for boolean logic, phrase matching, fuzzy search, numeric ranges, geographic searches, and more. Returns scored results with optional highlighting and pagination. Essential for implementing Django search features like job discovery, user matching, or content search.

Syntax

FT.SEARCH index_name query [options...]

Parameters

  • index_name: Name of the index to search (string).
  • query: Search query with optional operators and filters.
  • [options]: LIMIT, SORTBY, RETURN, HIGHLIGHT, etc.

Return value

Array: Search results with scores and field values.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Basic job search with filters
# Search for Python jobs in specific salary range
search_cmd = [
    "FT.SEARCH", "job_search_idx",
    "python django",  # Basic text search
    "FILTER", "salary", "80000", "150000",  # Salary range
    "FILTER", "status", "active", "active",  # Only active jobs
    "SORTBY", "salary", "DESC",  # Sort by salary descending
    "LIMIT", "0", "10",  # First 10 results
    "RETURN", "6", "title", "company", "salary", "location", "skills", "posted_date"
]

results = r.execute_command(*search_cmd)
total_results = results[0]
print(f"Found {total_results} Python Django jobs")

# Process results (every 2 elements: doc_id, fields)
for i in range(1, len(results), 2):
    doc_id = results[i]
    fields = results[i + 1]
    
    # Convert field list to dict
    field_dict = {}
    for j in range(0, len(fields), 2):
        field_dict[fields[j]] = fields[j + 1]
    
    print(f"Job: {field_dict.get('title')} at {field_dict.get('company')}")
    print(f"  Salary: ${field_dict.get('salary')} | Location: {field_dict.get('location')}")
    print(f"  Skills: {field_dict.get('skills')}")
    print("---")
Found 15 Python Django jobs Job: Senior Python Developer at TechCorp Salary: $120000 | Location: San Francisco Skills: Python,Django,Redis,PostgreSQL --- Job: Django Backend Engineer at StartupXYZ Salary: $110000 | Location: Remote Skills: Python,Django,AWS,Docker ---
Example 2: Complex boolean search with highlighting
# Advanced search with boolean operators and highlighting
advanced_search = [
    "FT.SEARCH", "job_search_idx",
    "(python | javascript) (senior | lead) -intern",  # Boolean logic
    "HIGHLIGHT", 
    "FIELDS", "2", "title", "description",
    "TAGS", "", "",
    "SORTBY", "posted_date", "DESC",
    "LIMIT", "0", "5"
]

results = r.execute_command(*advanced_search)
print(f"Advanced search found {results[0]} results")

for i in range(1, len(results), 2):
    doc_id = results[i] 
    fields = results[i + 1]
    
    field_dict = {}
    for j in range(0, len(fields), 2):
        field_dict[fields[j]] = fields[j + 1]
    
    print(f"\nPosition: {field_dict.get('title', '')}")
    print(f"Description: {field_dict.get('description', '')[:100]}...")
    
    # Show highlighting if present
    if 'title' in field_dict and '' in field_dict['title']:
        print(f"Highlighted: {field_dict['title']}")
Advanced search found 8 results Position: Senior Python Developer Description: Join our team to build scalable Django applications with Redis integration... Highlighted: Senior Python Developer Position: Lead JavaScript Engineer Description: Looking for experienced frontend leader to guide our React and Node.js team...
Example 3: Geographic search for nearby users
# Find users within geographic radius
geo_search = [
    "FT.SEARCH", "user_profile_idx",
    "@location:[-122.4194 37.7749 10 km]",  # Within 10km of SF
    "FILTER", "experience_years", "3", "10",  # 3-10 years experience
    "SORTBY", "last_active", "DESC",  # Most recently active first
    "LIMIT", "0", "5",
    "RETURN", "4", "username", "skills", "experience_years", "location"
]

    fields = results[i + 1]
    field_dict = {}
    for j in range(0, len(fields), 2):
        field_dict[fields[j]] = fields[j + 1]
    
    print(f"User: {field_dict.get('username')}")
    print(f"  Experience: {field_dict.get('experience_years')} years")
    print(f"  Skills: {field_dict.get('skills')}")
    print(f"  Location: {field_dict.get('location')}")
    print("---")
Found 12 nearby experienced users User: johndev Experience: 5 years Skills: Python,Django,JavaScript,Redis,AWS Location: -122.4194,37.7749 --- User: sarahcode Experience: 7 years Skills: React,Node.js,Python,MongoDB Location: -122.4094,37.7849 ---
Example 4: Fuzzy search with spell tolerance
# Fuzzy search to handle typos and variations
fuzzy_search = [
    "FT.SEARCH", "job_search_idx", 
    "%pythom% %djago%",  # Fuzzy matching for "python" and "django"
    "SORTBY", "salary", "DESC",
    "LIMIT", "0", "3",
    "RETURN", "3", "title", "description", "skills"
]

results = r.execute_command(*fuzzy_search)
print(f"Fuzzy search found {results[0]} results")

# Also try exact phrase search
phrase_search = [
    "FT.SEARCH", "job_search_idx",
    '"full stack developer"',  # Exact phrase
    "SORTBY", "posted_date", "DESC",
    "LIMIT", "0", "3"
]

phrase_results = r.execute_command(*phrase_search)
print(f"Phrase search found {phrase_results[0]} results")

# Demonstrate wildcard search
wildcard_search = [
    "FT.SEARCH", "job_search_idx",
    "develop*",  # Matches developer, development, etc.
    "FILTER", "type", "Full Time", "Full Time",
    "LIMIT", "0", "5"
]

wildcard_results = r.execute_command(*wildcard_search)
print(f"Wildcard search found {wildcard_results[0]} results")
Fuzzy search found 18 results Phrase search found 6 results Wildcard search found 23 results
Example 5: Multi-field search with custom scoring
# Search across multiple fields with different weights
weighted_search = [
    "FT.SEARCH", "job_search_idx",
    "@title:(senior python) | @description:(django redis) | @skills:(aws cloud)",
    "WITHSCORES",  # Include relevance scores
    "SORTBY", "_score", "DESC",  # Sort by relevance
    "LIMIT", "0", "5",
    "RETURN", "5", "title", "company", "description", "skills", "salary"
]

results = r.execute_command(*weighted_search)
print(f"Weighted search found {results[0]} results")

# Process results with scores
for i in range(1, len(results), 3):  # Every 3 elements: doc_id, score, fields
    doc_id = results[i]
    score = results[i + 1]
    fields = results[i + 2]
    
    field_dict = {}
    for j in range(0, len(fields), 2):
        field_dict[fields[j]] = fields[j + 1]
    
    print(f"\nScore: {score}")
    print(f"Job: {field_dict.get('title')} at {field_dict.get('company')}")
    print(f"Skills match: {field_dict.get('skills')}")

# Tag-based filtering for precise matches
tag_search = [
    "FT.SEARCH", "job_search_idx",
    "@type:{Full Time} @status:{active}",  # Tag filters
    "SORTBY", "salary", "DESC", 
    "LIMIT", "0", "10"
]

tag_results = r.execute_command(*tag_search)
print(f"\nTag-filtered search: {tag_results[0]} active full-time jobs")
Weighted search found 12 results Score: 2.8 Job: Senior Python Developer at TechCorp Skills match: Python,Django,Redis,PostgreSQL Score: 2.1 Job: Django Backend Engineer at StartupXYZ Skills match: Python,Django,AWS,Docker Tag-filtered search: 28 active full-time jobs

FT.AGGREGATE

Docs: Redis FT.AGGREGATE Documentation

Run a search query on an index and perform aggregate transformations on the results.

Full description

FT.AGGREGATE performs complex analytics and aggregations on search results, similar to SQL GROUP BY operations. Supports grouping, counting, summing, averaging, sorting, and data transformations. Perfect for Django analytics dashboards, reporting, and business intelligence without additional tools.

Syntax

FT.AGGREGATE index_name query [options...] GROUPBY fields REDUCE functions...

Parameters

  • index_name: Name of the index to aggregate (string).
  • query: Search query to filter data.
  • GROUPBY: Fields to group results by.
  • REDUCE: Aggregation functions (COUNT, SUM, AVG, etc.).

Return value

Array: Aggregated results with computed values.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Job market analytics by location
# Aggregate job statistics by location
location_analytics = [
    "FT.AGGREGATE", "job_search_idx",
    "*",  # All jobs
    "GROUPBY", "1", "@location",
    "REDUCE", "COUNT", "0", "AS", "job_count",
    "REDUCE", "AVG", "1", "@salary", "AS", "avg_salary", 
    "REDUCE", "MAX", "1", "@salary", "AS", "max_salary",
    "REDUCE", "MIN", "1", "@salary", "AS", "min_salary",
    "SORTBY", "2", "@job_count", "DESC",
    "LIMIT", "0", "10"
]

results = r.execute_command(*location_analytics)
print("Job Market Analytics by Location:")
print("=" * 50)

# Process aggregation results
for i in range(1, len(results)):  # Skip count at index 0
    row = results[i]
    row_dict = {}
    for j in range(0, len(row), 2):
        row_dict[row[j]] = row[j + 1]
    
    location = row_dict.get('location', 'Unknown')
    job_count = row_dict.get('job_count', 0)
    avg_salary = float(row_dict.get('avg_salary', 0))
    max_salary = row_dict.get('max_salary', 0)
    min_salary = row_dict.get('min_salary', 0)
    
    print(f"Location: {location}")
    print(f"  Jobs Available: {job_count}")
    print(f"  Avg Salary: ${avg_salary:,.0f}")
    print(f"  Salary Range: ${min_salary} - ${max_salary}")
    print("---")
Job Market Analytics by Location: ================================================== Location: San Francisco Jobs Available: 45 Avg Salary: $128,500 Salary Range: $85000 - $180000 --- Location: New York Jobs Available: 38 Avg Salary: $122,300 Salary Range: $90000 - $165000 --- Location: Remote Jobs Available: 52 Avg Salary: $115,700 Salary Range: $75000 - $155000 ---
Example 2: Skills demand analysis
# Analyze most in-demand skills across job postings
skills_analysis = [
    "FT.AGGREGATE", "job_search_idx",
    "@status:{active}",  # Only active jobs
    "APPLY", "split", "1", "@skills", "AS", "skill_list",  # Split skills by comma
    "APPLY", "substr", "2", "@skill_list", "0", "-1", "AS", "individual_skill",
    "GROUPBY", "1", "@individual_skill", 
    "REDUCE", "COUNT", "0", "AS", "skill_demand",
    "REDUCE", "AVG", "1", "@salary", "AS", "avg_salary_for_skill",
    "SORTBY", "2", "@skill_demand", "DESC",
    "LIMIT", "0", "10"
]

# Alternative approach using simpler aggregation
simple_skills_analysis = [
    "FT.AGGREGATE", "job_search_idx",
    "python OR django OR javascript OR react OR aws",
    "GROUPBY", "1", "@skills",
    "REDUCE", "COUNT", "0", "AS", "jobs_with_skillset", 
    "REDUCE", "AVG", "1", "@salary", "AS", "avg_salary",
    "SORTBY", "2", "@jobs_with_skillset", "DESC",
    "LIMIT", "0", "8"
]

results = r.execute_command(*simple_skills_analysis)
print("Skills Demand Analysis:")
print("=" * 40)

for i in range(1, len(results)):
    row = results[i]
    row_dict = {}
    for j in range(0, len(row), 2):
        row_dict[row[j]] = row[j + 1]
    
    skills = row_dict.get('skills', 'Unknown')
    job_count = row_dict.get('jobs_with_skillset', 0)
    avg_salary = float(row_dict.get('avg_salary', 0))
    
    print(f"Skillset: {skills}")
    print(f"  Jobs requiring: {job_count}")
    print(f"  Average salary: ${avg_salary:,.0f}")
    print("---")
Skills Demand Analysis: ======================================== Skillset: Python,Django,Redis,PostgreSQL Jobs requiring: 28 Average salary: $125,400 --- Skillset: JavaScript,React,Node.js Jobs requiring: 22 Average salary: $118,700 --- Skillset: Python,Django,AWS,Docker Jobs requiring: 19 Average salary: $132,100 ---

Example 4: Salary distribution analysis
# Create salary ranges and analyze distribution
salary_distribution = [
    "FT.AGGREGATE", "job_search_idx",
    "*",  # All jobs
    # Create salary ranges using APPLY
    "APPLY", "floor(@salary/20000)*20000", "AS", "salary_bucket",
    "GROUPBY", "1", "@salary_bucket",
    "REDUCE", "COUNT", "0", "AS", "job_count",
    "REDUCE", "AVG", "1", "@salary", "AS", "avg_in_bucket",
    "SORTBY", "2", "@salary_bucket", "ASC",
    "LIMIT", "0", "10"
]

results = r.execute_command(*salary_distribution)
print("Salary Distribution Analysis:")
print("=" * 40)

for i in range(1, len(results)):
    row = results[i]
    row_dict = {}
    for j in range(0, len(row), 2):
        row_dict[row[j]] = row[j + 1]
    
    bucket = int(float(row_dict.get('salary_bucket', 0)))
    count = row_dict.get('job_count', 0)
    avg_in_bucket = float(row_dict.get('avg_in_bucket', 0))
    
    range_end = bucket + 19999
    print(f"Salary Range: ${bucket:,} - ${range_end:,}")
    print(f"  Job count: {count}")
    print(f"  Average: ${avg_in_bucket:,.0f}")
    print("---")
Salary Distribution Analysis: ======================================== Salary Range: $60,000 - $79,999 Job count: 8 Average: $72,500 --- Salary Range: $80,000 - $99,999 Job count: 15 Average: $91,200 --- Salary Range: $100,000 - $119,999 Job count: 22 Average: $108,700 --- Salary Range: $120,000 - $139,999 Job count: 18 Average: $128,400 ---
Example 5: Time-based posting trends
import time
from datetime import datetime

# Analyze job posting patterns over time
time_trends = [
    "FT.AGGREGATE", "job_search_idx", 
    "*",  # All jobs
    # Group by month (convert timestamp to month)
    "APPLY", "timefmt(@posted_date, '%Y-%m')", "AS", "posting_month",
    "GROUPBY", "1", "@posting_month",
    "REDUCE", "COUNT", "0", "AS", "jobs_posted",
    "REDUCE", "AVG", "1", "@salary", "AS", "avg_monthly_salary",
    "SORTBY", "2", "@posting_month", "DESC",
    "LIMIT", "0", "6"
]

# Simpler version using numeric grouping
monthly_trends = [
    "FT.AGGREGATE", "job_search_idx",
    "@status:{active}",  # Active jobs only
    # Create monthly buckets from timestamps  
    "APPLY", "floor(@posted_date/2629746)*2629746", "AS", "month_bucket", # ~30 days
    "GROUPBY", "1", "@month_bucket", 
    "REDUCE", "COUNT", "0", "AS", "jobs_posted",
    "REDUCE", "COUNT_DISTINCT", "1", "@company", "AS", "unique_companies",
    "REDUCE", "AVG", "1", "@salary", "AS", "avg_salary",
    "SORTBY", "2", "@month_bucket", "DESC",
    "LIMIT", "0", "3"
]

results = r.execute_command(*monthly_trends)
print("Monthly Job Posting Trends:")
print("=" * 35)

for i in range(1, len(results)):
    row = results[i]
    row_dict = {}
    for j in range(0, len(row), 2):
        row_dict[row[j]] = row[j + 1]
    
    bucket_timestamp = int(float(row_dict.get('month_bucket', 0)))
    jobs_posted = row_dict.get('jobs_posted', 0)
    companies = row_dict.get('unique_companies', 0)
    avg_salary = float(row_dict.get('avg_salary', 0))
    
    # Convert timestamp to readable date
    date_str = datetime.fromtimestamp(bucket_timestamp).strftime('%Y-%m')
    
    print(f"Month: {date_str}")
    print(f"  Jobs posted: {jobs_posted}")
    print(f"  Companies hiring: {companies}")
    print(f"  Avg salary: ${avg_salary:,.0f}")
    print("---")
Monthly Job Posting Trends: =================================== Month: 2024-08 Jobs posted: 34 Companies hiring: 18 Avg salary: $124,500 --- Month: 2024-07 Jobs posted: 28 Companies hiring: 15 Avg salary: $119,200 --- Month: 2024-06 Jobs posted: 31 Companies hiring: 20 Avg salary: $126,800 ---
💡 Implementation Note: The remaining Redis Query Engine commands (FT.INFO, FT.DROPINDEX, FT.ALTER, FT.ALIASADD, FT.ALIASDEL, FT.ALIASUPDATE, FT.SPELLCHECK, FT.DICTADD, FT.DICTDEL, FT.DICTDUMP, FT.TAGVALS, FT.SYNUPDATE, FT.SYNDUMP, FT.PROFILE, FT.EXPLAIN, FT._LIST, etc.) provide comprehensive search infrastructure management, performance optimization, and advanced query capabilities for building production-ready search applications. Note that FT.SUG* auto-complete commands are now available in the dedicated Auto-suggest section.

Server Management Commands

Redis Server Management commands provide comprehensive tools for administration, monitoring, security, and operational maintenance. Essential for Django production environments where you need to manage Redis clusters, monitor performance, handle security, and ensure reliable operations.

🔧 Server Management Commands Overview

INFO - Server statistics and info
CONFIG GET/SET - Runtime configuration
ACL SETUSER - User access control
ACL LIST - List ACL rules
MONITOR - Real-time command monitoring
SLOWLOG - Query performance analysis
MEMORY USAGE - Memory diagnostics
LATENCY - Performance monitoring
BGSAVE - Background data persistence
FLUSHDB/FLUSHALL - Data cleanup
REPLICAOF - Replication management
ROLE - Server role information
TIME - Server time sync
DBSIZE - Database size metrics
COMMAND - Command information
🎯 Perfect for Django: Use these commands for monitoring Django application Redis usage, setting up secure access for different environments, performance tuning, automated backups, and ensuring Redis reliability in production deployments.

INFO

Docs: Redis INFO Documentation

Returns information and statistics about the server.

Full description

INFO provides comprehensive server statistics including memory usage, client connections, replication status, persistence info, and performance metrics. Essential for Django production monitoring, capacity planning, and troubleshooting Redis performance issues.

Syntax

INFO [section]

Parameters

  • [section]: Optional section name (server, memory, persistence, stats, replication, etc.).

Return value

String: Detailed server information in key:value format.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Monitor Django application Redis usage

Example 5: Complete Django monitoring dashboard
from datetime import datetime
import time

def create_redis_monitoring_report():
    """Generate comprehensive Redis monitoring report for Django applications"""
    
    info = r.info()
    
    # System overview
    system_info = {
        "redis_version": info['redis_version'],
        "uptime_days": round(info['uptime_in_seconds'] / 86400, 1),
        "tcp_port": info['tcp_port'],
        "process_id": info['process_id']
    }
    
    # Resource usage
    resources = {
        "memory_usage_mb": round(info['used_memory'] / 1024 / 1024, 2),
        "cpu_used_sys": info['used_cpu_sys'],
        "cpu_used_user": info['used_cpu_user'],
        "connected_clients": info['connected_clients']
    }
    
    # Data statistics
    data_stats = {
        "total_keys": sum([int(val.split(',')[0].split('=')[1]) for key, val in info.items() if key.startswith('db')]),
        "expired_keys": info['expired_keys'],
        "evicted_keys": info['evicted_keys'],
        "keyspace_hit_rate": round(info['keyspace_hits'] / (info['keyspace_hits'] + info['keyspace_misses']) * 100, 2) if (info['keyspace_hits'] + info['keyspace_misses']) > 0 else 0
    }
    
    report_time = datetime.now().strftime("%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S")
    
    print(f"Django Redis Monitoring Report - {report_time}")
    print("=" * 55)
    
    print("\n🖥️  System Information:")
    for key, value in system_info.items():
        print(f"  {key.replace('_', ' ').title()}: {value}")
    
    print("\n📊 Resource Usage:")
    for key, value in resources.items():
        print(f"  {key.replace('_', ' ').title()}: {value}")
    
    print("\n💾 Data Statistics:")
    for key, value in data_stats.items():
        print(f"  {key.replace('_', ' ').title()}: {value}")
    
    # Health indicators
    print("\n🏥 Health Indicators:")
    if info['connected_clients'] > 100:
        print("  ⚠️  High client connections")
    if data_stats['keyspace_hit_rate'] < 80:
        print("  ⚠️  Low cache hit rate")
    if resources['memory_usage_mb'] > 1000:
        print("  ⚠️  High memory usage")
    else:
        print("  ✅ All systems normal")

# Generate report
create_redis_monitoring_report()

ACL SETUSER

Docs: Redis ACL SETUSER Documentation

Creates and modifies an ACL user and its rules.

Full description

ACL SETUSER creates or modifies Access Control List users with specific permissions, passwords, and command restrictions. Essential for Django production security where different application components need different levels of Redis access - read-only cache access, write permissions for sessions, or admin access for monitoring.

Syntax

ACL SETUSER username [rules...]

Parameters

  • username: The username to create or modify (string).
  • [rules...]: ACL rules like passwords, command permissions, key patterns.

Return value

String: "OK" if the user was successfully created or modified.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Django application read-only user
# Create read-only user for Django cache access
readonly_user = [
    "ACL", "SETUSER", "django_readonly",
    "on",  # Enable user
    ">django_cache_pass123",  # Set password
    "+get", "+mget", "+exists", "+ttl", "+type",  # Allow read commands
    "+hget", "+hgetall", "+hmget",  # Hash read commands
    "+lrange", "+llen", "+lindex",  # List read commands
    "+smembers", "+scard", "+sismember",  # Set read commands  
    "+zrange", "+zcard", "+zscore",  # Sorted set read commands
    "~cache:*", "~session:*",  # Only access cache and session keys
    "-@all"  # Deny all other commands
]

result = r.execute_command(*readonly_user)
print(f"Django read-only user created: {result}")

# Verify user creation
user_info = r.execute_command("ACL", "GETUSER", "django_readonly")
print(f"User permissions: {user_info}")

# Test connection with read-only user
try:
    ro_redis = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0, 
                          username='django_readonly', password='django_cache_pass123',
                          decode_responses=True)
    
    # This should work
    ro_redis.set("cache:test", "readable")  # This will fail
except redis.exceptions.NoPermissionError:
    print("✅ Read-only restrictions working correctly")

Example 3: Environment-specific user setup
# Setup different users for different Django environments
environments = {
    "development": {
        "password": "dev_pass_123",
        "permissions": ["+@all", "~*"],  # Full access in dev
    },
    "staging": {
        "password": "staging_pass_456", 
        "permissions": ["+@read", "+@write", "~staging:*", "-flushall", "-shutdown"]
    },
    "production": {
        "password": "prod_pass_789",
        "permissions": [
            "+get", "+set", "+del", "+exists", "+expire",
            "+hget", "+hset", "+hdel", "+hgetall",
            "~cache:*", "~session:*", "~prod:*",
            "-@dangerous"
        ]
    }
}

for env, config in environments.items():
    user_cmd = [
        "ACL", "SETUSER", f"django_{env}",
        "on", 
        f">{config['password']}"
    ] + config['permissions'] + ["-@all" if env == "production" else ""]
    
    # Remove empty strings
    user_cmd = [cmd for cmd in user_cmd if cmd]
    
    result = r.execute_command(*user_cmd)
    print(f"Created {env} user: {result}")

# Show environment users
env_users = r.execute_command("ACL", "LIST")
print(f"\nEnvironment users configured:")
for user_rule in env_users:
    if 'django_' in user_rule:
        username = user_rule.split()[1]
        print(f"  {username}")

Example 5: Dynamic user management for Django
# Dynamic ACL management class for Django applications
import hashlib
import secrets

class DjangoRedisACL:
    def __init__(self, redis_client):
        self.redis = redis_client
    
    def create_app_user(self, app_name, permissions_level="read"):
        """Create user for Django app with specified permission level"""
        
        # Generate secure password
        password = secrets.token_urlsafe(16)
        
        permission_templates = {
            "read": ["+get", "+mget", "+exists", "+hget", "+hgetall", "+lrange", "+smembers"],
            "write": ["+get", "+set", "+del", "+exists", "+expire", "+hset", "+hdel"],
            "full": ["+@all", "-flushall", "-shutdown", "-debug"]
        }
        
        username = f"django_{app_name}"
        commands = permission_templates.get(permissions_level, permission_templates["read"])
        
        user_cmd = [
            "ACL", "SETUSER", username,
            "on", f">{password}"
        ] + commands + [f"~{app_name}:*"] + (["-@all"] if permissions_level != "full" else [])
        
        self.redis.execute_command(*user_cmd)
        
        return {
            "username": username,
            "password": password,
            "permissions": permissions_level
        }
    
    def list_django_users(self):
        """List all Django ACL users"""
        users = self.redis.execute_command("ACL", "USERS")
        return [user for user in users if user.startswith('django_')]
    
    def remove_user(self, username):
        """Safely remove Django user"""
        if username.startswith('django_'):
            return self.redis.execute_command("ACL", "DELUSER", username)
        return False

# Usage example
acl_manager = DjangoRedisACL(r)

# Create users for different Django apps
apps = ["blog", "ecommerce", "analytics"]
created_users = []

for app in apps:
    user_info = acl_manager.create_app_user(app, "write")
    created_users.append(user_info)
    print(f"Created user for {app}: {user_info['username']}")

print(f"\nAll Django users: {acl_manager.list_django_users()}")

# Store credentials securely (in production, use Django settings or env vars)
print(f"\nGenerated credentials for Django settings:")
for user in created_users:
    print(f"REDIS_{user['username'].upper()}_PASSWORD = '{user['password']}'")
Created user for blog: django_blog Created user for ecommerce: django_ecommerce Created user for analytics: django_analytics All Django users: ['django_blog', 'django_ecommerce', 'django_analytics', 'django_readonly', 'django_sessions', 'django_admin'] Generated credentials for Django settings: REDIS_DJANGO_BLOG_PASSWORD = 'K8mP2nQ7vR9xT3wY6zA1sD4f' REDIS_DJANGO_ECOMMERCE_PASSWORD = 'M5hL8jK2pN6qU9zX3cV7bG4n' REDIS_DJANGO_ANALYTICS_PASSWORD = 'P7rT1wQ4yE9vB5nM8kH2jL6s'

SLOWLOG GET

Docs: Redis SLOWLOG GET Documentation

Returns the slow log's entries.

Full description

SLOWLOG GET retrieves entries from Redis slow query log, showing commands that took longer than the configured threshold to execute. Essential for Django performance optimization, identifying bottleneck operations, and optimizing Redis usage patterns in your application.

Syntax

SLOWLOG GET [count]

Parameters

  • [count]: Optional maximum number of entries to return (integer).

Return value

Array: List of slow log entries with timestamp, duration, command, and arguments.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Identify Django performance bottlenecks
# Configure slow log threshold (microseconds)
r.config_set("slowlog-log-slower-than", "10000")  # 10ms threshold
print("Slow log threshold set to 10ms")

# Get recent slow queries
slow_entries = r.slowlog_get(10)

print(f"Found {len(slow_entries)} slow queries:")
print("=" * 50)

for entry in slow_entries:
    log_id = entry[0]
    timestamp = entry[1] 
    duration_microseconds = entry[2]
    command_parts = entry[3]
    client_info = entry[4] if len(entry) > 4 else "N/A"
    
    # Convert to milliseconds for readability
    duration_ms = duration_microseconds / 1000
    
    # Reconstruct command (limit argument length for display)
    command = " ".join([str(arg)[:50] + "..." if len(str(arg)) > 50 else str(arg) 
                       for arg in command_parts])
    
    print(f"ID: {log_id}")
    print(f"Duration: {duration_ms:.2f}ms")
    print(f"Command: {command}")
    print(f"Client: {client_info}")
    print("-" * 30)

# Performance summary
if slow_entries:
    avg_duration = sum([entry[2] for entry in slow_entries]) / len(slow_entries) / 1000
    max_duration = max([entry[2] for entry in slow_entries]) / 1000
    print(f"\n📊 Performance Summary:")
    print(f"Average slow query: {avg_duration:.2f}ms")
    print(f"Slowest query: {max_duration:.2f}ms")
Slow log threshold set to 10ms Found 3 slow queries: ================================================== ID: 15 Duration: 45.23ms Command: FT.SEARCH job_search_idx python django FILTER salary... Client: addr=127.0.0.1:52341 fd=8 name= age=145 ID: 14 Duration: 23.45ms Command: ZUNIONSTORE temp:ranking:jobs 3 jobs:python jobs:django... Client: addr=127.0.0.1:52341 fd=8 name= age=145 ID: 13 Duration: 15.67ms Command: KEYS cache:job:* Client: addr=127.0.0.1:52341 fd=8 name= age=145 ------------------------------ 📊 Performance Summary: Average slow query: 28.12ms Slowest query: 45.23ms
Example 2: Django-specific command analysis
# Analyze slow log for Django-specific patterns
slow_entries = r.slowlog_get(50)  # Get more entries for analysis

# Categorize slow commands by Django usage
django_patterns = {
    "cache_operations": ["GET cache:", "SET cache:", "DEL cache:"],
    "session_operations": ["GET session:", "SET session:", "EXPIRE session:"],
    "search_operations": ["FT.SEARCH", "FT.AGGREGATE"],
    "job_queue": ["LPUSH jobs:", "RPOP jobs:", "BRPOP jobs:"],
    "analytics": ["ZADD analytics:", "ZRANGE analytics:", "ZINCRBY analytics:"],
    "bulk_operations": ["KEYS", "MGET", "MSET", "DEL"]
}

categorized_slow = {category: [] for category in django_patterns.keys()}
uncategorized = []

for entry in slow_entries:
    command_parts = entry[3]
    full_command = " ".join([str(arg) for arg in command_parts])
    
    categorized = False
    for category, patterns in django_patterns.items():
        if any(pattern in full_command.upper() for pattern in patterns):
            categorized_slow[category].append({
                "duration_ms": entry[2] / 1000,
                "command": full_command[:100] + "..." if len(full_command) > 100 else full_command
            })
            categorized = True
            break
    
    if not categorized:
        uncategorized.append(full_command[:50])

print("Django Slow Query Analysis:")
print("=" * 35)

for category, entries in categorized_slow.items():
    if entries:
        avg_duration = sum([e["duration_ms"] for e in entries]) / len(entries)
        print(f"\n{category.replace('_', ' ').title()}: {len(entries)} queries")
        print(f"  Average duration: {avg_duration:.2f}ms")
        
        # Show worst offender
        worst = max(entries, key=lambda x: x["duration_ms"])
        print(f"  Slowest: {worst['duration_ms']:.2f}ms - {worst['command']}")

if uncategorized:
    print(f"\nUncategorized: {len(uncategorized)} queries")

Example 3: Slow query alerting for Django
import time
from datetime import datetime, timedelta

def check_django_performance_alerts():
    """Check for Django performance issues in Redis slow log"""
    
    # Get recent slow queries
    slow_entries = r.slowlog_get(20)
    
    # Performance thresholds
    CRITICAL_THRESHOLD = 100  # 100ms
    WARNING_THRESHOLD = 50    # 50ms
    
    alerts = {
        "critical": [],
        "warning": [],
        "info": []
    }
    
    for entry in slow_entries:
        duration_ms = entry[2] / 1000
        command_parts = entry[3]
        command = " ".join([str(arg)[:30] for arg in command_parts])
        
        if duration_ms >= CRITICAL_THRESHOLD:
            alerts["critical"].append({
                "duration": duration_ms,
                "command": command,
                "timestamp": entry[1]
            })
        elif duration_ms >= WARNING_THRESHOLD:
            alerts["warning"].append({
                "duration": duration_ms, 
                "command": command,
                "timestamp": entry[1]
            })
        else:
            alerts["info"].append({"duration": duration_ms, "command": command})
    
    # Generate alert report
    print(f"Django Redis Performance Alert - {datetime.now().strftime('%H:%M:%S')}")
    print("=" * 55)
    
    if alerts["critical"]:
        print(f"🚨 CRITICAL: {len(alerts['critical'])} slow queries > {CRITICAL_THRESHOLD}ms")
        for alert in alerts["critical"][:3]:  # Show top 3
            print(f"  {alert['duration']:.1f}ms: {alert['command']}")
    
    if alerts["warning"]:
        print(f"⚠️  WARNING: {len(alerts['warning'])} slow queries > {WARNING_THRESHOLD}ms")
        for alert in alerts["warning"][:2]:  # Show top 2
            print(f"  {alert['duration']:.1f}ms: {alert['command']}")
    
    if alerts["info"]:
        print(f"ℹ️  INFO: {len(alerts['info'])} minor slow queries")
    
    if not any(alerts.values()):
        print("✅ No performance issues detected")
    
    # Recommendations
    if alerts["critical"] or len(alerts["warning"]) > 5:
        print(f"\n💡 Recommendations:")
        print("  - Review Django ORM queries generating bulk operations")
        print("  - Consider Redis connection pooling")
        print("  - Check for N+1 cache query patterns")
        print("  - Monitor Django application load")

# Run performance check
check_django_performance_alerts()

Example 4: Historical slow query trend analysis
# Track slow query trends over time for Django optimization
import json
from collections import defaultdict

def analyze_slow_query_trends():
    """Analyze slow query patterns for Django performance tuning"""
    
    slow_entries = r.slowlog_get(100)  # Get more historical data
    
    # Group by command type
    command_stats = defaultdict(lambda: {"count": 0, "total_duration": 0, "max_duration": 0})
    
    for entry in slow_entries:
        duration_ms = entry[2] / 1000
        command_name = str(entry[3][0]).upper()
        
        stats = command_stats[command_name]
        stats["count"] += 1
        stats["total_duration"] += duration_ms
        stats["max_duration"] = max(stats["max_duration"], duration_ms)
    
    # Calculate averages and sort by impact
    for command, stats in command_stats.items():
        stats["avg_duration"] = stats["total_duration"] / stats["count"]
        stats["impact_score"] = stats["count"] * stats["avg_duration"]  # Frequency × Duration
    
    # Sort by impact score
    sorted_commands = sorted(command_stats.items(), 
                           key=lambda x: x[1]["impact_score"], reverse=True)
    
    print("Django Redis Slow Query Trend Analysis:")
    print("=" * 45)
    print(f"{'Command':<15} {'Count':<8} {'Avg(ms)':<10} {'Max(ms)':<10} {'Impact':<10}")
    print("-" * 65)
    
    for command, stats in sorted_commands[:10]:  # Top 10
        print(f"{command:<15} {stats['count']:<8} "
              f"{stats['avg_duration']:<10.1f} {stats['max_duration']:<10.1f} "
              f"{stats['impact_score']:<10.1f}")
    
    # Django-specific recommendations
    print(f"\n🔧 Django Optimization Recommendations:")
    
    top_command = sorted_commands[0][0] if sorted_commands else None
    if top_command:
        recommendations = {
            "KEYS": "Replace KEYS with SCAN for large datasets in Django",
            "MGET": "Optimize Django cache.get_many() batch size",
            "ZRANGE": "Add Redis pagination for Django leaderboards", 
            "FT.SEARCH": "Add Redis search query caching in Django",
            "HGETALL": "Use HMGET with specific fields in Django models"
        }
        
        if top_command in recommendations:
            print(f"  • {recommendations[top_command]}")
        
        print(f"  • Consider adding Redis query caching in Django")
        print(f"  • Review connection pooling configuration")

analyze_slow_query_trends()

Example 5: Automated Django performance monitoring
# Automated performance monitoring system for Django + Redis
class DjangoRedisMonitor:
    def __init__(self, redis_client, threshold_ms=20):
        self.redis = redis_client
        self.threshold_ms = threshold_ms
        self.performance_log = []
    
    def setup_monitoring(self):
        """Configure Redis for optimal Django monitoring"""
        # Set slow log parameters
        self.redis.config_set("slowlog-log-slower-than", str(self.threshold_ms * 1000))
        self.redis.config_set("slowlog-max-len", "200")
        print(f"✅ Monitoring configured: {self.threshold_ms}ms threshold")
    
    def get_performance_report(self):
        """Generate comprehensive performance report"""
        slow_entries = self.redis.slowlog_get(50)
        
        if not slow_entries:
            return {"status": "healthy", "slow_queries": 0}
        
        # Analyze performance metrics
        durations = [entry[2] / 1000 for entry in slow_entries]  # Convert to ms
        
        report = {
            "status": "needs_attention" if max(durations) > self.threshold_ms * 3 else "moderate",
            "slow_queries": len(slow_entries),
            "avg_duration_ms": round(sum(durations) / len(durations), 2),
            "max_duration_ms": round(max(durations), 2),
            "min_duration_ms": round(min(durations), 2),
            "commands_analyzed": {}
        }
        
        # Command breakdown
        command_count = {}
        for entry in slow_entries:
            cmd = str(entry[3][0]).upper()
            command_count[cmd] = command_count.get(cmd, 0) + 1
        
        report["commands_analyzed"] = dict(sorted(command_count.items(), 
                                                 key=lambda x: x[1], reverse=True))
        
        return report
    
    def health_check(self):
        """Quick Django Redis health check"""
        try:
            # Basic connectivity
            self.redis.ping()
            
            # Get key metrics
            info = self.redis.info()
            slow_count = self.redis.slowlog_len()
            
            health = {
                "redis_connected": True,
                "memory_usage_mb": round(info['used_memory'] / 1024 / 1024, 1),
                "connected_clients": info['connected_clients'],
                "slow_queries": slow_count,
                "hit_rate": round(info['keyspace_hits'] / (info['keyspace_hits'] + info['keyspace_misses']) * 100, 1) if (info['keyspace_hits'] + info['keyspace_misses']) > 0 else 100,
                "status": "healthy"
            }
            
            # Health assessment
            if slow_count > 20:
                health["status"] = "performance_issues"
            elif health["hit_rate"] < 80:
                health["status"] = "cache_issues"
            elif health["connected_clients"] > 50:
                health["status"] = "high_load"
                
            return health
            
        except Exception as e:
            return {"redis_connected": False, "error": str(e), "status": "critical"}

# Usage example
monitor = DjangoRedisMonitor(r, threshold_ms=15)
monitor.setup_monitoring()

# Get reports
health = monitor.health_check()
performance = monitor.get_performance_report()

print("Django Redis Health Check:")
print("=" * 30)
print(f"Status: {health['status']}")
print(f"Memory: {health['memory_usage_mb']}MB")
print(f"Clients: {health['connected_clients']}")
print(f"Hit Rate: {health['hit_rate']}%")

print(f"\nPerformance Analysis:")
print(f"Slow queries: {performance['slow_queries']}")
if performance['slow_queries'] > 0:
    print(f"Average: {performance['avg_duration_ms']}ms")
    print(f"Slowest: {performance['max_duration_ms']}ms")
    print(f"Top commands: {list(performance['commands_analyzed'].keys())[:3]}")

💡 Implementation Note: The remaining Server Management commands (CONFIG GET/SET, MEMORY USAGE, LATENCY DOCTOR, BGSAVE, FLUSHDB, REPLICAOF, ROLE, TIME, DBSIZE, MONITOR, etc.) provide comprehensive tools for Redis administration, security management, performance optimization, and operational maintenance in Django production environments.

🌊 Redis Stream Commands

Redis Streams provide a powerful data structure for handling continuous data flows and message queues. Perfect for Django applications requiring event streaming, real-time processing, job queues, activity feeds, and audit logs. Streams offer persistence, fan-out messaging, and consumer group functionality.

XADD - Add Entry to Stream

Purpose: Appends a new entry to a stream with automatic ID generation and field-value pairs.
Syntax: XADD key [NOMKSTREAM] [MAXLEN|MINID [=|~] threshold] *|ID field value [field value ...]
Parameters:
  • key - Stream name
  • NOMKSTREAM - Don't create stream if it doesn't exist
  • MAXLEN - Limit stream length
  • * - Auto-generate ID or specific ID
  • field value - Entry data pairs

Return: The ID of the added entry
Django Example 1: Job Application Events 
# views.py - Track job application lifecycle
import redis
from django.shortcuts import render
from django.utils import timezone
from .models import JobPost, User

redis_client = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

def apply_for_job(request, job_id):
    job = JobPost.objects.get(id=job_id)
    user = request.user
    
    # Add job application event to stream
    application_id = redis_client.xadd(
        'job_applications',
        {
            'event_type': 'application_submitted',
            'job_id': str(job.id),
            'job_title': job.title,
            'applicant_id': str(user.id),
            'applicant_email': user.email,
            'company': job.author.company,
            'salary': str(job.salary),
            'location': f"{job.location.city}, {job.location.state}",
            'timestamp': timezone.now().isoformat(),
            'source': 'web_portal'
        }
    )
    
    return render(request, 'application_success.html', {
        'job': job,
        'application_id': application_id
    })

Django Example 2: User Activity Audit Log 
# middleware.py - Track user activities
import redis
from django.utils.deprecation import MiddlewareMixin
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

redis_client = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

class ActivityTrackingMiddleware(MiddlewareMixin):
    def process_request(self, request):
        if request.user.is_authenticated and request.method in ['POST', 'PUT', 'DELETE']:
            # Track significant user actions
            redis_client.xadd(
                f'user_activity:{request.user.id}',
                {
                    'action': request.method,
                    'path': request.path,
                    'user_id': str(request.user.id),
                    'username': request.user.username,
                    'ip_address': request.META.get('REMOTE_ADDR', 'unknown'),
                    'user_agent': request.META.get('HTTP_USER_AGENT', 'unknown')[:200],
                    'session_key': request.session.session_key,
                    'timestamp': timezone.now().isoformat()
                },
                maxlen=1000  # Keep last 1000 activities per user
            )
        return None

Django Example 3: Real-time Notifications Stream 
# services/notification_service.py
import redis
from django.template.loader import render_to_string
from .models import Author, JobPost

redis_client = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

class NotificationService:
    @staticmethod
    def notify_new_job_posted(job_post):
        # Add notification to global notifications stream
        notification_id = redis_client.xadd(
            'notifications:job_posts',
            {
                'type': 'new_job',
                'job_id': str(job_post.id),
                'title': job_post.title,
                'company': job_post.author.company,
                'location': f"{job_post.location.city}, {job_post.location.state}",
                'salary': str(job_post.salary),
                'job_type': job_post.type,
                'skills_required': ','.join([skill.name for skill in job_post.skills.all()]),
                'posted_by': job_post.author.user.username,
                'description': job_post.description[:200],
                'url': f'/jobpost/jobs/{job_post.id}/',
                'priority': 'normal',
                'created_at': job_post.date.isoformat()
            }
        )
        
        # Also add to skill-specific streams for targeted notifications
        for skill in job_post.skills.all():
            redis_client.xadd(
                f'notifications:skill:{skill.name.lower()}',
                {
                    'type': 'job_match',
                    'job_id': str(job_post.id),
                    'title': job_post.title,
                    'company': job_post.author.company,
                    'skill_match': skill.name,
                    'salary': str(job_post.salary),
                    'notification_id': notification_id
                },
                maxlen=100  # Keep latest 100 per skill
            )
        
        return notification_id

Django Example 4: Performance Metrics Collection 
# middleware/performance_middleware.py
import redis
import time
from django.utils.deprecation import MiddlewareMixin

redis_client = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

class PerformanceTrackingMiddleware(MiddlewareMixin):
    def process_request(self, request):
        request._start_time = time.time()
        return None
    
    def process_response(self, request, response):
        if hasattr(request, '_start_time'):
            duration = time.time() - request._start_time
            
            # Log performance metrics to stream
            redis_client.xadd(
                'performance_metrics',
                {
                    'path': request.path,
                    'method': request.method,
                    'status_code': str(response.status_code),
                    'duration_ms': f"{duration * 1000:.2f}",
                    'view_name': getattr(request.resolver_match, 'view_name', 'unknown'),
                    'user_id': str(request.user.id) if request.user.is_authenticated else 'anonymous',
                    'query_count': str(getattr(response, '_query_count', 0)),
                    'content_length': str(len(response.content)) if hasattr(response, 'content') else '0',
                    'timestamp': time.time()
                },
                maxlen=10000  # Keep latest 10k requests
            )
        
        return response
Printout:

- Duration: 45.67ms

Django Example 5: Order Processing Workflow 
# services/order_service.py - E-commerce style job application workflow
import redis
from django.db import transaction
from django.utils import timezone
from .models import JobPost, User, Author

redis_client = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

class ApplicationWorkflowService:
    @staticmethod
    def start_application_workflow(job_id, user_id, cover_letter=None):
        with transaction.atomic():
            job = JobPost.objects.get(id=job_id)
            user = User.objects.get(id=user_id)
            
            workflow_id = f"application_workflow:{user_id}_{job_id}_{int(timezone.now().timestamp())}"
            
            # Start workflow stream
            initial_entry = redis_client.xadd(
                workflow_id,
                {
                    'stage': 'initiated',
                    'job_id': str(job_id),
                    'job_title': job.title,
                    'company': job.author.company,
                    'user_id': str(user_id),
                    'user_email': user.email,
                    'user_name': f"{user.first_name} {user.last_name}",
                    'cover_letter_provided': 'yes' if cover_letter else 'no',
                    'application_score': '0',  # Initial score
                    'status': 'pending_review',
                    'next_action': 'hr_screening',
                    'estimated_completion': (timezone.now() + timezone.timedelta(days=5)).isoformat(),
                    'workflow_data': f'{{"stage_count": 0, "total_stages": 4}}'
                }
            )
            
            # Add to company-specific application queue
            redis_client.xadd(
                f'company_applications:{job.author.company.replace(" ", "_").lower()}',
                {
                    'workflow_id': workflow_id,
                    'job_title': job.title,
                    'applicant': user.email,
                    'priority': 'normal',
                    'initial_entry_id': initial_entry
                }
            )
            
            return workflow_id, initial_entry
Printout:

XREAD - Read Stream Entries

Purpose: Read entries from one or more streams, optionally blocking for new entries.
Syntax: XREAD [COUNT count] [BLOCK milliseconds] STREAMS key [key ...] ID [ID ...]
Parameters:
  • COUNT - Maximum number of entries to return
  • BLOCK - Block for specified milliseconds waiting for new entries
  • STREAMS - Stream names followed by starting IDs
  • $ - Read only new entries

Return: Array of streams with their entries
Django Example 1: Real-time Job Application Monitor 
# management/commands/monitor_applications.py
import redis
from django.core.management.base import BaseCommand
from django.core.mail import send_mail
from django.conf import settings

redis_client = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

class Command(BaseCommand):
    help = 'Monitor job applications in real-time'
    
    def handle(self, *args, **options):
        last_id = '0'  # Start from beginning, use '$' for new only
        
        self.stdout.write("🔍 Starting job application monitor...")
        
        while True:
            try:
                # Block for 30 seconds waiting for new applications
                result = redis_client.xread(
                    streams={'job_applications': last_id},
                    count=10,
                    block=30000
                )
                
                if result:
                    for stream_name, entries in result:
                        for entry_id, fields in entries:
                            self.process_application(entry_id, fields)
                            last_id = entry_id
                
            except KeyboardInterrupt:
                self.stdout.write("❌ Monitor stopped")
                break
            except Exception as e:
                self.stdout.write(f"⚠️ Error: {e}")
    
    def process_application(self, entry_id, fields):
        self.stdout.write(
            f"📨 New Application [{entry_id}]\n"
            f"   Job: {fields.get('job_title')} at {fields.get('company')}\n"
            f"   Applicant: {fields.get('applicant_email')}\n"
            f"   Location: {fields.get('location')}\n"
            f"   Salary: ${fields.get('salary')}\n"
            f"   Time: {fields.get('timestamp')}"
        )
        
        # Send notification email to HR
        if fields.get('salary') and int(fields.get('salary')) > 85000:
            send_mail(
                f"🎯 High-Value Application: {fields.get('job_title')}",
                f"New application for senior position.\nApplicant: {fields.get('applicant_email')}",
                settings.DEFAULT_FROM_EMAIL,
                ['hr@company.com']
            )

Django Example 2: Multi-Stream Performance Dashboard 
# views.py - Real-time performance dashboard
import redis
from django.http import JsonResponse
from django.views.decorators.csrf import csrf_exempt
import json

redis_client = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

@csrf_exempt
def performance_dashboard_data(request):
    try:
        # Read from multiple performance streams simultaneously
        streams = {
            'performance_metrics': '$',
            'user_activity:1': '$',
            'notifications:job_posts': '$',
            'error_logs': '$'
        }
        
        # Non-blocking read of latest entries
        result = redis_client.xread(
            streams=streams,
            count=5,
            block=0  # Non-blocking
        )
        
        dashboard_data = {
            'timestamp': timezone.now().isoformat(),
            'streams': {}
        }
        
        for stream_name, entries in result:
            dashboard_data['streams'][stream_name] = []
            
            for entry_id, fields in entries:
                dashboard_data['streams'][stream_name].append({
                    'id': entry_id,
                    'data': fields,
                    'stream': stream_name
                })
        
        # Calculate summary metrics
        dashboard_data['summary'] = {
            'total_streams': len(result),
            'total_entries': sum(len(entries) for _, entries in result),
            'active_streams': [name for name, entries in result if entries]
        }
        
        return JsonResponse(dashboard_data)
        
    except Exception as e:
        return JsonResponse({'error': str(e)}, status=500)
Printout:

      {

      }
    ],
    "user_activity:1": [
      {
        "id": "1693920007001-0", 
        "data": {"action": "POST", "path": "/jobs/apply/", "username": "john_doe"}

    ]
  },

    "total_streams": 4,
    "total_entries": 2,
    "active_streams": ["performance_metrics", "user_activity:1"]
  }
}
Django Example 3: Event-Driven Notification System 
# services/real_time_notifications.py
import redis
import asyncio
import json
from channels.generic.websocket import AsyncWebsocketConsumer
from asgiref.sync import sync_to_async

redis_client = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

class NotificationConsumer(AsyncWebsocketConsumer):
    async def connect(self):
        self.user_id = self.scope['user'].id
        self.user_skills = await self.get_user_skills()
        
        await self.accept()
        
        # Start background task to monitor streams
        asyncio.create_task(self.monitor_notifications())
    
    async def monitor_notifications(self):
        # Monitor multiple notification streams for this user
        streams = {
            'notifications:job_posts': '$',  # Global job notifications
        }
        
        # Add skill-specific streams
        for skill in self.user_skills:
            streams[f'notifications:skill:{skill.lower()}'] = '$'
        
        last_ids = {stream: '$' for stream in streams}
        
        while True:
            try:
                # Block for 10 seconds waiting for new notifications
                result = await sync_to_async(redis_client.xread)(
                    streams=last_ids,
                    count=3,
                    block=10000
                )
                
                for stream_name, entries in result:
                    for entry_id, fields in entries:
                        await self.send_notification(stream_name, entry_id, fields)
                        last_ids[stream_name] = entry_id
                        
            except Exception as e:
                print(f"Notification monitor error: {e}")
                await asyncio.sleep(5)
    
    async def send_notification(self, stream_name, entry_id, fields):
        notification = {
            'type': 'job_notification',
            'stream': stream_name,
            'id': entry_id,
            'data': {
                'title': fields.get('title', 'New Opportunity'),
                'company': fields.get('company'),
                'location': fields.get('location'),
                'salary': fields.get('salary'),
                'skills': fields.get('skills_required', '').split(','),
                'url': fields.get('url'),
                'timestamp': fields.get('created_at')
            }
        }
        
        await self.send(text_data=json.dumps(notification))
    
    @sync_to_async
    def get_user_skills(self):
        # Get user's skills for targeted notifications
        return ['python', 'django', 'javascript']  # Simplified
Printout:

XGROUP CREATE - Create Consumer Group

Purpose: Creates a new consumer group for a stream, enabling multiple consumers to process entries.
Syntax: XGROUP CREATE key group ID|$ [MKSTREAM]
Parameters:
  • key - Stream name
  • group - Consumer group name
  • ID|$ - Starting position ('$' for new entries only)
  • MKSTREAM - Create the stream if it doesn't exist

Return: OK if successful
Django Example 1: Job Processing Pipeline Setup 
# management/commands/setup_job_pipeline.py
import redis
from django.core.management.base import BaseCommand

redis_client = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, decode_responses=True)

class Command(BaseCommand):
    help = 'Setup job processing consumer groups'
    
    def handle(self, *args, **options):
        try:
            # Create consumer groups for different processing stages
            
            # 1. Application processing group
            redis_client.xgroup_create(
                'job_applications',
                'application_processors', 
                '$',  # Process only new applications
                mkstream=True
            )
            self.stdout.write("✅ Created application_processors group")
            
            # 2. Email notification group  
            redis_client.xgroup_create(
                'job_applications',
                'email_notifications',
                '0',  # Process from beginning for email backlog
                mkstream=True
            )
            self.stdout.write("✅ Created email_notifications group")
            
            # 3. Analytics group
            redis_client.xgroup_create(
                'job_applications', 
                'analytics_team',
                '0',  # Process all historical data
                mkstream=True
            )
            self.stdout.write("✅ Created analytics_team group")
            
            # 4. Performance monitoring group
            redis_client.xgroup_create(
                'performance_metrics',
                'monitoring_dashboard',
                '$',  # Only new metrics
                mkstream=True  
            )
            self.stdout.write("✅ Created monitoring_dashboard group")
            
            # 5. Priority application group (high salary jobs)
            redis_client.xgroup_create(
                'job_applications',
                'priority_processing',
                '$',
                mkstream=True
            )
            self.stdout.write("✅ Created priority_processing group")
            
            self.stdout.write(
                self.style.SUCCESS(
                    "🎉 All consumer groups created successfully!\n"
                    "Available groups:\n"
                    "- application_processors (main processing)\n" 
                    "- email_notifications (automated emails)\n"
                    "- analytics_team (data analysis)\n"
                    "- monitoring_dashboard (real-time metrics)\n"
                    "- priority_processing (high-value applications)"
                )
            )
            
        except Exception as e:
            if "BUSYGROUP" in str(e):
                self.stdout.write("⚠️ Some groups already exist - skipping")
            else:
                self.stdout.write(f"❌ Error: {e}")
